A.H.A.
The Irish
Quite the most readable book on Ireland
is My Irish Year, by Padraic Colum (Mills
and Boon, per Sydney Book Club). The
Ireland this Irishman writes of is merely
Catholic and Peasant 'lreland, and in this
localised strip of country the author views
the life chiefly in its agrarian aspect. But
he claims that the Catholic peasantry are
not merely the bulk of the Irish population,
but, roughly speaking, the historic Irish
nation. The life described is utterly strange
to Australian readers. He shows how the
marriage customs arc largely responsible for
emigration. For instance, Bridget, the
second daughter of an Irish farmer, has to
emigrate to America to earn her dowry;
without it she could not marry in her own
class. She could stay and marry a laborer
without a dowry, but not a farmer’s son.
So off she goes to become a servant in
America, to come back some day and marry
a farmer in Ireland. That is, if one will
have her, for a dowry is not earned easily
or quickly. Half of the Bridgets are never
heard of again. Out of 40 young men and
women who leave Ireland, most of them
intending to return, 36 don’t.
The sort of speech with which the Irish
dramatists have familiarised us occurs on
nearly every page.’ Colum states that this
picturesque imagery, infused with a sense of
rhythm, is the common speech of the
peasants. Incidentally, he prints “The
Tinker’s Curse” :
“The Tinker never goes under a roof.
Here’s what the tinker says when he passes
your house and mine:—
You build houses! Ay, like the crows, you put stick
and stick together.
May I see a scatter of sticks and the kites a-chase
through the wood!
You live man and wife, you say, like the goats, two
and two a-tether.
For fear ye would reach to the hedge-tops, and
the wild taste- get in your blood!
But there are more blistering curses re-
corded —one bard curses his three enemies
in every one of 26 Verses, mentioning them
by name, with a variety of invective super-
bly Irish. Here is a nice little “rann” :
May a messenger come from the high place of God,
To bear up your soul to a throne— V
But a robber be robbing him on the way back,
And your fall be as dead as a stone. *
May your tables be laden with gold and with jewels,
And your hands be upon them for proof,
When the Devil whips in by your' beggarly door,
And tears your red soul through the roof.
The author has made a delightful collec-
;ion of peasant love-songs. Here is the man
to the woman: —
O Una, O maiden, 0 friend, and 0 golden tooth!
O little mouth that never uttered an injustice,
I had rather be beside her on a couch, ever kissing
her,
Than to be sitting in Heaven on the chair of the
Trinity.... -
O fair Una, it is you that set astray my senses;
O Una, it was you who went close in between me
and God;
O Una, fragrant branch, twisted little curl of the
ringlets,
Was it not better for me to be without eyes than
ever to have seen you?
And here the woman to the man: —
And farewell henceforth to yon town westward
among the trees; it is there I am drawn early and
late. Many is the wet, dirty morass and crooked
road going between me and the town where my treas-
ure i 5.... 0 Paddy; are you sorry that I am ill, and
do you think bad of me that I am going to the
I churchyard ? O Paddy, of the bound black hair, it
is your mouth that is sweet; and until I go under
the ground, my affection will be on you for your con-
verse with me..,.And Oh, dear Virgin, what shall
I do if you go from me? I have no knowledge of
your house, your haggard, or your stacks. Ah, faith-
ful was the counsel that my people gave me not to
elope with you, for you have the hundred twists in
your heart and the thousand tricks.
Or Sappho in Ireland: —
I am sitting tip
Since the moon rose last night,
And putting down a fire
And ever kindling it diligently ;
The people of the house are lying down,
And I am by myself;
The cocks are crowing,
And the land is asleep but me.
That I may never leave the world
Till I loosed from me the ill-luck,
Till I have cows and sheep,
And my one desire of a boy.
I would not think the night long,
That I would be stretched- by his smooth white
breast,
And sure I would-allow the raoe of Eve after that
To say their choice thing of me.... The curse of the
Son of God
Upon the one who took from me my love,
And left me by myself
Every single long night in misery.
And, O young boy,
I am no material for mockery for you ;
You have nothing to say
Except that I am without dowry. You are not my
love,
And my destruction, if I am sorry for it ;
And if I am without cattle,, I am able to lie alone.

Eardley Turner objects to a “Red Page”
statement that Zangwill is “the latest liter-
ary recruit to the drama.” He states that
Z. had a one-act piece produced in London
25 years ago, and has been busy since, find-
ing a ready market in the U.S., and during
the last five years has had a play running
every season in America. But the conten-
tion was that his recent plays, The War
God and The Next Religion were his first
contribution to “the modern drama of ideas.”
That contention stands.
Fiction
The Red Page
The main objection to fiction is that so
often it is fiction. The novel presents
characters purporting to be real; for a few
chapters you accept the author’s premises
and takes his carefully constructed
puppets on trust. The hero is at
first a decent enough chap; but the
crisis comes, and' he behaves with a
heroism that instinctively you know
could not exist in real life, while the heroine,
beginning as a mere pretty woman, develops
into a subtle, complicated piece of psychol-
ogy that certainly does not exist in
your suburb. Of course, if you want fic-
tion, you can always depend on getting it;
but if you desire a basis of truth, the solid
earth _ beneath you, few fiction waiters will
give it to you. No matter how realistic
they are, their heroes and heroines are a few
inches taller, by a few subtleties finer, than
even the most heroic individual you happen
to know. They are even more heroic than
yourself. The fictionists will tell you that
this is their trade, to depict imaginary
characters; but no mature reader can go on
being interested in beings whom he knows
■'do not, and cannot, exist except in the illus-
trations of the average novel.
Take a novel like The Green Vase , by
William R. Castle, Junr. (Dodd, Mead, per
Dymock’s Library). This is a book with an
original plot, admirably written. There is
the crude, ambitious, good-fellow husband
and the cultured bride, taken to live in South
Boston, in a house with lace curtains and a
hideous green vase, the gift of a Philistine
Uncle John. There is the cultured 'aristo-
crat who loves her. An accident happens;
the wife is supposed to have been killed, but
is rescued in a senseless condition by the
other man. When she recovers conscious-
ness months afterwards, she finds herself
apparently the wife of the other man. She
has lost all memory of her past. Her child
—her husband’s child—is born, and the
alleged husband takes her away out west.
The woman does not love the man; all her
affection is for her child. The man conveni-
ently dies after six years ; and the real hus-
band conveniently arrives, woos his unsus-
pecting wife again, re-marries her, and
takes her back to the house of the green
vase. The sight of that atrocity restores
her memory. And the crudeness of her hus-
band has disappeared. After her supposed
death he had recognised his lack of culture,
and had succeeded in making himself the
man she had wished him to be. He remains
the one real person in the book. The wife,
a mere snobbish, foolish, cowardly girl, is
presented to us by the author as a divinity
of complex mentality—the rather nauseat-
ing American worship of the woman in fic-
tion. Both she and her lover have been
etherealized and sublimated out of recog-
nition as part of our common humanity.
#
Nineteen years ago, on the appearance of
her brilliant Ships that Pass in the Night,
Beatrice Harradan’s name suddenly fore-
shadowed great literary possibilities. But
none of her succeeding novels, though show-
ing honest workmanship, sustained the hope
of her first book. Now in Out of the Wreck
I Rise (Nelson, per Dymock’s Library), she
shows the same qualities that gave her
Ships such popularity. The novel starts
brilliantly, with the threatened exposure of
a celebrated dramatic agent, whom his
clients find to have been regularly appropriat-
ing huge amounts of their royalties. The
dramatic agent has not fallen through a
temporary weakness: he is temperamentally
a clever thief; and no attempt is made by
the author to palliate or excuse his crime.
Nor do the two women whom in the past he
had subjugated by his dominant personality,
and thrown aside, make any excuses for
him. In a revival of their old jealous
rivalry they set to work to save him—
though both know that the sacrifices they
make are doomed to futility. One of the
women, a Jewess, who deals in antiquities
and jewels, is herself a professional cheat.
Both women are drawn with a masterly
hand, and the complex character of the
dramatic agent is admirably and convinc-
ingly presented. There is also a strange
clergyman, who seems merely a woman’s
abstraction —purely fictional. The book
does not end : the suicide of the dramatic
agent is an admitted failure of the author’s
craftsmanship. - The style is delightful. The
effort of the author seems to be to prove
that the failure of the man’s life is necessary
to develop and enrich the lifes of all that
he has come into contact with. The very
wounds that he has inflicted are shown to
be the beginning of the healing of their
souls. Out of his wreck they rise.
Algernon Blackwood continues on his
chosen path. His latest volume, Pan's
Garden (Macmillan), is a collection of
nature stories ; but his Nature is consciously
alive, just as it took visible form in his
preceding book, The Centaur. ' This time
we have stories dealing with the “group-
soul” of such things as a forest, the sea,
fire, snow, sand, a township and clay.
“The Man whom the Trees Loved,” for in-
stance, begins on the note: “I wonder
whether a tree in any lawful meaning of the
term can be —alive. I remember some writ-
ing fellow telling me long ago that trees
had once been moving things, animal organ-
isms of some sort, that had stood so long
feeding, sleeping, dreaming, or something, in
the same place, that they had lost the power
to get away.” Then comes a statement by
Prof. Darwin, president of the Roval Soci-
ety, suggesting that “we must believe that
in plants there exists a faint copy of what
we know as consciousness in ourselves.”
Prom this historical premise it is but a step
to the suggestion of a group-consciousness
in a forest.
“Behind a great forest may stand a
rather splendid Entity that manifests,
through all the thousand individual trees,
some huge collective life, quite as minutely
and delicately organised as our own. It
might merge and blend with ours under cer-
tain conditions, so that we could understand
it by being it, for a time at least. It might
even engulf human vitality into the immense
whirlpool of its own vast dreaming life. The
pull of a big forest on a man can be tre-
mendously and utterly overwhelming.”
So. set the scene in a lonely cottage sur-
rounded by a forest, with the great herded,
mass of the trees wanting to “get” the man
they loved, protected only by a cedar
tree, and you have, all the eerie properties
with which Algernon Blackwood can so
shudderingly play.
And if trees have a group-soul, why not
sand? Suppose there were big souls, so
huge and complex that they could not ex-
press themselves in a single individual, but
needed an entire group for manifestation?
“In the lower ranges of humanity—cer-
tainly in animal and insect life—one soul
is shared by many. Behind a tribe of
savages stands one Savage. A flock of
birds is a single Bird, scattered through the
consciousness of all. They wheel in mid-
air, they migrate, they obey the deep intelli-
gence called instinct —all as one. An ant-
heap is a single Ant; through the bees
spreads the consciousness of a single Bee.”
Why not, then, the group-soul of a large
flat thing like the desert? Away to Egypt
and evoke the ancient intelligences of lost
Atlantis, and by the right incantations make
the Desert stand on end! How an artist is
caught in this stupendous experiment is
told by Blackwood in Sand, with a magic
not second to that of The Garden of Allah.
*
Again, if a human soul, at the moment
of death, can return to that which gave it
life, that which it loves best, why not the
apparition of a group-soul? In “The De-
struction of Smith,” the authbr comes peril-
ously near farce in his extreme application
of this idea. Ilis apparition, his ghost, is
no_ single soul, but the soul of a whole town-
ship. A man named Smith founded a min-
ing township in. America. While he is away
on a hunting trip he becomes conscious that
something has happened to his beloved
ship. And in that lonely camp his companions
see. the tremendous ghost of Smithville,
which had been suddenly wiped out by fire,
roaring across the sky, returning to the soul
of the one man who had given it being.
“The personality of the town, set free and
loosened in the moment of death, had re-
turned to him who gave it birth, who loved
it, and of whose life it was actually an ex-
pression. The Being of Smithville was lit-
erally a projection, an 'emanation of the
dynamic, vital personality of its puissant
creator. And, in death, it had returned on
him with the shock of an accumulated power
impossible for a human being to resist. For
years he had provided it with life—but
gradually. It now rushed back to its source,
thus concentrated, in a single terrific mo-
ment.”
Of course, the soul of burnt-up Smithville-
(loss of life, 3000) exploded Smith. The
moral seems to be not to found townships.
But this is the only story in this weird eal-
lection that “lets dotvn” the reader. The
others, granting the author’s premises of
group-entities—of which there is some
scientific probability—carry sufficient surface
conviction to allay the reader’s suspicions,
and make them admirable examples of a new
form of ghost story. A most readable and
interesting book.
**
Sequels are always a mistake. Elsie
Lindt tier (Lane), the sequel to Karin
Michaelis’s striking book, The Dangerous
Age, is a catastrophe. There was a hitherto
untouched theme in that noteworthy book;
but the heroine grows wearisome, and'a trifle
ridiculous, in her continuous harping on that
theme. Her later life is scarcely interest-
ing. She travels, adopts a child from the
gutter, tames him and sees him started on
the road to success on a farm, and feels for
him the mother-love that all her life she had
missed. But, as in the previous book, she
has too much time to think ; only in her care
for her adopted child and her new interest
in the lives of her friends does she find relief
from her sterile selfishness. But the book
is carelessly written, and her observations
have none of the originality that made her
first book so interesting. Exit Elsie Lindtner
and her obsession, with relief.
The Matador of the Fire Towns
(Methuen) shows Arnold Bennett’s mastery
of the short story. The title story has
about it the atmosnhere of great art: this
story of sordid happenings to commonplace
persons—“mean, harsh, ugly, squalid, crude,
barbaric”—has in it “the true, fine, romantic
savor of life.” Bennett has the sense of
true romance, .as. Conrad has defined
Romance. But it is chiefly in the tragic
stories, and not in the “frolic” stories, that
Bennett has the sure touch of the mature
artist. His knowledge of life, his shrewd-
ness, his genial humor, are to be found in
these light-hearted frolics, such as “Catching
the Train” and “The Long-lost Uncle,” but
the slightness of the plots too often detracts
from this amiable fooling.
J*
The novel which is rightly said to mark
the flood-tide of the genius of Anatole
France, La Rotisserie de la Heine Pedauque
appears in translation in Lane’s sumptuous
edition of this author’s works as At the Siqn
of the Reine P6dauque (per Lockley Li-
brary). This work—not meant, of course,
for babes or flappers-Mntroeluces us to the
Rabelaisian Abbe, Maitre Jerome Coignard,
the pax-ent, in his turn, of such a hero as
William J. Locke’s Beloved Vagabond. And,
as Locke, in an excellent foreword, says, of
all. France’s heroes we love most this scape-
grace Abbe.
“Even were he a layman we should call him
a pretty scoundrel; but, priest that he is, we
have no words wherein to summarise the
measure of his fall from grace. He drinks,
he brawls, he cheats at cai-ds ; he cannot pass
a pretty girl on the stairs but his arm slips
round her waist; to follow in Pandarus’s
footsteps causes him no compunction; he
“borrows” half a dozen bottles of wine from
an inn, and runs away with his employer’s
diamonds. At first sight he appears to be
an unconscionable villain. But endow him
with the inexhaustible learning, the philoso-
phy. the mansuetude, the wit of Monsieur
Bergeret, imagine him a Sylvesti'e Bonnard
qualified for the personal entourage of Pan-
tagruel, and you have a totally different con-
ception of his character.”
The story of the adventures of the narra-
tor and this great-hearted scoundrel affords
the author full scope for his learning, his
naivety, his quiet irony, his broad and genial
philosophy. The Abbe and his pupil are
taken into the service of an alchemist, where
the youth finds adventures and the AbbC
good philosophy and good liquor. One esca-
pade is gloriously and unctuously told—the
strange drunken supper of four ill-assorted
individuals. Swift follows an elopement,
pursuit and the death of the Abbd, who makes
an end as edifying as that of ITalstaff—from
whose recoi-ded death the author is not
above borrowing well-known details —and
the youth, bereft of his teacher, finds in a
book-shop the quiet so. dear to a scholar’s
soul. The book is a virile, sanguine tour de
force. Humor broad and ironic, philosophy
pagan and devout, and a sense of life har-
monising the whole outlook, create for us a
chai’acter unforgettable.
The only golf-expert on the staff, the Reli-
gious Editor, now being on his annual six
months’ vacation in Paris, this office is un-
able critically to review The A.rt of Golf, by
Joshua Y. Taylor, with a chapter on the
evolution of the Bunker, by J. H. Taylor,
ex-champion (Laurie) ; but the book seems
to have the usual allowance of photogi’aphs
of hard-faced men swinging sticks, and, con-
sidering the poorness of golf-links soil, the
crop of bunkers seems remarkably encourag-
ing.
Mary Gaunt, whose book, ALlone in West
Africa, recently reviewed here, is having a
big sale, hopes to do for the Northern Terri-
tory what she did for the black continent—
prove that the white wotnan can adapt her-
self to life in the tropics without discbm-
fort or ill effects. She intends to ride
through the whole of the Territory, and
from her experiences produce a book which
should be as informative and as interesting
as 'her reeent work.
Sumner Locke, the author of Mum Daw-
son, Boss —that bright piece of Australian
humor —On-our-Selection brand, but up
to sample—follows with further cheerful
chronicles of her back-blocks family. This
time it is The Dawsons’ Uncle' George
(N.S.W. Bookstall Co.). The worst feature
of this school of humor, of which this is an
excellent specimen, is that it paints the back-
blocks as the scene of such continuous orgies
of hilarious happenings that there is a dan-
ger that the cities will be depopulated in the
rush for the bush.
A valuable statistical volume is the re-
written new edition of Porter’s Progress of
the Nation (Methuen), brought up to date
by F. W. Hirst, editor of The Economist.
The Nation is Great Britain, an admir-
able survey of all that nation’s leading
trades, accompanied by official statistics,
chapters on foreign commerce, on coinage,,
banking, insurance, postal developments, in-
ternal transit, the growth of national wealth
and capital, etc., is of immediate value to
the world.
The sevenpenny novels (9d. here) of
Heinemann ( per George Robertson, Syd-
ney) are well worth purchase. A list of
five samples, excellently produced in cloth
binding: The Ebb-Tide (R. L. Stevenson),
Soldiers of Fortune (R. H. Davis), The
War of the Worlds (H. G. Wells), The Gall
of the Wild (Jack London), The Bondman
(Hall Caine).
The late Andrew Lang, translator, scien-
tific smatterer, psychical researcher, Scots-
man, literary Sherlock Holmes, historian,
biographer, causerie writer, minor poet, edi-
tor, collector of fairy lore, journalist, re-
viewer, author of published works that
it takes the British Museum Catalogue 16
pages to enumerate, will not be long remem-
bered. He did so many things so indiffer-
ently well.

When the delicate mechanism of your
brain is disorganised by worry and over-

speedily revitalise your poor, over-strained

act promptly. No man can think
with a worn-out brain. But here is

to stimulate your brain, nourish your
starved nerves, and give you back your
old-time mental activity.
Anaemia.
The pallid complexion—the transpar-
ent skin—the lustreless eyes—tell, only
too plainly, the story of insufficient blood.
But “Wincarnis” will soon give a bright-

to the cheeks. And as you take your

by day, getting stronger—brighter—
healthier —as the grandly nourishing
qualities of “Wincarnis” create a fund of

through your veins, to carry health,
strength and nourishment to every part
of the body.
Sleeplessness
Only those who suffer from sleepless-
ness realise the intense misery of the in-
cessant tossing to and fro —the agonising
wait for daybreak—and the feeling of
utter exhaustion in the morning. Sleep-
lesness is due to a disordered state of the
brain cells, therefore, although the body
may be thoroughly worn out, the brain
is in such a highly-strung condition that
sleep is impossible. A wineglassful of
“Wincarnis” taken just before retiring
will soothe that highly-strung brain, and
ensure you an uninterrupted and sweetly
refreshing night’s rest.
Convalescence
In prolonged convalescence after a
severe illness, the effect of “Wincarnis”
is almost magical. From the first, and
after each succeeding wineglassful, “Win-
carnis” imparts a delicious feeling of
renewed vitality. And day by day the
patient gets stronger and brighter, the
appetite improves, and the blush of health
returns to the cheeks. A wealth of rich, red
blood is created which circulates through-
out the body, and rebuilds the worn-out
tissues, revitalises the weakened nerves
and braces up the system so that the
whole body pulsates with renewed life.

A Melbourne citizen was lately farewelled
with a gold watch presentation on the eve
of his departure on a trip abrawd. In his
long, unnecessary speech he covered all the
ground from Dan to Beersheba, dragging
in references to his native land, his fore-
fathers, school days, college, travels, aristo-
cratic friends and acquaintances, employers,
fellow motorists, intentions, probable move-
ments, and other more or less relevant
matters. Then he sat down. The only
thing he forgot was to thank the audience
for the watch.
AMENDED.
[For, The Bulletin.]
“Peruvian Rubber Atrocities —Horrible Revela-
tions.”—Scare cable headings, scanned hastily in the
tram.
Of rubber horrors in Peru I read without a tear;
I am not grieved exceedingly—l’m hardly pained, I
fear.
In vain with far atrocities they ply us in the dawn ;
Man’s inhumanity to man makes countless thousands
yawn.
N.s.w. CONSTANT READER.
July Pearson’s has some ecstatic puff-
paragraphs about its forthcoming serial,
“The Recipe for Rubber,” by Ralph Stock,
with strenuous pictures (samples given) by
Norman Lindsay. The tale has run its
course in The Lone Hand, and has for some
time been out in book form! Australian
stuff must be popular in England if a pro-
minent magazine like Pearson’s is willing
to use it at second-hand in this way.
The Fisher Government has encouraged
the importation of pretty well everything
but widows; and now Bramwell Booth, boss
of the Salvation Army, proposes to complete
the list by shipping out loads of sighing
ladies in sad, black garments with black-
framed portraits of the deceased in their
trunks. Fisher’s non-committal answer,
when asked if he intended to adopt the new
Booth’s proposal, leaves the question
shrouded, so to speak, in croom (which is
part crape and part gloom). Widows vary
considerably as regards disposition, looks
and wage-earning capacity; but in the aver-
age they are saddened, hard-up ladies of
limited business experience, who have lost
the first blush of youth. The field of useful-
ness open to the somewhat-faded widow is a
small one. She is unable to swindle on the
Stock Exchange, or go mining; she can’t be
a professional footballer, or fight under
Mclntosh management, or be the flying
angel attached to a steam crane. These and
many other less picturesque jobs are closed
to her on account of her sex, and age, and
faded condition. The kitchen is her most
promising sphere. If she is prepared to fill
the apron of departed Mary Ann. and man
the deserted washing-up tin and dustpan,
this country will gladly give her a nail to
hang the departed’s picture on, and listen
sympathetically to her reminiscences. It is
healthier to have the housework done in a
spirit of youthful gaiety ; but if there is no
gaiety or youth available, the imported lady
of grief is welcome to groan over the gas-
stove and go sighing after the dust like a
lonely midnight wind. But the Australian
sunlight, that champion dissipator of sorrow,
may revive her so much that she will refuse
to consider anything but an engagement, in
the chorus. Andrew Fisher, should think
deeply before he orders any foreign widows.
“Chudah” writes from Bethlehem: —
The Chief Rabbi-ship of England, in succession to
Adler, is not filled, although a year has elapsed. A
Hebraic writer in the last Contemporary Review
tips five men, and the first one is Dr. Joseph Abra-
hams, of the West Melbourne Synagogue. There is
no Chief Rabbi in Germany, France, Austria or the
United States.' Consequently .it is. argued that none
is required in England. Adler was intensely conser-
vative. He was supposed to control Australia, and
threatened to cut both Sydney and Melbourne off in
consequence of independent Synagogic practices, such
as ladies singing in choirs, sexes sitting together, and
the introduction of the English language into the
service. Montefiore leads the Reformed English
Jews, with synagogues in London, Manchester and
Bradford. Lord Rothschild heads the Conservatives,
with a body called the Great Synagogue. This de-
mands a Chief Rabbi, and he must be English. What-
ever the danger may be, there is more hazard now
than at any time of Jewry falling to pieces. Maybe
only Russian persecution holds it together. Liberal
Synagogues are just becoming Christian churches of
the free and easy type to which many churches are
reduced. Higher Criticism of the Bible makes in-
roads corresponding to those in Churchianit.y.
“San Down” speaks a piece, fragment or
scrap:—
It is quite likely that, when Wootton, senior, made
his assertion (cabled 29/8/’l2) that the English
Jockey Club stewards are “hounding” his son Stanley
the jockey, out of the racing game, he was entirely
correct. The stewards invariably perform this act
in the case of any lad who gets a bit above himself —
which accounts for the circumstance that the British
jock, however successful and affluent, is about the
most humble, self-abasing, forelbck-dragger on God’s
green earth. The classic example of the exercise of
the wiping-out prerogative was the case of Tod Sloan.
Sloan’s extraordinary success in the saddle in the
late ’9o’s, revolutionised the flat race riding game in
England, and earned him a fortune. It also gave him
swelled head. He dined ostentatiously at restaurants
like the Savoy (sacred to the upper classes and the
corps de ballet ), gave glittering and highly-alcoholic
parties at his hotel in London, and, worst offence of
all, he gambled freely and publicly. I have often
seen Tod arriving, in the large barouche he used to
affect, with three gaming pals, at Lingfield, or one of
the other courses within driving distance of London,
and only putting aside the cards as his conveyance
stopped at the gates. The word went round that he
was backing his own mounts. Of course he backed
his own mounts, as do 90 per cent, of the crack
riders of the present time. What was never charged
against him was the far more serious offence of
laying against the horses he steered —a common habit
in the days of the “jockeys’ ring,” which burst
shortly before Sloan went to England. Anyhow, it
was decided that he should go. His gaming proclivi-
ties were rhentioned casually as the reason. The
real explanation was, probably, his rash act in bid-
ding at Newmarket for a yearling when a representa-
tive of King Edward was in the ring, and after the
animal. Also, there was the waiter-felling episode at
Ascot. However, no reasons were ever officially
given for his extirpation. That is the way with
turf-rulings in England, for the most part. The
Jockey Club decides that such and such an action
is “for the good of racing,” and the thing goes.
Tod was noiselessly wiped out. To be . wiped out in
England is tantamount to being wiped out everywhere
that big racing flourishes, and Tod has not figured
aboard a racer at any important meeting since. If
the Jockey Club stewards have a down on young
Wootton he is doomed in the capacity of race-rider.
In any case, his narent will but hasten the downfall
by making a public outcry about the lad’s troubles.
The Jockey Club has shown repeatedly that it cares
about as much for the enraged clamor of the popu-
lace as the Spanish Inquisition did for the chidings
of foreign heretics. The Wootton family’s best hope
of evading the trouble that apparently threatens is
for each and every member thereof to remove his or
her hat, and kneel reverentially before addressing a
person of title; for the whole crowd to join a
Christian Temperance and Anti-Gaming League;
and for them all to be very, very ’umble as regards
clothes, deportment and social habits. Incidentally,
it would be a subtly astute move to gag Father
Wootton when in the presence of newspaper reporters.
In fact, this would probably be the most effective
manoeuvre of all.

“ It is impossible to over-estimate the import-
ance of the teeth and mouth both as factors
of health and as sources of disease. Every
neglected decaying tooth in the mouth is
a well-spring of poison in the body.”
Odol does more than cleanse and
beautify the teeth; it preserves them from decay;
it refreshes the whole mouth to the point of exhilaration,
keeps the gums healthy and free from tenderness, and
delightfully perfumes the breath.
And by a remarkable property, peculiar to Odol alone,
it permeates the mucous membrane of the entire oral cavity
j
with its antiseptic elements, leaving the mouth proof
for hours afterwards against the attacks of injurious bacteria,
which, if not guarded against, inevitably destroy the teeth.
Until science produced Odol the world was without a
dentifrice that was capable of completely preventing or
arresting dental decay, but when it was found that Odol really
did this, its success became a matter of course, and quickly
spread over every civilised region. Millions of bottles are
now sold annually, and every month the sale largely increases.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Whiskers of George.
The wide world wears a shaven chin,
And bare and cold appear to me
The rigid lip and visage thin
Forlorn of manly mystery;
I dream of whiskers in the sun,
Wild whiskers flaming down the world,
And manly jaws that would not shun
The hair that nature round them curled.
Ah, beard to beard at windy Troy,
The foemen shook the shining spear;
What razored fool or hairless boy
In such a battle might appear?
Their armor clanged, their voices threw
Defiance in tremendous words,
The while like fencing wire outflew
Jaw-growths entangled in their swords.
Goat-tufted merchants of the prime
From Tvre and Carthage pushed their
ships,
And Israel’s prophets wove sublime
The whiskers of Apocalypse ;
And up and down the living world
Were splendid jaws that would not shun
To wear their hirsute flag unfurled
Flaunting defiance at the sun.
Thor beat his hammer on the cloud.
And flashing in the tonmost height,
While thunder bellowed long and loud,
His face adornments split the night;
The Goth and Vandal southward sped
Their conquering arms by land and sea,
Vaunting o’er kings and nations dead
Their chin-mats red with destiny.
Then the glad maid in street and hall
Walked on bright feet that only feared
The rapture of a sudden fall
Through tripping in her lover’s beard ;
The times seemed vbry bright and fair
From Babylon to Astrakhan
When cataracts of rolling hair
Fell earthward from the face of man.
Ah. bare and cold appear to me
The rigid lip and visage thin
That, lorn of manly mystery,
Surmount the harsh and shaven chin ;
But some kind fate that guards my
Has sent George from his Queensland
home
All bearded like the morn of Greece.
And whiskered as was purple Rome !
w.s.w.
MARY McCOMMONWEALTH.
The Price of Peace.
After the battle, through the night,
We rode along the line of fight.
And one there was whom men had caught
To kill him for the lies he taught;
The lies that nursed in sluggard ease
Our fenceless land, our naked seas.
“He was the first to fly,” they said,
“And left his hearthstone to its dead.”
But ere they tore him limb from limb,
We horsemen laid our hands on him;
And all along the tortured track,
Careless of death, we led him back.
We showed him where his rafters stood,
Tops black with flame, roots red with blood.
With butchered babe and shame-slain wife
We stabbed his eyes as with a knife.
And in that place of dreadful shade,
We left him to the Peace he made.
QUEENSLAND.
L.F.
The Visionary.
I set my heart on a single end,
I pledged my life to a certain cause;
The world against me —I did not bend;
High mountains fronting—l would not
pause.
I gave the best that my soul could give—
The morning hope and the nightly dream ;
“My star.” exulting. I cried, “shall live.
Shall brighten man with its lustrous
beam !”
Old Time limped by on his worm-bored staff.
And, sidewise eyeing me, shook his head ;
The world passed on with a mocking laugh :
“What fool yells yonder?” the wise men
said.
T borrowed the painter’s magic brush,
The zealot’s, fire and the poet’s thought;
Like some high peak in the dawn ablush,
Alone in a roseate glow I wrought.
How fared I? 111, in truth—so ill
That I, myself, am moved to iest- —
To think that one so weak of will
Should tread the stars in glorious quest.
I failed, and all my splendid youth
Is spent with all its great desire;
For me remains this homely truth —
One log can never make a fire.
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.
Premiums.
The Ballarat Council of Churches passed a resolu-
tion to-day condemning the granting of the maternity
bonus in the case of illegitimate children, on the
ground that it was really offering a premium to vice.
—The Age.
I love these worthy foes of Sin,
And bend on them my curious gaze
To see their brains revolve and spin,
And mark with what resounding phrase
They beat the Devil andTiis ways.
What maids are these with notions queer.
Whom want alone keeps from the
slough—
Who’ll cry with rapture: “Lo, ’tis here,
The babv bonus,” and avow,
“So we can all be wicked now”?
Consider, holy men, the creed
Based on a Saviour’s sacrifice,
And tell us, is this splendid meed
To sinners all, your Paradise,
Also, a premium to vice?
VICTORIA.
EDDYSON.

How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and, to an extent, themselves) hy taking
some care as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case he addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin,” is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.

fused.

part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-

SYDNEY, SEPTEMBER 12, 1912.
THE INDICTMENT OF ANDREW
FISHER, FISCAL SINKER.
More than once, of late, The Bulletin has
felt called upon to remark that capital is
fleeing out of Australia, and it now notes
that the process is going on with increased*
velocity, if such a thing be., possible. It is
not a case of some disgusted or anguished in-
dividual selling a few gas shares, and going
away with the proceeds in his carpet bag;
or realising on some scrappy suburban mort-
gages, and sending the money to be invested
in real estate at Slocum-on-the-Wollop, in
the county of Slummockshire, 'England.
Things of that sort are liable to happen any
day, and they don’t create a rinnle that is
wortk 9d. on the surface of events. It isn’t
even a matter of some English bank, or in-
surance office, or land company, or bull-and-
cow-and-grass corporation, putting its capi-
tal into a liemid form, and sending it for in-
vestment to Paraguay or Tripoli or the Val-
ley of Jehoshaphat, where democratic legisla-
tion ceases from troubling, and the minimum
wage is at rest, and the only troubles are
occasional revolutions, and massacres, and a
tendency on the part of brigands to put the
Let of capitalists in the fire in order to make
them say where their money is hidden. The
country has been hearing of that sort of de-
parture for years past, but the event has
never happened in any large sense, though
the Tory press wept bitterly over it, and
has urged Australia to reconsider its ways
in time, and to treat the absentee with
respect and even kindness. The present
flight of capital is a real and obvious affair.
It is serious. Even if every English bank
and finance co. and pawnbroker} - that is
doing; business in these parts gathered up
its assets and fled —a thing which is often
foreshadowed but which never comes to
pass—it' would be of less importance than
the matter now, under consideration. The
amount involved would be smaller, and at the
worst Australia would be getting rid of bor-
rowed capital, which costs a fearful amount
per annum in interest and dividends. In
other words, this country would be paying
off old debts. The calamity of the hour is
that Australia is getting rid of its own capi-
tal. And, by doing so, it is discouraging
well-paid local industry; discouraging local
employment and settlement and population ;
offering a helping hand and a leg-up to the
foreign sweater and cheap laborer, and non-
unionist, and nigger; doing a grievous wrong
to White Australia; and making the country
more and more dependent on the extraneous
Cohen.
* * * *
. During the last few years the constant up-
ward movement of wages has made the tariff
more and more inefficient. It never was
very efficient, but when it was created it
made some pretence of covering the differ-
ence between Australian high wages, short
hours and comparative newness in the manu-
facturing business, and the lower wages,
longer hours and greater experience of cer-
tain foreign competitors. But when Aus-
tralian wages were persistently raised, and
hours were, in many cases, shortened, while
customs duties continued the same, and the
cheapest variety of foreign competitor re-
mained almost as cheap as of yore, the tariff
became a glaring failure. Within the last
few months there have been marvellous de-
velopments in Australian commerce. This
country, as has been mentioned on many occa-
sions, is a deeply-indebted place. It has
followed the primrose way to the three balls
of Lombardy until it has to pay between
£14,500,000 and £15,000,000 a year in inter-
est to. its foreign creditors, merely to pre-
vent its indebtedness from becoming any
worse than it already is. That is to say, if
it can export, in goods (which include silver
and all the base metals) and in gold, about
14£ million pounds worth per annum more
than it imports, and can do this without
reducing its local stock of money, it is pay-
ing its way. If it does better than that it is
reducing its debts. If it does worse than
that it has started a downward movement
which, if the movement isn’t checked, will
lead to low wages, long hours, poverty, hun-
ger, failure, pauperism and insolvency.
The foreign trade returns for the first
seven months of 1912 are now available.
They show that exports have somewhat dim-
inished, owing to poorer seasons and, in some
degree, to lower prices. A lot of people have
been clamoring for decreased cost of living,
and if the selling prices of this country’s
exports continue to go down they will get
it. The returns also show that imports have
increased far beyond the country’s capacity
to pay for them. The only visible reason is
that Australian wages have risen so far, that
the tariff, which hasn’t risen, no longer gives
the Australian worker an advantage; in fact,
that there are, outside Australia, low-wage
and long-hour artisans, or extra-expert arti-
sans, whose goods can pay the miserable
little duties and still undersell those of the
Australian. This is the record up to the
end of July
7 Months 7 Months
- to 31/7/Tl. to 31/7/’l2. Increase.

The statement may be complicated, yet
the principal features are obvious enough.
During a period of seven months the Com-
monwealth, by reason of its foreign debts,
requires to export, in goods and gold, about
£8,500,000 more than it imports in order to
keep its accounts square, and it requires to
do this without seriously reducing the local
stock of gold, unless it proposes to prepare
the way for a panic and a crash. During
the first seven months of 1911 the Common-
wealth failed to meet its obligations, so far
as' the mere export and import figures go, by
about £9,000,000, but as the exports of gold
didn’t nearly equal the production of gold,
the deficit was of little importance. The
country didn’t actually pay its bills; still it
had in hand the means to pay most of its
bills. But during the first seven months of
1912 the situation was different. The im-
ports increased immensely. The exports of
goods decreased. The , f gpld> production went
down disastrously and the gold* export went
up amazingly. With it all, imports were
much further in excess pf exports than they
were last year. Generally speaking, the
position is thus: So fgr as the year 1912
has 'gone, Aii’stralia has failed to meet its
obligations by /dmewhe.re between £8,000,000
and £9.000.001), and yet it has exported
much mure gold than 1 it produced. Conse-
quently. the banks have exhausted, or
gregtly reduced, (he, stocks of- money they
aoeff.mulated in London in the days when
Australian wages were not so, high, and im-
ports were not so' 'enormous, and when the
tariff held the balance more evenly between
the high-wage Australian and' the low-wage
Englishman. Fijrther, the banks have re-
duced their local stocks of coin to a serious
extent, just when the Australian output of
the precious metal is falling; and if the de-
mand for gold keeps on at the present rate,
and the output of gold diminishes at the
present rate, there is nothing ahead save
another great smash, and another long
dreary spell of poverty. The Australian
Governments’ have been already driven to <
the old, shiftless device of raising money in
England at high interest in order to keep the
boom afloat. And when somebody who wants
to borrow locally, even on the best security,
in order to develop the great natural re-
sources of Australia, and make money for
himself, and. incidentally, give employment
to the high-wage and short-hour worker, goes
to the local financial institutions, he is gen-
erallv met with the cold shoulder and the
hostile eye. So much gold has gone away
to pay for the goods of the sert and the
“scab” that the financial institutions are
holding on hard to what remains for the
protection of their depositors. If their cash
resources get down much lower, they must
begin to call in their advances. And that
means forced sales, and slump, and closing
up of industries, and poverty,, and pauper-
ism, and soup kitchens, and relief works, and
sand-shifting jobs, and all the old misery
over again. # #
The Australian Labor Government lias
“sunk the fiscal issue,” and declines, for
the present, to interfere with the tariff.
There is no reason why it should interfere
with the tariff if it can, while leaving the
tariff alone, find some means of checking
the outflow of gold, and the increase of
foreign liabilities, which arise through the
influx of more foreign goods than this coun-
try can honestly pay for. . The tariff ,is
neither a gospel nor a fetich, It is in-
tended to give work and good wages to the
people, and to put the, country into such a
position that it won’t require to pawn its
soul and its' prospects and ifs children to
the outside money-lender. If these things
can be achieved without a tariff, then that
institution is superfluous, and is of no more
value than a roofless dog kennel without
sides or a dog. Unfortunately, while the
Australian Government leaves the tariff
alone, it offers no other remedy for the pre-
sent difficulties. It is a slave to dry old
shibboleths that were evolved when the
Labor party had no prospect of securing
office. In the days when these precepts were
constructed, the party didn’t expect to bear
the responsibility of running the country, so
it merely provided for the easy job of criti-
cising the people who ran the country.
When the Labor men were not required to
carry the political burden, it was easy
enough to be neither Protectionist nor Free-
trade, nor anything else, just as a monkey
need have no views on naval tactics. But
the situation is difficult when the crowd
which refuses to take any responsibility for
the customs policy of the country has seized the
responsibility. Fisher and co. are people
with no fiscal opinions, who collect a salary
for having fiscal opinions. So far as this
matter goes, they are getting wages under
false pretences. In the struggle to avoid
committing themselves, they profess that
they are willing to raise duties when they
obtain power to raise wages to correspond,
which profession, considering that wages
have been going up for a long time, while
duties have been stationary, is the thinnest
kind of hypocrisy. It may save the face of
the Labor Government, but it doesn’t save
its character. The charge against Andrew
Fisher, the apostle of Labor and high wages
and short hours and White Australia, is
that, under his rule, this country has
become, more than ever before, the employer
of the slave, the sweated workman, the low-
wage workman, the long-hour workman, the
nigger, the Chinaman, the non-unionist —in
fact, the employer of everybody who is
repugnant to good Labor principles. These
accusations rest on the import returns, and
if Andrew Fisher has any evidence to
show to the contrary, it is time that he pro-
duced it.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Cost of an Unpatriotic Railway.
Further light has been thrown of late on
the lamentable Northern Territory railway
scheme, and it hasn’t tended to improve the
business. A new and unpleasantplement of
cost has been added to the matter. Even
before the new factor came in, the case
looked like a shameful sacrifice of the in-
terests of Australia to the interests of a
weakly-led political party. The Common-
wealth is pledged to the completion of the
transcontinental railway from Port Augusta
to Port Darwin —pledged hot only according
to the terms of the agreement whereby it
acquired the Territory, but by, the patriotic
necessity for opening up, settling and garri-
soning this great isolated possession, and
giving it, for defence purposes, .the shortest
possible line of communication with the
cities whence it may look for assistance in
time of need. But a party wants to substi-
tute for the short road to Adelaide a much
longer road to the inure remote and less im-
portant city of Brisbane, with a vast exten-
sion to the still more remote city of Sydney.
This line, instead of traversing the high
temperate tableland of the Territory, is to
invade the lowest, hottest and least promis-
ing parts of the Two eastern States. In fact,
it is to leave the Territory undeveloped. The
scheme is a purely State grab for Federal
unearned increment, but there are suffrages
behind it, and, under the party system, these
are not to be lightly disregarded. To keep
faith means losing votes in two States ; to
break faith also means the loss of votes,
but not so many votes. From a party stand-
point, the safest device, for the time at least,
is to be ’non-committal, to hang the matter
up as long as possible, to wait on the, chap-
ter of accidents, .and to leave the defence of
the North —the point which, by reason of
its nearness to Asia, wants defence most of
all —to chance or Providence for an in-
definite time longer. The railway can’t be
built from the south without the Govern-
ment committing itself as to the route, and
thus arousing the bitter animosity of some-
body. But it can be built from the north,
by way of a pretence at doing something,
down as far as the parting of the ways
without precipitating the quarrel, and if this
little bit of work is done gradually enough,
the very awkward decision may be post-
poned for quite a long while. That is the
course that it is proposed to adopt. No
doubt, as The Bulletin pointed out last
week, there are many objections to this
device from a patriotic point of view, how-
ever well it may suit party exigencies. So
long as the line is unfinished, which seems,
by present signs, to be a state of things
that will last for many years, building from
the north is merely supplying to any strong
enemy, who may secure Port Darwin and the
sea, so much more mileage of convenient arid
valuable and isolated- railway, whereby he
may overrun the country without any. possi-
bility of troops from the south coming to
oppose him. Six hundred miles of line
built from the south would carry Aus-
tralian soldiers well into the Territory,
while the invader at Port Darwin
would only have the present 145 miles
of railway to help his operations. The same
bit of work built from the north as an iso-
lated undertaking would give the invader
745 miles of line to assist his operations,
while the Australian troops wouldn’t have
a yard of iron road in the Territory at their
disposal. The southern idea is building for
Australia, while the northern one looks more
like building for Australia’s enemies to suit
party exigencies. And in addition to all
these drawbacks, it is now pointed out that
the unpatriotic method will be vastly the
more costly. It involves humping men and
stores and heavy material half-way round
Australia by sea, and then by rail, in order
to reach the scene of action, and paying
special wage rates because of the remoteness
and isolation of the job. Even on easy coun-
try where there are no private landlords to
be bought out, the invoice is estimated at
£lO,OOO a mile, which is about twice the
original guess. The Parkes-Condobolin
(N.S.W.) line, of similar gauge and also
over easy country, was built for ,£2085, and
the Dubbo-Coonamble line for £2516 per
mile. Making all allowance for the greater
cost of a railway that is to carry express
traffic, it is reckoned that the policy of
building from the north instead of the south
—building for our enemies instead of for
ourselves —is going to enhance the bill by
£3OOO per mile. Some very remarkable bills
are born of the Party Government system,
but this is a devil of a lot to pay for thi?
privilege of fixing a great work so that the
enemy will have, for many years, the bes(
possible chance of getting hold of it.

The Censure Vote that Failed.
There used to be—possibly there still is—
a saying among the simple-minded Metho-
dists of Wales and Cornwall to the effect
that a good man can afford to be a great
deal wickeder than a bad man “because the
Lord forgives much to His elect.” Conse-
quently it was the good man who sold the
worst adulterated goods, and had the most
dubious weights, and against whom it was
most necessary to lock the fowl house at
night. It may be that there is a similar
idea in politics, and it is held that the honest
party can afford to look more politically dis-
honest than the dishonest party, because it
has its honesty to fall back upon. The good
man of Wales is the Methodist Church mem-
ber. and the honest man of N.S.W. politics
is the Labor member. These reflections are
based on the fact that a motion of censure
propounded in the N.S.W. Assembly re the
case of Nielsen, M.L.A., was rejected with
great promptitude and by a fair majority.
Yet the Nielsen case doesn’t look credit-
able. That politician skied his portfolio
over a difference with his colleagues in the
matter of leasehold v. freehold. The part}
is committed to the leasehold principle, but
Nielsen considered, rightly or wrongly, that
his colleagues were, beginning to temporise
and hunt for the line of least resistance. A
man who threw up his portfolio on a point
of doctrine might vote against his party, on
the same point, and the Government majority
wasn’t large enough to endure shocks of
that sort. Or he might resign and stand
again as a man who had differed from his
party. In that case, the men he had differed
from might allow him to. be re-elected as an
Independent, which,, as matters stood; Would
probably, have been political suicide. Or they
might have put up a candidate against him,
and split the old Labor vote ; and, seeing that
the Labor majority at Yass in 1910 was only
408 in a vote of 5818, that proceeding .would
probably have made a present of the seat to
Wade, That would, almost certainly have
been political suicide. There is no direct
evidence that the party considered these diffi-
culties, but the fact remains that the posi-
tion was desperately awkward. And when
Nielsen was sent to the United States on
a rather hazy and indefinite mission, and a
very reliable enemy (Waddell) was in-
duced to pair with this very unreliable
friend, the position of the, : McGowen Gov-
ment was greatly .improved. Nielsßn
absent, but paired, with an opponent,
was equal to an assured supporter;
while Nielsen present was noth If Nielsen
had gone away to serve N.S.W. in the United
States on a salary, he would have liad to
resign his. seat, and the seat might have been
lost, and Waddell could not have paired
with him, for when he resigned there , would -
have been nobody to pair with.'. - But he
could, and did, go away with ah allowance
for expenses and retained his seat. The allow-
ance seems to be the magnificent stuu .of
£4 4s. (sometimes mentioned as £4) a day,
which makes £2B or £29 a week. There is
no evidence to show that Nielsen’s <?Nt
penses amounted to this or anything like
it; certainly the published record (if. { his.
travels doesn’t suggest that they did. And
if his “ex.’s” only amounted t<6 (say) half
his allowance, then the other half was pra<; !
tically ‘ salary,,,whatever ;it might be’’,called.
Then some people, possibly of an ejefra 'Un-
appreciative ’■ or . crudcly-hostile; : sort, reckon
that £1 a day for expenses plas ;tl^)', 'Parlia-
mentary salary, that Was going on all the
time, would have been good value- for the
work done, so -far as that wprk >hasfapneared.
Of course, the proceedings of the McGowen
Government were quite legal. In fact., this
writer can find nqthing in the;,letter of the
law to ■prevent it sending Nielsen,’ or any
other member to Byrock with £lO a-day for
expenses to study 'the dust- problem for the
hidden cause of anthills. But the business
doesn’t look pleasant, and if the N.S.W.
Labor Government finds its tombstone over
this affair, there will be no great cause for
astonishment.
The Alleged Degenerates of England.
Thus Dr. Forbes Winslow, the eminent
mind specialist, in a recent deliverance on
England :
The mental condition of the people is most dis-
quieting. Degeneracy is alarmingly on the increase,
and crime is becoming markedly more prevalent.
And, after all, what else can be ex-
pected? There are now in England and
Wales (or there were over a year ago
—doubtless the number has increased)
619 persons to the square mile, the popu-
lation having almost doubled in 50 years.
This represents the greatest case of
crowding on earth save that of Saxony, and
if England is taken by itself it would, per-
haps equal Saxony. Overcrowding in itself
leads to degeneracy. The births in lull


numbered 897,100 —a considerable number
of them, as in all countries, and especially
in densely peopled ones, the progeny of
people who should never have been allowed
to marry. As a set-off to this, 302,722
people emigrated (this is still dealing with
the case of England and Wales alone), and
as these wanderers almost all went to places
which have a more or less rigid system of
inspection, they, whatever their drawbacks,
must have represented a fairly good sample
of the material available. That leaves an
alarmingly large proportion of the inferior
material to breed and multiply under loose
and casual laws. Almost every European
country has loose and casual laws on the
subject of marriage, but no other country,
save perhaps Italy, has England’s record for
emigration, and that has a material bearing
on the case. It is a matter of simple arith-
metic. If 300 people are born, of whom, as
a general thing, 25 are more or less unfit,
that makes 75 desirables to 25 undesirables.
But if 34 of the former emigrate (and they
have to at least pass desirables, to be
allowed to emigrate to any place worth going
to), that leaves only 41 desirables to 25 un-
desirables, and the proportions become
alarming in the present, and still more un-
promising in the future. There are four
special features in the case: (1) The emi-
gration from England and Wales has grown
steadily, till last year it reached a record
figure. (2) The system of examination has
grown more and more strict in the places
where most of these emigrants go to, there-
fore they probably now represent, more than
thev never did before, the pick of the nation.
(3) Despite all this emigration, those who
remain behind are more and more crowded,
which isn’t good for them. And (4) there
never before were so many philanthropic
agencies at work, struggling to keep the unfit
alive, so that they may reproduce their
species, and hand down their debilities and
misfortunes to future generations. So it is
hardly wonderful that the insane of England
and Wales are increasing much faster than
the population, and that the chest measure-
ment of the British soldier is a diminishing
quantity. In Scotland and Ireland there is
still room to breathe, and swing a cat, and
grow a chest. Scotland has 160 people to
the square mile and Ireland has 136. But
England and Wales, with 619 people to the
square mile (they had only 344 in 1861),
and especially England, which is much the
more densely peopled of the two, are ceasing
to be good places wherein to raise big, healthy
folk of the kind who take an heroic size in
waistcoats. When population is being
crowded out, and yet onlv the better speci-
mens are allowed to go, something must
happen to those who remain behind.
A Sanctuary for Crooks.
Last week there died, in Sydney, an old
gentleman by the name of Cook, who was
able to recall a mass meeting of the citizens
and a subsequent deputation to Governor
Fitzroy, with the object of protesting
against the despatch of more convicts to
N. S. Wales. The convicts are still arriving,
but instead of coming from England they
hail mostly from the United States. And,
nowadays, hardly anyone protests—not even
the police or the State Law authorities.
Over six months ago this paper drew atten-
tion to the fact that a Yankee criminal
named Bower had been sentenced to “five
years in Australia,” by a U.S. Judge, or
sludge, or something, for robbery with vio-
lence. His record, according to the San
Francisco papers, was a bad one —“gun
practice,” jffie local term for shooting with
intent to kill, being apparently an absolute
hobby of his. The boat on which Bower
left his native land, to begin the expiation
of his crimes in this country, was known to
the N. S. Wales authorities. He didn’t even
take refuge under an alihi. The way was
clear and easy for those whose duty it is to
handle such matters, to head off this emin-
ently undesirable immigrant, demand that
he should write a piece in Choctaw or Ro-
mance or some such bizarre tongue, and in
the likely event of his failing, bump him
back to the country which had the ill-fortune
to produce him, as provided for by. Aus-
tralia’s admirable Immigration Restriction
Act. That was the course which The Bul-
letin urged six months ago. The authori-
ties elected enthusiastically to do nothing.
As one result, Edward Ryan, an inoffensive
burgher of Rozelle, was last month violently
assaulted and robbed by Bower, his cash
loss being £25. Also, since the only means
of livelihood of persons of the Bower breed
is robbery and kindred enterprises, various
other citizens have possibly to thank official
remissness for the loss at his hands of their
property and mayhap their very lives. In
Ryan’s case he was caught, but how often
he did the deed and wasn’t caught it is im-
possible to say. When a private individual
omits to perform some obvious duty and
thereby causes serious financial or bodily
dhmage to another, the Law leaps on him
with no uncertain feet. Thus, one who was
injured, through falling into a publican’s cel-
lar, owing to the flap of the same being left
off, .would have a sound and extensive claim
against the. owner of the beer-vault. On the
same principle private individuals who are
subjected to painful assaults and pecuniary
losses because of the gross carelessness of
State officials in regard to matters within
their province, have an equitable right to re-
dress against the State —or, better still,
against these negligent officials in their pri-
vate capacitv. Edward. Ryan, the victim
of Bower, Yankee thug and robber, should
clrtainly get his £25 back from the law,
official who ought to have administered the
Act but didn’t, together with a solatium for
the physical and. mental and other damage
he has endured, .since none .of his misfortunes
would have occurred had the said official
taken the trouble to do those things which
he is there to do. This paper would vastly
like to see the above point raised in some
fdrm. or another, if only for the reason that
it might tend to coerce the police into grap-
pling with at least one feature of the exist-
ing crime-wave. According to Svdnev Tele-
graph (6/9/T2)
A gang of-notorious American “crooks” of various
degrees of lawlessness, is in Sydney, and its num-
bers are being augmented with the arrival of each
American liner.
Where is the sense' in a country admitting
nptorious “crooks” and such like undesir-
ables when it has an elaborate mass of legis-
lative machinery expressly constructed to
keep their sort out;
Bespattering a National Principle with
Party Mud.
A significant and unedifying business is
the insistence by the bitterer Tories of Aus-
tralia, and by the anti-Australian press, on
every conceivable item that is calculated to
cast discredit on the system of; compulsory
service. After the first few yelps of protest
the idea of compulsory “training” instead
of “service” was accepted with something
like enthusiasm. Gradually, however, it
seems to have dawned on certain party tac-
ticians that there was something to be done
in the way of an alliance between anti-
militarism and capitalism. A careful selec-
tion of professedly hard cases among the
cadets who are prosecuted for shirking drill
is being made. One of the latest was that of
a lad who said he had to keep, clothe, feed
and wash and possibly boil a family of
allegedly vast extent. It included, if this
writer remembers aright, six aged grand-
mothers and eight small brothers and sisters
under one year of age, together with droves
of bedridden uncles and aunts. Harassed
by his domestic duties he starkly refused to
drill; though one opines that friendly neigh-
bors would cheerfully have undertaken suffi-
cient baby-washing, etc., to have left him
free to attend to his military duties for
about li hours per week, had he been seri-
ously disposed to fulfil the same. The pathe-
tic plight of this virtuous youth was indus-
triously held up to public view in virulently
anti-Labor sheets. The magistrate almost
burst with misery in the act of imposing on
him a nominal fine. The recalcitrant child-
minder intimated his intention of going to
gaol sooner than pay the unjust impost, and
withdrew proudly in an atmosphere, as it were,
of suppressed cheers, and bowling his
halo in front of him as if it were
a hoop. For several days Toryism
kept him in a niche of the sort occupied
by the Drummers of the Fore and Aft, the
Deck who stood on the Burning Boy, and
other juvenile heroes. By these means, and
by innumerable hints and innuendoes regard-
ing the tyranny and futility and “un-Bri-
tish” (whatever that may mean) character
of the system under which they are called
upon to serve, Australia’s future defenders
are being encouraged to mutiny and ma-
ligner. In pursuance of what,looks like a
deliberate* plan of campaign Onslow.
M.L.A., for Waverley, recently abandoned
the local:;volunteer regiment in which he
held >a harmless commission that would feed
out of the hand. He put on much nup—the
thing that becomes'dog when it is grown
up ; and exnlained last week in Sydney, as
he lias explained with wearisome iteration
elsewhere, that he left: the service because
he considered his “sword” was “stained” by
association with the infamous compulsory
system. His sword may not have been
stained with blood on a dozen battlefields,
but such as it is Onslow will not see it
blotted by. political crime. Yet this bellow-
dramatic fustian is unlikelv to deceive the
discerning. If a Liberal Administration had
been responsible for Australia’s present Re-
fence system, it is a Dreadnought to a toy
rowing-boat that Onslow Would not have
considered that his hardware was polluted
by its connection with what he now calls
“conscription.” Similarly, if the Laborites
had not put the Defence Act on the Statute
Book, the papers which tried unavailingly to
prevent Labor putting any legislation on
any Statute Book, would not now be so
concerned to discredit the measure. In
brief, the whole agitation which is at pre-
sent in progress against compulsory service
is the outcome of our demented Party Gov-
ernment system. Politicians like Onslow
and partisan newspapers of the Sydney
Telegraph stamp think they see a chance
of pinking the enemy through the latter’s
defence legislation. That in so doing they
stand to emasculate the only defence system
Australia is ever likely to have (for whatever
the changes in detail the broad principle
that military service must be compulsory is
going to stand) does not perturb these
agonising patriots one iota. They are so
absorbed being loyal to their party that they
have no time to worry about loyalty to Aus-
tralia.

Art, Letters, the Drama and John Hop.
Dear Bulletin, —The other day a Sydney
paper announced with smug self-satisfaction
that: —
The police are maintaining a vigorous censorship
of all moving pictures exhibited at the numerous
shows now established in the city and suburbs. As
a result of their careful watching, and the speedy
investigation of representations made by citizens,
many decidedly undesirable presentations have been
prohibited by the Chief Secretary, and other pictures
have been improved by the elimination of objec-
tionable scenes.
Parenthetically, it should be explained that
the paper referred to is one which con-
sistently visualises the police as a body of
Admirable Crichtons who not only could do
no wrong, or make a mistake, but who would
never by any chance, or conspiracy of events,
or concatenation of circumstances, allow,
permit, countenance, or be cognisant of any-
body else doing anything wrong, or unusual,
or irregular! It is the paper which always
carefully prints the full name, degree and
status of every cop, slop or John Hop who
may be in the vicinity of any corpse-finding,
runaway, football match, funeral or motor-
car collision; the paper that always keeps
“heroic conduct,” “magnificent coolness,”
“admirable presence of mind,” and similar
gurgitative superlatives in type, and which
always applies them to the presence and con-
duct of the Force upon all conceivable —and
a vast number of inconceivable —occasions !
Now, what I want to know, is, Why the
police are to be made censors of picture—or
any other shows? Also, who made them so?
Is a policeman ex officio a competent judge
and critic of Art, Letters and the Draum ?
Is he, necessarily, able to distinguish Use-
majeste from common or garden treason,
ribaldry from blasphemy, or indecency from
suggestiveness? Has he the brains, educa-
tion, experience, tact and culture denied to
999,998 out of every million persons—-which
alone should constitute the qualifications for
censorship? Is he born with them, or are
they handed to him with his first uniform,
or what? Does Day or O’Callagiian con-
duct a special School of Criticism, which all
probationers attend and triumphantly emerge
from, stuffed with knowledge of codes, classi-
fications, technique, ethical distinctions, legal
dittos and moral dittos? Are these para-
gons of policemen prepared to condemn at
sight and prohibit the film of “Hamlet,” “La
Tosca” or “Pink Dominoes,” while passing
the film of “The Worst Christian in
Katoomba” or the “Passing of the Star
Boarder?” Because, if so—and apparently
it is so, very much so! —Australians have a
right to know why. If our police can safely
be accepted at their own valuation, or at the
valuation of the aforesaid Sydney journal,
why then we ought to hold a national
Policeman’s Day to adequately express our
bursting pride! Also, if (as we are assured)
our slops are so eminently capable and
worthy to act as cepsors of Letters, Art and
the Drama, their ,pay should be instantly
raised fourfold,ifijt' least. Otherwise, we are
degrading ourseßmh by sweating a Peerless
Body—or else (lie John Hops are philan-
thropists of the largest size. Other countries
pa v their Censors of Plays anywhere from
£B5O to £2OOO a year—and even then' the
censors don’t always give satisfaction, not
even to the newspapers! Our censors are
getting only a few paltry bob a day, and how
much of the genuine, informed, critical
faculty can you get for a few bob a day?
Outside of Australia, that is. Here, appar-
ently, we are getting it-—tons of it —but how
much longer is such under-payment to be
tolerated by this enlightened community?
We pay our censors of boxing a better figure
than the intellectuals in uniform who “guard
our morals” for us; because, perhaps, while
any ordinary constable is de facto a compe-
tent critic of Art, Letters and the Drama,
fight-critics must be at least sub-inspectors!
Hence it is, too, that the most “prohibitive”
Chief Secretary Australia ever had was a
house-painter by trade. Awaiting patiently
some authoritative assurance on these points.
—Yours, etc., Clarence O’llagan.

Smash!
Dear Bulletin, —Consider the latest Mel-
bourne horror how it happened !! A
train leaving Spencer-street, Melbourne, f6r
Coburg suburb travelled op the Williams-
town line for a short distance, but immedi-
ately after passing the signal-box at Frank-
lin-street. it turned to cross the line of “cou6-
try” rails: running midway between the
aforesaid Williamstown line and that?which
runs direct't.o. CobUrg. The train from Flem-
ingtbn Agricultural Show Ground travelled,
as the Flemington racecourse trains travel,
on the central line, and was coming into
town whilst the Coburg train was outgoing.
And’ just at the crossing points the two
engines met face to face. The train for
Coburg, it seems, was a few minutes behind
table time. Had it been a fhw more seconds
late' the- train from Flemi ngton would bnVe
cut into the middle of it instead of striking
the .engine, .Given the combination of cit-
eumstanees that led to this disaster —the
lateness of one or other train, and the dis-
regard of a'signal by, one or other driver, or
a failiire in the working of a signal—a more
or les§ horrible-accident wgsvery likely jite
occur. \Vhy do vtralns-for Coburg leiivo
Soqhger-stredt oti the ; Williamste>wn instead
of the Cdhurg and !-run w-hatiseems To
be a prep’psfefoiisly unnecessary rink in prov-
ing the . route of “country” trains? : Some
excuse for the workers“ concerned in this
accident is evident in tile statements pub-
lished next morning. We are told : “It is
the general practice for the Coburg train to
cross from the Williams town rails and over
the country rails to get on to the line for
Coburg.” For this dangerous general prac-
tice the Commissioners who sanction such
traffic management are responsible. Again:
“Show specials follow the same route as
Flemington race trains, running through
North Melbourne, as a rule, on the Essen-
don (or country) rails. On the up or home-
ward journey, especially during ‘rush’
suburban hours —that is to say, after 5
o’clock —-each special is passed along from
one section to the other with the greatest
caution.” The “rush” hours had not begun
when the accident occurred, so the greatest
caution would not be considered necessary.
But the fact that caution —small, medium or
great—is expected of train controllers at this
point is an admission of its dangerousness,
for which the Commissioners are responsible.
The question that occurs to every layman
critic is, “Why does the train for Coburg
start its journey on the direct line to Wil-
liamstown, seeing that the Essendon and
country lines (which, at Melbourne’s great-
est holiday times, are required for a rapid
service of special trains ) run between the
direct lines to Williamstown and Coburg
respectively?” The explanation, apparently,
is that the Railway-Commissioners were not
content to have the terminus of the northern
suburbs lines as of yore at Spencer-street
station (which is the terminus for the Flem-
ington show and race trains), but decided
to book Coburg passengers at Flinders-street
in order to establish direct competition with
the tramway service to that unfashionable
district. Possibly one result of last week’s
tragedy will be the abolition of the perilous
Coburg train service between the two metro-
politan stations. Passengers for Sydney or
any other big city have to book at
Spencer-street. Passengers from these places
are turned out at Spencer-street. What is
good enough for the inhabitants of Ballarat,
Bendigo, etc., ought to be good enough for
the denizens of Coburg or any other northern
suburb. Because there was a trifle more
money per annum to be made by starting
the Coburg trains on the Williamstown line
at Flinders-street, the Railway Commission-
ers have been unnecessarily jeopardising the
crowded special trains that run to and fro
Flemington on 30 days of the year, not to
mention the lesser risk that other trains on
the country line are exposed to by the cut-
across system. It is all very well for these
autocratic colleagues and officers of the de-
parted Tait to say: “The junction was pro-
perly locked. When the junction is locked,
and the signal given for one train to cross,
the conflicting signals are supposed to be
locked.... no system can provide entirely
against the possibility of error on the part
of railway officials,” etc., etc. In this case
there ought not to have been any junction,
or signals, or locking and unlocking. The
system was- rotten in that it provided the
signalmen and engine drivers with a possi-
bility of error. It was especially rotten in
that it created this danger to the special
trains that run crowded to and fro Fleming-
ton during many hours of many days in; the
year. The sin of running Coburg trains
across the country lines below Spencer-street
is a scarlet sin, and the Commissioners com-
mitted it. Not the engine drivers and signal-
men. As for the rottenness of the rolling
stock which is invariably used for Fleming-
ton specials—the stinking second-class tinder-
boxes which cost the public thrice the normal
first-class fare to ride in—the Commissioners
lack the impudence to offer any excuse. The
Commissioners can only shrug their shoul-
ders and say “Sorry” when a passenger is
found dead in a crumpled railway carriage
because it —the carriage, not the passenger—-
was about 25 years too old to stand the
shock of a collision. —Yours, etc., O.K.
