The Weaklings,
“Henri Singemoine’s” letter: —
A glaring defect in the character of the McGowen
Ministry is its tendency to fraternise with, and on
occasion to humbly take advice from, the enemy.
The Ministry or man or other entity which habitu-
ally does this is bound to fall, especially where the
enemy is not exactly bursting with chivalry, and the
McGowen Administration has duly fallen in, not
once merely, but many times. Take its recent atti-
tude towards the Farmers and Settlers’ Association.
The F. and S. Assoc, is a frankly partisan body, com-
posed almost entirely of bovine hayseeds, in whose
eyes anyone professing Democratic principles 1 is on
the same plane with a dangerous malefactor. Yet,
the other day, Ministers gave the F. and S. confer-
ence a fictitious importance by attending its delib-
erations and speechifying at great length. Also,
they allowed Railway Commissioner Johnson to do
the same thing, and he showed his appreciation of
their idiocy by delivering a smart attack on
McGo\VEN-ism and all its works. Shortly after-
wards a Farmers and Settlers’ deputation called on
the Minister for Lands. On , this occasion, Beeby,
with unusual bluntness and horse-sense, requested his
visitors to cut out the political aspect of the Lands
question. (As Beeby and his Government stand immut-
ably for leasehold, and as the F and S. crowd had just
previously, by resolution, committed itself irrevo-
cably to freehold, the, reasonableness of the Minister’s
attitude is plain. Neither side could have convinced
the other on the broad nrinciple of the Lands ques-
tion, had argument raged for months; and it was
only business, therefore, to get, without purposeless
delay and. recrimination, to those subjects which
might produce some tangible results.) Beeby’s re-
ward for attempting to humor the absurd bucolics, in
the first instance, was an enraged attack in about 14
different quarters, and wild cries concerning Labor’s
oernicious habit, of “stifling free speech”—as though
it is a 'Minister’s duty to offer himself as a target for
all the verbal electioneering matter that his foes
feel like projecting at him.
The more recent attitude of the Ministry, in the
matter of Speaker Willis, is another example of the
same enfeebled tendency. Willis considered it ad-
visable, the other day, to fling the Sydnev Telegraph
out' of the N. S. Wales Assembly. It is not denied
anywhere that, in taking this step, Willis was within
his legal rights—as much within his rights as a bar-
man is who flings an objectionable citizen out of his
bar. Of course the usual old howl arose to the effect
that the Speaker’s action constituted the '“suppression
of. free speech.” The Telegraph actually had the
■nerve to tell its readers that Willis had caused it
“to be prevented from publishing the proceedings in
Parliament.” It is perhaps unnecessary to mention
that none- but a lunatic would swallow such com-
plaints as these 1 . Neither Willis nor anyone else
anticipated for a moment that the ejectment of the
Telegraph reporters would debar that paper’s readers
from getting precisely the same amount of Parlia-
mentary-news as they had been wont to achieve in the
past,. All that the treatment-meted out to the Tele-
graph amounted to, was a salutarv warning or grave,
public rebuke—the same sort, of penaltv that the
stewards at a well-conducted race meeting hand a;
jockey who is making trouble, by his refractoriness,
among the other lads. The Telegraph was solemnly
censured, and that, was really: all there was to it.
The question the’ McGowEN-ites ' might well have
debated before allowing themselves to be rushed by
Liberalism into a measure designed to “clip the
Speaker’s -wings;” as a Liberal sheet exultantly put
it, was: Had Willis reasonable justification for the
step he took? In this writer's opinion he had.
Uncle.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
London, Friday.—Professor G. Elliott Smith —a
graduate of Sydney Universitj', and now professor of
anatomy at the University of Manchester-—read a
paper before the anthropological section of the
British Association on the “Evolution of Man.” 'He
regards the orang-outang, the chimpanzee, and the
gorilla, not as ancestral forms of man, but as unen-
terprising members of man’s family. “They are
simply men,” the Professor argues, “whose . ancestors
chose physical strength rather than intelligence as
their means of livelihood.”- — Cable.
“The time is not yet ripe, ” he said,
And leaped upon a i, , gh.
Our tailed and hairy father fled
Upon the ground to seek his bread.
And where is Uncle now?
Some twenty thousand years ago,
Two hairy apes fell out;
The first was quick, the second slow;
To ev’rything he chattered “NO !”
And rolled his head about.
His head sloped backward from his brows:
Each arm was like a flail;
He liked destruction, sloth and rows,
And did his thinking in the boughs
While swinging by his tail.
He would not trust himself upon
The flat and treeless ground ;
His red eyqs in their sockets shone
When down the trunk his twin had gone.
And run off like a hound.
That was the parting of the ways;
It happened up a tree
In dim and unrecorded days ;
But lie who at the turnstile pays
Our Uncle still can see.
He glares behind the iron bars,
And cracks nuts with his jaws ;
Our self-respect it slightly jars
To think our ancestry he tars
With grimy, apish paws.
But, sad to say, he did not stay
Completely up his tree;
Long after Pa had gone away
His grandson, on a valiant day,
Came down the earth to see.
A thousand years or so behind,
He followed Father’s track;
And ever on the scornful wind
The voice of him with terror blind
Rang out: “Come hack! Come hack!”
He hobbled on from age to age,
Too frightened to return;
He went in horror stage by stage—
A coward slinking in a rage
To some mysterious bourne.
He altered as he went along;
His hair began to fail;
His mighty limbs were not so strong,
And something else he felt was wrong....
Alas! Where was his tail?
When he caught up, he sought a bough
Whereto his feet might cling;
And, as men know, he has it now.
(The Upper House, you will allow,.
Now holds this ancient thing.)
“The time is not yet ripe!” Of course.!
He fears that he will" fall!
Still down the ages, mad and hoarse,
It sounds with undiminished force-
The ling’ring ape’s recall! .

WHERE
EVERYBODY
GOES
It is just a year now since he embarked on the
stupendous job of altering one of the most disorderly,
inebriated and blasphemous of Assemblies into a de-
cently-behaved institution. Changes of that magni-
tude take a deal of accomplishing, even under the
most ideal conditions. Willis, in his efforts at re-
form, has been hampered consistently by that section
of the press which usually refers to itself as “re-
sponsible.” The Liberals in the N. S. Wales Parlia-
ment have answered automatically to whatever
promptings in the direction of violence and outrage
they received from their party newspapers. They
were informed, bn the day previous to Willis’s elec-
tion, that “of all the tempests that have swept
through the Legislative halls of the State, none will
equal the one Mr. Willis will face when he presents
himself,” etc., etc. Of course there was a riot, and
Willis had things thrown at him, and narrowly
escaped personal injury; just as there would be a
riot in a street or pub mainly frequented by Liberals,
if, in similar circumstances, someone got up on a
stump and mentioned that Willis was coming, and
that trouble was inevitable, and hinted that if, unfor-
tunately, the approaching traitor was torn to pieces,
it would be, after all, something of a boon to the
State. The morning after the uproar in the Assem-
bly, the Telegraph remarked complacently that the
whole affair was “humanly inevitable,” and on those
lines it has proceeded ever since.
The Telegraph has lately been making use of pre-
cisely the same incitements to Parliamentary violence
(no less effective from the fact that they were covert
and wary) as it?employed to such good purpose last
year. The moth disreputable spirits in the House
were getting busy in the desired direction, accord-
ingly. Willis was offered the choice between mak-
ing an example of the real offender, and letting the
Assembly drift back into the condition of chaos and
anarchy that characterised it before he took charge.
He made a bold move, and the journal which has all
along done most to foment strife went out briskly on
its ear. No Government which favors the mainten-
ance of law and order in the Assembly should have
objected to that occurrence, and what the McGowen-
ites were doing in the same galley with the Liberal
press in the matter is a mystery. To the writer it
appeals as yet another lamentable example of their
inability, as a party, to appreciate the right time
to turn starkly on the enemy and tell him to go to
Hades with his bogus and intelligence-insulting
grievances.

From the business paper of the House of
Reps:—
Mr. Bamford : To" move. .. .when any article or
paragraph appears in a newspaper reflecting upon
the. good conduct or integrity of a member which,
in the opinion of the said member, is calculated to
prejudice him in the eyes of the community, and the
member affected, by personal explanation or other-
wise, declares that the statements so made in regard
to himself are '.erroneous, misleading and injurious,
and the House in good faith accepts such statement,
no representative or representatives of the newspaper
implicated be allowed within the precincts of Parlia-
ment House unless, or until, the explanation or con-
tradiction made by the aggrieved member be given
in the aforesaid newspaper prominence equal to that
given to the offending article or paragraph.
And what about two more provisoes: (1)
That there must not be a three-column ex-
planation in reply to a three-line paragraph;
and. (2) that the reply must not be any
more dreary or less readable than the asper-
sion? Every editor will speak feelingly on
the first point, anyhow. Many an aggrieved
individual with a weak case sends a tankful
of MS. in reply to a charge that is no bigger
than tuppence, and when it simply can't get
in he explains to his friends that a c’rupt
press shut out his overwhelming refutation
because if it * had been published it would
have covered the Archimedean lever with
shame.
“F.C.,” from Melbourne :
We were coming back from the Show, and had got
clear of Flemirigton, and were running at an easy
pace down to Spencer-street. Behind was tne Hur-
ried day, with its dust and wind; before us the
evening meal, the fireside, or the theatre. The
compartment was comfortably filled, and conversa-
tion ran lightly round. All at once there was a
jerk, rather a violent jerk, and those facing the
engine were pitched head-first into those opposite.
While we were struggling, with just a dim feeling
of fear or doubt, there came a horrible, rasping,
tearing sound, as of some giant hitting a streetful
of wooden houses with an axe. Shivered glass flew
against our faces, the partition behind us ripped and
cracked, as we came to a dead stop—in perhaps 10
seconds. Then, for just as long as one could supnort
it, a deathly stillness—a silence of helpless fear.
After that, moans and screams, a wild scramble
through doors and windows, where possible. Many,
as they got out, though quite unhurt, ran 20 or 30
yards away from the wrecked train before they
stopped. Some sat down, their heads in their hands.
Others felt themselves, as though to be sure there
wasn’t a leg or arm missing. They went back and
looked at the wreck, shuddering. Help there was;
plenty. In less than an hour the last sufferer was
safely bandaged up in hospital. But few of the
survivors gave any help. They couldn’t. They
could hardly think or talk sanely till they got over
the memory of that ripning, smashing noise and the
awful silence that followed it. But for two of us
there was no fear, or pain, or suffering—nor any to-
morrow.
A certain Professor Smith, once of
N.S.W., and now of Manchester, in Grime-
land, has propounded an interesting theory
about the large apes. An old idea -was that
man descended from them, and that the
present apes are certain superfluous material
that remained —in other tvords, that a suffi-
ciency of ape was used up to make all the
man that -was required, and there Avas a cer-
tain amount of ape over Avhich never changed
or varied. Smith’s view is that the ape
really is man-—that he represents a branch
of the family which chose to develop its
muscles rather than its brains, anu owing to
the fact that the two sections stuck each to
its special line, progress caused a wide, con-
spicuous divergence to set in. The process of
separation began very early. When Adam
sat in his office in Cain-street, Genesis,
devising the first wild cat, he looked through
the window and saw his brother Mandril
coal-heaving on the wharf. So profound
thought ran in one family, and profound
coal-heaving in the other. But at last man
invented machinery and appliances and com-
plications, and lie found that they were
cheaper than his hulking, hairy, idiot rela-
tive, and, besides, the hairy relative was too
clumsy, and broke the machinery. So, as
the natural reward for long service, the sons
of Adam gave the sack to the children of
Mandril, and told them that they would be
shot if they w<?re seen about the' place, and
the children of Mandril fled without their
back salary, and took to the woods. Thus
did thfei gorilla differentiate himself from
the counterjumper, and they remain unlike
to this day—especially in the matters of
tight boots, hair oil qnd affability.

“Passers-by,” a new play by Haddon
Chambers, was produced last week at Syd-
ney Royal. Like so many modern dramas
of British origin, it features one of the 12J
millions of unemployables with whom the
United Kingdom is understood to be afflicted.
Samuel Burns, when urged to work, objects
feebly that the thing is impossible, work
being “for workmen.” He has never had an
opportunity to learn the art, and does not
wish to, any more than he desires to wash or
shave. He drifts into the piece, off the rain-
swept streets, at an early stage, and at the
finish drifts back whence he came to resume
the frozen hell on earth which is his life.
Even that sturdy optimist, the dramatist,
does not attempt to adumbrate a happy fin-
ish for Samuel. He is simply indicated
hopelessly, like some dreadful oleograph in
a gallery of old masters, and then an effort
is made, as it were, to forget him, and be
cheerful again. Which is strikingly illus-
trative of the way society at large, in the
Cold Country, treats the simmering volcano
that is forever being fed by persons of the
Burns stamp. With the exception of the
social misfit, who has no real connection
with the action of the play, the characterisa-
tion is commonplace and unconvincing. Pine,
the intolerantly conservative man-servant, is
employed to throw up, in high relief, on the
one hand the splendid broad-mindedness of
his superiors, as regards the lower orders,
and. on the other, the squalid and uncivilised
habits of the submerged. He is but a faint
shadow of a human being. The time is
surely ripe • for the death and burial of the
ancient convention which insists that all
stage servitors shall be the lineal descendants
of Thackeray’s incredible Jeames von Yel-
lowplush. There may be thoughtful, philo-
sophic, poverty-stricken, intellectual and
withal contented and humble old cabmen
such as Nighty in London. However, the
writer, as the outcome of years of experi-
ence of this class, makes hold to doubt it.
Nighty is another conventional stage figure
of the mendacious order.
“PASSERS-BY," AT SYDNEY ROYAL.
Harcourt Beatty as Peter George Miller as
Waverton. “Nighty" the Cabman.
The plot is simple, and with the addition
of a few grains of credulity, may be swal-
lowed by any carper. Peter Waverton
“wrongs” his step-sister’s governess. The
distracted girl flees in disorder into the vasty
unknown of business life, and Peter, though
he tries desperately, is unable to find her —
London, as it is explained shrewdly, being
.“a large place.” So he gets engaged after
seven years, to another. The wronged lady
comes and coughs violently on his doorstep
by night. She does not know it is his door-
step, though he is a figure in society, and
though she has never ceased to adore him.
She merely recognises that it is a doorstep.
He brings her in, and learns for the first
time that he has a son. After a certain
amount of sentimental-by-play, the girl to
whom he is engaged gives him up with a
sportsmanlike goodwill that borders on the
hilarious, and he clasps the faithful ex-
governess to his errant chest.
* * ? *
Harcourt Beatty grapples valiantly with
the trying role of Waverton. He does well in
the emotional scenes, but so far as creating
the desired atmosphere is concerned, he fails.
As this paper has remarked before, Beatty’s
striking mannerisms handicap him in the
portrayal of a modern young man of fashion.
The flashing eye, the convulsive workings of
the brow, the lip writhing in scorn —all of
which are brought into action on the scan-
tiest provocation—would be received with
stupefaction and stern disapproval at (say)
the Bachelors’ or White’s. They might go
well enough at the Green Room Club or the
Savage, but then the Waverton type rarely
visits such resorts. Miss Hilda Spong’s rep-
resentation of the governess is full of quiet
charm and pathos. Some fine character-act-
ing is done by Robert White, George Miller
and A. J. Patrick ; and Miss Bessie Major
and Miss Dorothy Cumming are altogether
admirable.

When a bogus company promoter finds
such an easy proposition as Battlesburg, he
is not likely to leave the place till he has
got every dollar in the till; and J. Rufus
Wallingford shows no .(signs >df departing
from Sydney Criterion for months yet. He
must have made some hundred millions of
dollars out of his covered carpet tacks since
his arrival in Australia.
* * * *
The Bulletin is disappointed. It was
promised “sensations in spasms” and “whirl-
winds of surprises” at “Her Forbidden Mar-
riage,” at the Adelphi, on Saturday; but
during the whole five acts there wasn’t one
sensation which could lawfully be accused of
being, a “spasm”; and the “whirlwinds of
surprises” all subsided to gentle breezes of
expectations. The plot is the old, weather-
worn one of the villainous gent, who wishes
to marry a wealthy girl, s who doesn’t want
him, and, with the necessary melodramatic
complications, marries someone else. Then
all would have been satisfactory, and the
audience could have gone home, but the vil-
lain decided to have another attempt, and
contrived to dump the hero —the girl’s hus-
band—into gaol, and proceeded to become a
nuisance. But just as he had almost suc-
ceeded in being a profound bore, the hero
escaped from gaol and—then the “house”
went home. There is for about 2| hours
out of three a very noticeable superabund-
ance of “funny man,” and writer recommends
a generous use of shears—or, if there are
no shears handy, cannon would do, or a
crocodile, or a mad elephant, or poison, or
Judgment Day—to exterminate about 98 per
cent, of him. The other 2 per cent, might
be stunned. Hugh Buckler is a big
success as the inventor-hero, and brings down
the house, in his argument with his rich boss
by exclaiming, “Well, it’s Labor against
Capital, and, take, my word, Labor will win,”
which it does. Violet Paget gaits the entire
sympathy of the house as the wealthy maiden
aforesaid. The treachery is divided among
three: Jennie Pollock, as the second wife of
Sir George Lancaster; Godfrey Cass, the
villain’s understudy; and Charles Lawrence,
the villain proper, who allows himself to be
flouted, time and again by the stipendiary
humorist. Each is remanded for sentence.
Elwyn Hervey, as servant in the house of
Lancaster, does nothing in particular, but
does it well.
** * *
When the English mail is a day or so late,
the supply of films for the Sydney picture
shows doesn’t come to hand to time, so the
shows have to get along as well as they can
under the circumstances. And that means
that they have to make a choice from their
old stock. Thus, on Monday, the J. D. Wil-
liams co. couldn’t stick to its promises,
though for the rest of the week the original
programme will be followed. All the same,
on Monday each of the four Sydney theatres
had a very good series to unroll. The second
edition of the Crystal Palace is still proving a
big draw. The machines are changed each
fortnight, so there is always something new.
Neptuna, the young American swimmer, who
is putting in a season in a glass tank, is still
drawing well.
* * * *
At the National Amphitheatre one Hugh
Huxham is presenting a band of serenaders.
They are habited as Pierrots, but in other
respects are the same old Serenaders that the
world has known from the days of Old Cas-
tile down to those of Manly Beach. Gilbert
Lauri’s turn, “In 1950,” is amusing, and Roy
Redgrave and Mab Paul appear in a cheerful
playlet. For the rest, the Olracs, Victoria
Cross, and Mabel and Malfe continue to
decorate the bill.

The Tivoli bill had nothing fresh to say
last week-end. The balancing Schenck
Brothers were mentioned with pride, and
Minnie Kaufmann, trick cyclist, was referred
to with arrogance. Juggler Chinko was
trumpeted loudly as “wonderful.” and Rich
and Rich were hurried to the footlights as
“real racy comedians.” Then the jolly
Sprightly girls were confidently presented
and the public were called upon to admire
Jessie Stuart’s voice. But, after all, the fact
remained that none of these performers were
quite new, and the novelty which is the spice
of entertainment was lacking.
“The Girl from Wayback” is still holding
the frying-pan up to Nature at the Palace,
and shows no disposition to cease. The
managerial advertisement speaks very-highly
of her, and asserts that the season is boom-
ing. The loud, gong-like note of a falling
frying-pan will probably not be heard until
the indignant voice of the next show is raised
in argument at the stage door.
The “Nightbirds” fly from Her Majesty’s
(Sydney) on Friday evening. Next night
(Saturday) “The Chocolate Soldier” returns,
and storms the Pitt-street roost as gallantly
as any farmburner, for the sake of the
golden eggs laid in the pay box by the cack-
ling public.

At Adelaide Royal, the Anderson co. has
turned from the romance of the Beggar-
Maid Princess, which, though steep, not to
say perpendicular or even overhanging, was
credible, to that of the telephone-wire' and
the faked idol, which aren’t. The title,
“When London Sleeps,” is conscientiously
altered to suit one lurid third-act scene,
“While decent London Sleeps.” The vari-
ety houses have wisely strengthened their
programmes on the showy or athletic side.
The three Alginas are back at the Tivoli, and
Vivian and Alton continue to assault and
batter each other. The musical side is very
strong, too. At the King’s, Marguerite and
the Hanleys are back, and several American
mixed pairs exchange amusing insults.
Wirths’ circus has just established itself at
the old Exhibition for Show week, which
promises to be a lively period. The dhief
picture houses are strongly dramatic —apart
from their reproduction of Wattle Day and
other local scenes: West’s with the Zenda-
like drama, “The Black Chancellor”; the
Empire with “Comrades in Arms,” or the
two enemies who stop shooting each other
to shoot jointly at savages ; and Wonder-
graph with the spy-and-fetters thriller,
“Saved from Siberia.” The last-named
house has also been showing the adventures
of an escaped panther, who proved a far
better low comedian than most humans who
haunt the filmy stage at present.
At Melbourne Princess’s, where seasons
come and go like shadows on the sands of
time, a revival of “A Girl’s Temptation” is
promised by the Marloughby management
for Saturday next. And one week of that
alleged record-buster will close the present
season, it is said. Meanwhile, .“Brought to
Ruin” continues to carry the ill-fated Baby
of the girl who fell from the washtub to
become the victim of an eminent Iv.C. The
authoress, of which sad sweet story is the
same British matron who committed the
bellowdrama which .will ensue on Saturday.
It seems . about time for an Australian
woman-writer to steep her pen in impro-
priety and scribble off a four-act play en-
titled “A Flapper’s' Folly, or How Bella
Won the Bonus.”

Ilugard is the audacious deceiver who pro-

ball-cartridge,, and a bloodthirsty populace
is. rolling up in the Amin hope that a bullet
will lodge in the magician’s meat instead
of falling harmless on his dish.

The clowning Webbs are back at the
Opera House for a few nights, and some of
their methods of making music are different
from any of their previous novelties. The
Webb turn is ingeniously woven and har-
moniously designed, and some of their inci-
dental antics are even funny. But why do
they ever talk? Where silence is golden it
is folly to introduce a tone of tin. Aubrey
Mallalieu and co. in their comedy sketch,
Hurley with his picks, La Freya, the Rallis-
Wilson trio, and many other people, are
competing for the plaudits of the mixed mul-
titude at Rickards’s. Some of them will
drop out of the competition this week.

Flappers spent some tearful moments at
West’s Palace, Melbourne, on Saturday, when
“At the Last Moment” was given a run.
The film hails from Berlin or thereabouts,
and contains about half a mile of hugger-
mugger ending in yards of death-bed a la
Mimi and Camille. A large piece of Turk-
ish mystery was “The Three Sultanas,” a
Pathe colored picture of a bad old Sultan
who, after long and expensive polygamy, is
gradually convinced that one wife is better
than 9S, and talks less, and wears fewer
clothes. “Their Golden Anniversary” flick-
ergraphed a dear old story that attracted the
multitude; and “The Spirit Awakened” re-
counted the tale of a good, Kerristian youth
who paid off a mortgage by self-denial and
prayer—partly one and partly the other,
but chiefly" both.

A number of the very naicest amateurs
have conspired to act on behalf of the

*
Among the swag of pictures at the Prah-
ran Lyric (Vic.) last Saturday evening, was
“The Turning Point,” a sermon for husbands
who lead the tight life and spend a lot of
their nights apart from their wives. In the
picture the young wife teaches her lord a
lesson by ipeans of a bogus note and a tear-
ful baby, and he promises to reform and go in
for early bed for the rest of his mixed
career. “Love’s Test” gave precious moral
tableaux of two young people who regard
true love as more important than a moun-
tain of dollars. In the end, of course, they
get both. That is the way things go in
books and dramas and films. In real life
people give up dollars for love, and the love
goes, while the dollars stay away. Time
rolls on, and the miserable desire for the
plunks gets the young couple down. They
revile each other because they sacrificed the
rocks for a shadow. They see the splosh in
their dreams, and the brass in their waking
hours. They grow slovenly and prematurely
middle-aged, and take to living in cheap
boarding-houses, and breakfasting in bed, and
drift into scandal and curl-papers and faded
wrappers and red noses. In fact, they find
life extremely flat, like salt that has lost its
savor, and when they think of their early
romance they would fain kick it all round
the backyard, but the lack of a commodious
backyard in a hash tower makes that impos-
sible.
Premier Peake says his Government in-
tends to complete Adelaide Parliament
House, so that it may stand as a monument
in marble of a fixed determination to uphold
State independence and State Frights. He
has since stated that instead of building the
new wing in solid marble, like the old part,
the Government will merely face the walls
vAh a veneer of marble “to save expense.’’
So the monument of State. Frights is to be
a whited sepulchre—ra pine circumstance
with a cheap veneer—a lady with a painted
face —a tlfing that is granite and Queen
Anne in front, and lath-and-plaster and ash-
barrel and Mary Ann behind.
“Galleryman” says:—
Last week, > in Melbourne Divorce Court, a plasterer
named John JThomas Smith asked for a divorce from
his wife on the ground of desertion. The wife, who
was 29 last birthday, lived with her husband only
five days, smoked, used hostile expressions and stayed
out at night. When Smith married he was a
widower, with seven children, and the unblushing
bride was a widow with none. Mrs. Smith delivered
a short address to the household before she finally
left. It was this: “To Hades with the lot of you!’’
Counsel for said they had “scoured the
country” for! respondent, without coming up with
that swift lady. The name of this lawyer, who per-
haps joined >nn the chase, was Woolf. The five-day
wife was giyen another chance to get a husband,
which seems, rough on the possible husband. I hold
that she should have been disqualified, and struck off
the roll and her certificate suspended.
“Mr. Ocky”: C. L. Forrest, Victorian ex-
M.L.A., who shuffled off this mortal coil a
few days ago, after haying reached the 74tli
timestone, was in Parliament as member for
Polwarth almost continuously from 1886 to
1911, when he gave it best and made a halt
to consider the state of his health. He was
rather a strange individual —a big Scotch-
man, who said little and smoked a great
deal. Indeed, I have never seen him without
a cigar. He was not much of a politician,
but he was generous and honest. Forrest
never once delivered a real speech in the
House. As an early settler he had some
rough experiences, and, of course, was in the
gold rushes (some of the stones for the
Eureka stockade were taken from his
claim). Forrest (Vic.) is named after him.
It is his only child.
The jubilee of McDouall Stuart’s trans-
continental march was celebrated only just
in time. Whatever a man’s constitution may
be, half a fcentury after he has done any big
work may find him pretty much on the edge
of things. Adelaide banqueted the survivors
of Stuart’s ’party on July 26; and on Sep-
tember 2 the besl known among them was
dead. Pat Auld Was not only with Stuart,
but also with Finniss when the first capital
°f the Northern Territory was selected at
the mouth of the Adelaide River. After-
wards he did very well for himself with the
Auldana vineyard and in a wine and spirit
business in Adelaide. Pie was a prominent
amateur actor and huntsman, and when he
got elderly it was one of the sights of the
local hunt to see him dismount, climb a
fence, and then call his pony, which jumped
over after him. George Auld, the well-
known rowing man, is his son.

AT POVERTY POINT.
“Stargazer”: It is false pretences to call “Ben
Hur” a play; it being merely a procession of events
with colored illustrations. It is a pieture'.film drajrta-
tised. You jump from one chromatic happening to
another, and the dialogue in between is of little
consequence. It is often allowed to become ex-
tremely dull, when preparations for the next climax
are somewhat delayed, and it is always superfluous.
With the present staging, “Ben Hur” might as well
be a dumb show. In fact, its interest would be
considerably increased if every performer were smitten
speechless at 8 p.m. There is not an incident in it
that needs to be told, which remark the gifted man-
agement may take as inferring that the action is
very “telling.”
Last week the industrious Miss Margaret Basker-
ville made a spread of her artistic goods at her
studio in Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, and a few
people mottled her show with the small red wafer of
commendation. The display contained the usual
platitudinous studies of fruit and flowers and one or
two figure subjects for the sensitive eye to ache about.
And it was interesting in including Bft'. of the late
Tummy Bent in clay, the object being a model for
the ultimate casting of that deceased hustler in
bronze. Bent, as Miss Baskerville sees him, looks
like an ex chef-d’oeuvre in human architecture. A
head that is a fairly passable likeness of Tom sits
nobly, rather than bull-neckedly, on a figure clothed
in a perfect frock-coat and unimpeachable pants.
The right hand clasps the usual rolled document, the
left is outstretched in conventional affability, and
the whole pose of the thing expresses; the dignity of
a picture in the Tailor and Cutter. But where
Miss B. has been most idealistic is in giving Tommy
a front of elegant contour. In life Bent had a pro-
vision department that sagged most obtrusively. But
his' sculptress has shored up his bingey by lining
the interior with the woodwork of a barrel (stuffed
with oddments), and has obtained 'an embonpoint
that is something more than a picture.' It is almost
a poem. Posterity will get a beautifully incorrect
notion of Thomas Adolphus Bent from his bronze
memorial at Brighton.
“Floater”: Gregan McMahon talks of giving Mel-
bourne more Shaw during his next Repertory season.
“Man and Superman” is foreshadowed, provided
McMahon can find a means of presenting Shaw’s
glacial hell and other scenic effects —including a full-
sized motor-car—on the limited of the Athen-
aeum Hall. Also, there is a possibility of the newer,
and considerably heavier, “Getting Starried,” being
submitted to Gregan’s patrons. Aljl will depend,
however, on the sweet reasonableness of one or two
players who want to be the entire show, whatever it
-is, including hell and the backcloth and the foot-
lights. They were given fat parts in the past, with
the result that the fat has gone to the top part of
them.
The amende honorable, or some such ceremony,
was made recently by a soulful-looking artist of the
Kirkby Lunn Company, who had been motored to
Healesville in order that he might inhale the wattle
perfume while drinking in Australian scenery. Dur-
ing luncheon he was introduced to a bunch of mis-
chievous girls, and rapidly found his limited stock
of v Ze Englis’ was being worked overtime. Pre-
sently the climax arrived in the shape of a query as
to his opinion of the Australian girls. With upturned
eyes, he declared they were the “most vol-wp-tuous”
he-had ever seen. A male friend whispered that this
could hardly be regarded as complimentarv, and the
visitor hastily explained that he understood the term
meant, “beaii-ti-ful,” adding, with great earnestness
that, if he had “offended m’amselle, he was b
sorry. He accentuated the Great Australian Adjec-
tive with charming empressement.

The alleged salaries of not a few music-hall per-
formers must be swallowed with salt; still,., the
wages paid to the top-notehers are amazing. ' The’ Bir-
mingham Theatre Royal Co. has just recoverei!U£ ! sso
and costs from an insurance co. It secured Wilkie
Bard for its Christmas pantomime, and insured
against the risk of his non-appearance through ill-
health for £5O for each performance at which he
failed to transpire. He was attacked by quinzy and
missed 11 performances. It came' out in evidence
that Wilkie’s contract with the theatre provided for
eight performances per week, at a salary of £225.
But it is. a case of making hay between showers, and
England is a wet climate. Wilkie is just now at
the top of the tree, but he may be an extra turn at
a guinea a night ere long, and after that he may be
a scene-shifter.
Oswald Stoll, the Melbourne-born rusher who runs
the London Coliseum and a score of other variety
theatres of less renown, has issued a proclamation
against the Sunday opening of the picture palaces.
He is not keen upon the opening of the regular
theatres and music-halls on Sundays, preferring to
spend the Sabbath in his library or his garden
(Oswald is something by way of being a semblance
of an author), but he believes in fair play all round.
A million people, he says, pay for admission to the
picture palaces every Sunday, and, the amusement
fund in their pockets is depleted to that extent, to
the detriment of the theatres and the music-halls
that are not allowed to open. That state of things,
he contends, is not cricket. It is hitting the drama
below the belt, and amounts to a kick in the
stomach. Nominally the Sunday receipts at the
picture palaces go to charities, but that is a mere
blind. Out of £60,000 taken at the London picture
palaces on Sundays during a given period, only
£6OOO went to charities, and charities were very
lucky to get that much.
J. M. Barrie is not a hustler in the matter of
play-writing. He takes his time, and that is why
his work is always so neat and polished. Four years
have passed since a new full-sized play from his pen
was produced in London, but now we have the defi-
nite promise of one ere long. Charles Frohman
announces that he will present a ' three-act drama,
by Barrie, in February. Frohman is also having a play
specially written by that old Sydney boy, Haddon
Chambers, who, he adds, “is now living in a little
riverside cottage, in order to concentrate his attention
on the work.” Frohman says Chambers’s new piece
will be “of somewhat stronger texture than ‘Passers-
By, although the comedy element will predominate.”
“Hullo”: Pleased to learn that ip New York meio-
drama has died of poor circulation and the failure of
box-office receipts. According to a recent book,
moving pictures struck the death blow. Only seven
theatres in New York were presenting melodrama in
1905. Now three are devoted to the film, two to
“H, e . s 0 ue > one to vaudeville, and one to drama in
Yiddish.
“S.S.”: Saw a film production of “Lady Audley’s
Secret the other night, in which the hero dashes off
*■o Australia, and makes his fortune scooping gold
out of the most unpromising creek I ever set eyes on.
lhe methods of the gold-seekers were elaborately
portrayed. They ran down into the creek pell-mell
like a crowd hunting an escaped canary, dug a hole
ln .,. th ® unlikely water-way, examined the shovel
critically, then scampered off to prospect in fresh
places. It was always the same “crick” though.
Ihe hero struck it in large lumps. He filled a dish
that looked like a cake tin, wagged it in the stream,
and then hooked out about half a cwt. of nuggets
But the cradle” amused me most. It was a shal-
low, square structure, with two flat slabs for legs
and might have been the food-box of the party’s
patient neddy. 'The man on my right assured me it
was a contrivance in which the simple people of
Cornwall put their babies to sleep.

AN IRON SUITOR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Eric Maxon, as the hero in “Ben Hur,” does most
of his love-making in armor, with the result that
Miss Dorothy Dix, who is appearing as Esther, sus-
tains many severe bumps and abrasions from contact
with the metal. —News item.
Sweet Esther has a dinted nose,
A scratch or two upon her chin,
Upon her head two bumps repose,
Her pretty ear is twisted in.
These towers of blood and iron, no doubt,
Are excellent in war, but when
The game is love, the kind of men
Who’re in demand are soft and stout,
With not a trace of iro-n about.
Ben Hur involves in metal holts
The daughter of Simonides,
He jabs her cheek against his bolts,
His gauntlet gives her hand a squeeze,
And breaks a nail. His buckles hurt,
lie hugs her to his steely breast.
Imprints upon her lip his crest,
The gods no sympathy assert
For Benno’s mangled bit of skirt.
Upon the stage chain-males are not
In hot demand as lovers now,
For Esther has a little lot
Of damages upon her brow,
Where gorget rough and visor ill
Have scraped and bruised the surface oft.
In Love's canoodling sweet and soft.
Poor lass, she feels that she is still
Enamored of a rolling mill!
VICTOIUA.
EDDYSON.

ARITHMETIC AND ART.
EMPLOYER: "What we most require op Our
lady clerks is correctness in figures.”
EMPLOYED : “Oh, you’ll have nothing to com-

Adelaide hotelkeepers are not likely to
follow Sydney’s lead in abolishing the coun-
ter-lunch. They mostly lay it out from 11
till 12 only—having one o’clock dinners com-
ing on —and find it no great expense, though
a good deal of trouble. But the chief point
is (as they tell the Adelaide dailies) that
the unemployed, or “Domain loafer,” is
practically unknown in that city, and nobody
wants to make a meal off the “snack.”
* * * *
An Anglican church, costing about £5OO,
has just been built at Lismore, near Cam-
perdown (Vic.), and in the published list of
donations to the building fund there appears :
“Society for the Propagation of Christian
Knowledge (London), £25.” This is a
British society that exists to. spread the
gospel among the heathen. Lismore is .in
the heart of the Western District, which
is one of the most fertile sections of the
Commonwealth. It is the home of the
squatter, and swarms with motor-cars. Yet,
to build a little wooden church, it goes cap
in hand to Bull for a subscription. Is it
any wonder that a large section of Bull-
Land regards us as uncivilised? It is pretty
certain that the subscribers to the S.P.C.K.
think that their money is spent for the
spreading of Good Tidings amongst the
blacks, and that Lismore is a collection of
mia-mias round a head-house, instead of a
collection of squatter homesteads.
Very often you can hardly see the dead
whale for birds, and this seems to be a case
in point. When the late Dr. Black, of Mel-
bourne, died, he left sundry properties, in-
cluding that choice city site, Queen’s Walk,
on the corner of Collins and Swanston
streets, to certain beneficiaries, to whom an-
nuities were to be paid up to March 13, ’OB.
Then the trustees were ordered to sell the
properties and distribute the proceeds.
Queen’s Walk, however, though valuable,
proved unsaleable at a decent figure, so the
trustees held on. Certain, beneficiaries asked
the Supreme Court what it thought about
their action, and Justice Cussen opined that
the trustees were right, and could keep on
paying annuities till the blessed Walk, or
Run, or whatever it is, was sold. Five or
six expensive barristers, instructed by as
many firms of equally expensive solicitors,
took a hand in this argument. Two bene-
ficiaries, however, were dissatisfied with the
decision, and took the Full Court’s opinion
(Justices Madden, Hood and Hodges). The
Pull Court listened to the arguments of an-
other half-dozen costly gentlemen learned in
the law, and opined that Justice Cussen was
wrong, and that the trustees should sell at
once and divide up the estate. But it ap-
pears that these two conflicting judgments
both stand on the records, which, to the legal
eye, is untidy and improper, and liable to
create a dangerous precedent. So “it was
suggested” to the beneficiaries that another
Complete Court should be asked to formally
upset one or other of the decisions, and put
matters in straight legal trim ; and last week
Justices a’Beekett, Hodges and Hood listened
solemnly while nine erudite and costly poten-
tates argued the tremendous point afresh.
The Court reserved its decision —while the
legal profession retired to get its second
wind. There still remain the High Court
and the Privy Council, and, if the matter is
delicately handled, and the estate lasts, quite
a lot of excitement may yet be squeezed out
of the late Black’s savings.
Just in the middle of Victoria’s Police
Chief O’Callaghan’s recent expatiations on
the merits, popularity and general satisfac-
toriness of the “force,” the monthly examina-
tion of candidates happened along. Of the
192 applicants called up, 76 had either got
better billets, or couldn’t be bothered to turn
up. That left 116, 82 of whom were rejected
through failing to reach the physical standard.
Remained 34. of whom nine couldn’t pass
the simple educational exam. The 25 rem-
nants, or superior dregs, then stood before the
doctor, who fired out four. So of the orig-
inal 192 only 21 finally arrived at the honor
and glory of a baton and 6s. 6d. per day.
The worst of it was that, no sooner had these
21 got into the force, th,an they began, with
others recently joined, to consider how they
could get out of it. Their pay is so low
that even the most casual unskilled laborer
would scorn it. Their present lodging—
Russell-street barracks—is a comfortless,
unhealthy and unclean place, and their board
or mess contribution has lately gone up from
£2 15s. to £3 10s. per month per man. They
had got as far as the preliminary arrange-
ments for forming a union when the wild
O’Callaghan heard of it, and gave every man
in the barracks two days to . state, in writing
whether (1) he had given any information
to the press regarding the condition of the
force; (2) whether he wished to resign from
the force. The absurd Questions were framed
so that O’Callaghan could flaunt the answers
in the face of the public as proof that every-
thing was as it should be; and he got the
replies he anticipated with great unanimity.
But 97 out of the 105 dwellers in the bar-
racks tacked on a rider which O’C. would
rather have done without, to the effect that
they were “anxiously waiting to see what the
Government intended to do, as present condi-
tions and pay were unsatisfactory.” If he
wasn’t so superfluous, O’Callaghan would be
an amusing cuss.

Following on Geoffrey Syme’s capture of
a dangerous Melbourne burglar, a Parkville
(Melb.) citizen and his sister last week par-
tially slew another midnight intruder, while
Franklin Peterson’s son just missed captur-
ing, and possibly beheading, a third
by a hair’s-breadth. If other Yarra-
siders will follow suit, there will pres-
ently be no need for O’Callaghan’s helpless
minions, and that effete person will find
himself so far unemployed that he will be
eager to go and do “point duty” outside the
Town Hall.

The habitues of Melbourne’s Anglican
Cathedral are discussing the recent erection
of a mixed bathing font in that sacred, edi-
fice. The new bath, intended for total im-
mersions of adults, is a memorial to the late
Bishop Goe, and as a cathedral fitting it is
said to be. unique. Baptismal fonts in
general are shallow basins, suitable only for
washing or sprinkling the head of the bap-
tisee. The Goe memorial is expected to be
in great demand during the hot months.
Grown-ups who were baptised in infancy but
have forgotten what it felt like, are booking
a total immersion-CMwi-cOnfirmation, and
sweet things in bathing gowns for ladies are
looked forward to.
A well-known writer
on Australian history
recently brought a
storm about her ears
by alleging that a
“currency lass” was
the daughter of an ex-
convict. The term was
applied to all native
born white girls to dis-
tinguish them from
immigrant children,
who were termed “ster-
ling.” One hundred
jears ago the Austra-
lian circulating me-
dium was of a very
mixed character. Rum.
which varied in price
with the quantity on
the market, promissory
notes and lOU’s for
sums from threepence
up to fl, the holey dol-
lar, the dump, and
the Spanish or Mexi-
can dollar, were de-
nominated “currency,"
while the guinea and
the silver and copper
coins of Great Britain
were “sterling.” Then
£1 sterling was con-
sidered equal to 255.
currency. A facetious
paymaster of the 73rd
has the credit of hav-
ing applied the terms
“sterling” and “cur-
rency” to the imported
and the native born
respectively. The
phrase is not now in
use. At one time “Cur-
rency Lass” and “Cur-
rency Lad” were used
as publichouse signs.
On the S.W. corner of
Hunter-street was once
the Currency Lass,
and on Brickfield Hill, many years ago. was
another, while there was a Currency Lad in
Market-street. To-day there is a Currency
Lass on the Glebe-road, and no doubt some
old towns in the interior still cherish the
sign. It is said that the original potentate
of the S. M. Herald never smiled again
after receiving a letter which described him
as “Mr. Fairfax, opposite the Currency Lass
Inn.” 4
A gentleman of color is complaining bit-
terly to the Adelaide papers of the wild rush
made by the women of England for his hand.
He wrote to some public body up in Lan-
cashire, where they used to have “purring”
( i.e., kicking) matches, saying that he could
do with a wife; and that was enough. He
got 24 applications by the first mail, and
tried to consider them in turn. By the next
mail, which was last week, he got 86, and
he began to feel worried. The prospective
brides range in point of age from 18 to 50.
and many of the letters and photos, enclosed
show them to be apparently refined and
assuredly comely. (He produced these “ex-
hibits” to the reporters.) He admits that
he had absentmindedly described himself as
chef at the Grand Central Hotel, whereas
his real position there is somewhat lower.
Still, he has a position and a reliable wage.
But the fact that over a hundred women
could be found in a week, ready to rush
across the world to an unknown colored in-
dividual on his own mere statement that he
could support a wife, speaks volumes for the
condition of affairs in the Cold Countrv.
**. * *
Masses of wattle —golden, black, silver and
Cootamundra —brightened up Melbourne s
streets on September 2. The shimmering,
feathery blossom lends its pale gold to decor-
ation, but true Australians do not need to
wear it to remind themselves of its existence
and meaning. The blossom looks loveliest
where Nature put it. but of late years many
citizens have helped Nature. In Yarra-
side suburbs, where but a few years ago in-
digenous shrubs and trees were held of no
account as compared with imported and
mostly deciduous plants, one now sees Aus-
tralian acacias and eucalypts by the hun-
dred. Though its term of transcendent
beauty is but brief, the Cootamundra wattle
revels in such luxuriance of blossom that it
comes easily first favorite. After it, the
golden wattle, the blue gum and sugar gum
are frequent. Of late, the gorgeous scarlet
flowering gum has come into. notice. The
marvel is that the precious pioneers passed
over these beautiful evergreens for imported
frauds like the untidy. soil-impoverishing
pinus insiynis. or dispiriting winter skele-
tons like the elm, oak, beech, etc.

“The original skirt was the fig-leaf in the

lar fashion.” —Extract from■ cabled report of
a sensational Minneapolis (TJ.B.A.) sermon.
What Eve wore, women little care;
They scorn what Paris doesn’t wear.
A fig-leaf suited Mother Eve,
And now her daughters take French leave.
THE VILLA STANDS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mrs. Morton depicted the condition of Carlton as
infamous. She discovered rows of tiny two-roomed
hovels, erected in the back yards of more pretentious
houses, and often a third row of huts built in the
diminutive back yard belonging to the second row.
The owner of some pretentious residence has cut up
his back yard into pocket handkerchief allotments
for the erection of hovels. The authorities never in-
terfere with him. None of these dreadful dens has
a bathroom. You may stand at the back door and
touch the back fence. —-Melbourne Age.
Behold the Villa —how it stands !
Upraised, as ’tis but meet, on gentle hillock,
Vine-girt, with lovely shrubs and flowers embosomed,
With deep verandahs pent, and choicely fronted
With gleaming pillared portico,
Behold! It stands.
Hark to the strain that streams from out the Villa !
Some sweet Sonata or some chaste Nocturne
The dainty daughters of the Fatted Household
Pour from the grand piano ; and, at times,
Some radiant Girl, whose voice is all of Faery,
Rolls out a flood of song.
Behold again the Villa !
This time we gaze upon it from the rear,
And ask ourselves what slice of squalid slum,
What grievous, fever-haunted Ghetto hovel,
Has been transplanted by some Djinn accursed,
And dropped, to shame the spotless, shining mansion,
Hard by its backmost door.
A two-roomed hut, poor serf of that proud palace,
In lowliness bows down
And licks the heels the Villa fine exposes
As from a lordly gown ;
And lo ! some feet to rear of that same hovel,
Behold another hut
Of rotting timber and of rusty iron,
Devoid of paint anfl reeking all of squalor,
And bidding welcome to the fiends of Typhoid,
Stands out in grime.
No witching burst of music therefrom issues ;
At times a laugh inane,
Or maudlin burst of bawdy song outrushes,
And fouls the ambient air ;
I wot the filth that hapless humans live in
Creeps in by slow degrees, till, like a lichen
It crusts their very souls.
But Villa folk must lie full soft and riot,
And Villa folk must pass their days at ease,
And portly Wealth must go not short of purple,
And stately ladies languish as they please.
Wherefore it is but meet that human middens
A compost rich should breed,
Where Pride may raise the myriad scarlet blossoms
That root in human need ;
And so it comes that, girt by huts and hovels,
Which meekly pay a tributary toll
And call the Mansion Master of their soul,
The Villa stands!
QUEENSLAND.
F. BORN.

FILLING THE CHURCHES: A BLOW
AT VAUDEVILLE.
Minneapolis, Monday Evening.—Rev. G. Morrill
in his sermons, denounces women’s fashions in, such a
manner that his church is crowded every Sunday
The original skirt, he says, was a fig leaf from the
Garden.of'Eden, and apparently the women of to-day
are returning to a somewhat similar fashion. The
ministers shows lantern slides to prove, how immodest
are the styles now in vogue.—Recent cable .
THE REV. ENTERTAINER : "The next slide,
dear br-r-rethren, will reveal the last sta«e
of transition from the costume of to-day to
that of Eden , some ” (Shrieks of im-
patient applause.)
“Monkoarns,” being suddenly incited by
the rush of nothing in particular, and having
no more cause to write on the undermen-
tioned matter now than any other day, ,ut
simply feeling that the Time is Ripe, holds
forth :
Somewhat about Melbourne bookshops, drinkeries
of the Pierian stream. Memory hardly goes behind
the rivals Robertson and Mullen. The yarn is that
Squatter Moffatt sent an order to Robertson, “Please
forward one ton books,” when he expected to enter-
tain the Duke of Edinburgh. There was also once
a rival, W. Robertson, in Elizabeth-street, as book-
seller. G. Robertson began in Collins-street East,
above the Athemeum ; Buzzard and Vale were i i
Bourke-street East; Vale went to Ballarat; Cade
was in Collins-street, a little west; Stephens fallowed
him ; Coppin was in Caple’s shop when he received
the staggering news of the loss of Brooke in the
London. Dwight, top of Bourke-street East, about-
next to the V/hite Hart, was the principal second-
hand man. Bennett, lower down Bourke-street, kept
up a good rivalry. His wife was very -able, carrying
on while he bought books ,in England. Charlwood
and Sons were in Bourke-street, near the Herald
office and post office. They did not care so much for
books, as for theatrical printing, which they pretty
well monopolised till Shaw, Harnett and Co. stepped
in, slicing off the Theatre Royal business for a time.
Charlwoods also dealt more in plays than any firm.
Muskett took over the bookselling business. Davis,
in Collins-street, is more recent. The secondhand
line has considerably brightened of recent ’years, with
fresh dealers coming in. I had almost got through
Hamlet without the Prince of Denmark, both in new
and old, Arcadian .Cole.
“Pessimistic remarks from “J.R.”; The
Panama Canal looks like bringing a heap of
trouble on the tJ.S. Already the tolls ques-
tion has enraged the Powers, and filled the
earth with ravings on the subject of Yankee
chicanery and sharp practice. Then there is
the question of competition with Suez. Ac-
cording to a recent cable:—
Professor Emory Johnson, the Panama Canal ex-
pert, told an interviewer that if the Panama propo-
sition was to be a financial success, the tolls must
be lower than those of Suez. Even lower tolls would
not withdraw from Suez the greater part of the
Oriental, trade, because of Suez’s greater traffic op-
portunities, etc.
It may be taken for granted that the Suez
Canal was immensely cheaper to construct
than that of Panama, and it is certainly
cheaper to maintain. It has no locks, and
little, if any, rock-cutting. It can be wid-
ened and deepened to almost any desired
extent by the simple process of dredging. No
one yet knows what the Panama Canal will
have cost when it is finished, and no one has
the _ remotest idea,
within a few millions
of dollars, how much a,
year it will cost to
maintain it. It should
also be remembered
that the vast work
runs through country
where earthquakes are
well nigh as common
as thunderstorms. How
would a serious ’quake
affect the heavy earth-
work and rock-cut-
tings of the Canal?
Disastrously, mayhap.
It is certain that only
the most capable engi-
neers will be put in
charge of the works,
but the enormous arti-
ficial lake which is
necessary to feed the
locks must always be
a source of danger in
the rainy season, and
will need constant
watching. On the At-
lantic side, the Canal
opens into the Carib-
bean Sea, where ports
are few and naviga-
ti o n dangerous.
Whether the Canal
will, in any case, be
used by many ships
beside the toll-free
Lj.S. coasters is very
hard to say. If not,'
the U.S. taxpayer will
find himself saddled
with a maintenance
bill that will make
him sit up and speak firmly through his nose.
The Argus, having considered the possi-
bility of some peculiar development in the
Australian race, grudgingly admits that our
people may vary from the old stock, but
almost tearfully advises us to be careful what'
we are about. “Of course, we are desirous
of cultivating to the utmost the qualities
which best suit life in this land. The action
of environment must tell on the mental and
physical character of our people.” But the
Argus goes on to trust in the good sense of
the people to see that the developments are not
ot such a nature as to make us a nation
apart from the grand old nation from which
we sprung-” Plainly what is wanted is a
hasty Bill to Retard the Progress of Racial
Development. If not we may wake up some
morn nig, and find ourselves distinctively and
definitely Austmligin. Not English, or Irish
or Scottish, or even British, but just Aus-
tralian. The English became English the
Bermans German, the French French, but
the Australian must remain a tadpole, or
capital will leave the country.
“Henry Horsecollar” writes:—
Where do our friends the Germans come in as re-
gards the washing habit? Dr. Quine, of Manchester
(England), has given an estimate to the Royal Sani-
tary Institute s Congress re the frequency of the
National Wash. He nuts Scotch people first, then
(2) Englishmen, (3) Irishmen, (4) U.S. women, (5)
English women (6) U.S. men, (7) Frenchmen, and
(8) Germans, let, at many hotels, where all--these-
nationalities can be observed, he reckons tfi at, any
day, about 1 per cent, of the guests have .a bath.
A MATTER OF HALF A CROWN,
HENRY: “ Stay, Rupert,’ I would hate; speech
with thee,”
RUPERT: “Nay, Henry, too well I know
speech is silver. ”
Therefore, the whole lot of the guests will get bathed
in 100 days, or (roughly speaking) 3$ times a year
—a case of 3i baths per annum per individual.
Now, if the Scotch folk at the head of the list are
notably clean, and wolf more than their share, of
such wash as is going, how much wash is left for
the travelling German at,the other end of the list?
Then there are people of smaller nationalities. \yho
don’t supply enough travellers lo enable an hotel 1
average to be made —Greeks, Chilians, Portuguese,
Danes, Spaniards, etc. If any of these races wash to
excess, as excessive wash is understood in England,,
the miserable German must dry-blow himself on his
travels. At the very special hotels the rates are
higher, but Dr. Quine’s estimate was that, at the
best, one guest in 20 has a bath on any given day,
and 19 do not. If that is the best result where there
is abundance of hot water and a roomy vessel to 101 l
in, what is it likely to be in homes where there is
only a bleak receptacle and a rush of bitterly cold
water? And what is it likely to be in homes where
there is no receptacle at all? The Englishman, says
Quine, washes less • as he grows old, but even while
he is still young enough to go about.,, he doesn’t
appear to make an heroic splash. Therefore, let Aus-
tralia hold on with both .hands to its blessed inheri-
tance, and establish all over this country a Society
for the Promotion and Preservation of AVashing. It
should make' war on houses without baths, promote
water.-conservation and better water supply for coun-
try towns, .encourage the expulsion of dirty people
from trades unions, Shakspeare societies, Orange
lodges hnd other institutions, insist on frequent and
intense washing in gaols, lunatic asylums, orphanages,
bp'ardihsvschools, monasteries and the like, and endow
Turkish baths. There is nothing more beautiful than
a well-endowed Turkish bath. It might even, found
the AVash Church, with a broad creed, and affiliate
it,, with any other existing sect that is prepared to
accept; the Wash doctrine, and walk in beauty ac-
cordingly. Then there is a wide field for the hosing
o‘f sundowners at country police stations, and other
possibilities too numerous to mention.
The peacefully-disposed anti-militarists in
Maoriland have been at it again. Recently’
they created a disturbance by turning a fire-
hose on a meeting convened to discuss the
military training scheme. Now they have
robbed Christchurch of one of its warlike
treasures. Until recently a pom-pom which
had been captured in the alleged Boer war
rusted proudly in Queen’s Square. The
mass of old iron got on the anti-militarists’
nerves, and they petitioned the local council
to have it removed. The loyal council of
course refused, and, one day, Christ-
church woke up to find that the gun,
which had been chained down to a concrete
base, had been chiselled from its anchorage
and shoved into the Avon River. The Avon
isn’t a very deep river, and if many more
things are thrown into it the scenery will be
spoiled.

bourne District Court: “You say the de-
fendant tore your dress off, pulled out a
great deal of your hair, threw you down,
dragged you about the yard, and hit you with
a clothes-prop ?” “Yes, sir. He was most
discourteous.”

THE GOOD, OLD-FASHIONED STYLE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Cancer, consumption and the communion cup
formed the triple argument of Dr. Neil McDougall,
"’ho argued at the annual meeting of St. DaVid's
Presbyterian Church, Christchurch, Maoriland, for a
change in sacramental methods. He quoted the
British Medical Journal on the danger of germ-
transmission from mouth to mouth—and urged that
the common cup be replaced with individual ones.
Mr. Duncan Macpherson, City Missioner, differed
from Dr. McDougall—quoting St. Luke—and urged
members to continue in the good old-fashioned style.
—Daily pager.
Mr. Duncan, called Macpherson,
Urges each communion person
When he joins the giddy Wowsers with a smile,
Not to try and use his gumption
To dodge cancer and consumption,
But to catch ’em in the “good old-fashioned style.”
Do not stop, beloved brother,
For in many, many other
Ways the present age the good old saints would rile ;
And they’d curse in desperation,
Should they find no Christian nation
Is now working in the ’’good old-fashioned style.”
M e can’t now, beloved brother,
Catch a party of another
Church and truss him up to stew in boiling “ile,”
And-the thumb-screws and the racks, too,
And the “boot” and melted wax, too
Oh! mv heart weeps for the “good old-fashioned
style.”
In these days a common person—■
Bow it must annoy Macpherson—
If they amputate his head or test his bile,
M ill have, nurse and doctors messing
Round with antiseptic dressing.
Ah-h! ’twas different in the “good old-fashioned
style.”
Oh ! to get some tar a-hoiling—
Never mind about it soiling—
Or a red-hot bar or furnace-heated file,
Stick ’em on the bleeding stump, Mac ;
Let the beggars howl and thump, Mac,
Just remind ’em it’s the “good old-fashioned style.”
Once the wretches shrieked and shouted,
And no earnest Christian doubted
But that all the horrors all the blessed while
Were by Providence provided—-
We’d be damned if you or I did
wl 'th that dear “good old-fashioned style.”
{Give vs back, 0 Lord , our “good old-fashioned
style !”)

made me feel good after I had taken a few doses.

ever used. I often take a dose, and would not be
without them.”*

ABORIGINI-

“Hogg” : Montfort l’Amaury, in Seine-et-
Oise (France), has leaped into fame. A wealthy
resident left the handsome manager the sur-
rounding estate and a fortune for the use of dead-
beats, paupers, swaggies and sundowners of every
description. He bequeathed his possessions to the
unwashed, the dosser, the loafer, the sleeper-out and
the chicken-snatcher. They have been flocking to the
favored locality from every point of the compass,
and the municipal authorities are tearing their hair,
Cursing the testator, nd wondering how to stop the
invasion and get rid of the plague. That is exactly
what the testator desired and contemplated. The
municipal council offended him by cutting down trees
and effecting various other supposed improvements
around his estate. He swore to be revenged upon
them, and now in the Elysian Fields or Hades or
wherever he is, he is doubtless laughing at the
success of his valedictory jest.

“Wal” : For a country that has only had
a few years experience of life as a part of the civil-
ised world, the Commonwealth’s dependency of
Papua has a variegated population. The figures of
1911 show the following:—
Malays .. .. .. .. . . 14
Japanese . . .. . . . . . . 8
Chinese . . . . . . . . . • 3
Filipinos . . . . . . 47
Solomon Islanders . . .. 57
Samoans .. . . . . . . . . 127
Fijians .. . . . . . . 30
West Indians (negro) . . . . . . 1
East Indians (Hindoos) . . . . 4
Javanese . . . . . . . . . . 3
Rarotongans . . . . . . 61
Other countries .. . . . . .. 95
450
“Other countries” comprise a motley lot, there
being a sprinkling of Afghans, Arabs, Cingalees and
nondescripts. Over two-thirds of the mob are em-
ployees of the various mission societies. Some of
them are married to Papuan women, and have added
a number of weird mixtures to the color scheme of
the population. What the hash will be like in
another decade is hard to say.
Dear Bulletin, —Don’t see that Vigi-
lans” (B. 8/8/T2) has any just cause to spread
the wet blanket of his disapprobation over the
scheme to develop the agricultural lands of the Daly
River (N.T.). Because, in old times, C. B. Fisher
and others were badly advised in trying to rear
bullocks on the raw, sour grasses of the coastal
region of the Territory, and because no one has
since rushed the locality with a capiel farm, or a
goat-breeding outfit, or a pig ranch, or a mule cor-
poration, or a scheme for raising wild cats, there
is no reason why the country shouldn’t be useful
for something else. As a matter of fact it is. I
consider the new Administrator is wise in turning
his attention to the agricultural possibilities of the
Daly River lands. The flood-plains of the Lower
Daly are probably the pick of the agricultural lands
of the Northern Territory, being second neither to
the Adelaide River country nor to the rich region
of the scrub-lands further north. Furthermore, it
isn’t far from the nearest intersection of the railway
at Brock’s Creek, and an outlet by water (river and
sea) has been provided by Providence. The man
who knows the Daly River region better than any
person living is J. H. Niemann, of Pine Creek. He
lived there for some years, and is a man of big in-
telligence, an optimist, a good Australian, and a
white man through to the heart; and the close
settlement of the wonderful Daly River country was
the dream of his life when I first knew him. That
raw coastal region that won’t produce bullocks will
grow cotton and sugar-cane, and tobacco and hemp, and
most other sub-tropical products in profusion, in-
cluding all the fruits and vegetables necessary to
a white man’s wants. It will also grow white
babies if it is required to. It is in the matter of
cotton plantation that Dr. Gilruth has probably
got his eye glued on the Daly River. Cotton has
grown there, in a half-wild state, for years. Bushes
of cotton ( Gossypium Barbadense ) grew at the back
of the ruined R.C. chapel, and otherwhere about the
ancient settlement, that were planted by missionaries
many years ago. These never received any atten-
tion, and were choked with weeds and couch-grass
and fathom-high marshmallows; yet they continued
to bear cotton, good and plenty, and they never had
a pest on them. And, what is more, the astounding
vegetables withstood periodical scorchings from fierce
grass-fires each year. At the Howley I saw per-
ennial cotton bushes (again the good old Gossypium
Barb.) that were Bft. high by 20ft. or 30ft. in
circumference. They were covered with fat bolls,
and looked as if their surroundings suited them right
down to their roots and up again. My conclusions
were that the climate was everything, and that the
quality of the soil, providing it was well drained,
was a matter of indifference. So, doubtless, not-
withstanding the sneer of “Vigilans,” Providence
and Dr. Gilruth may yet make a “do” of the Daly
River district. —Yours, etc., Mallarapoonie.
* * * *
“Gip” : What seems to me to yell aloud to
science, Heaven and the Ohapman-Alexander mission
for elucidation is the iron determination on the
part of frogs to haunt particular spots. One dinky
little green chap has, to my knowledge, been sitting
on a window-sill for the last three months, and
another of my acquaintance chose the top of a bath-
room shower for his roosting place. But the most
pertinacious frog I ever knew was a native of Graf-
ton. He was a large and portly amphibian, and he
chose as his squattage the washstand of a person in
the Government ' service. To squash the creature
appeared an obscene act, but when, after being ten-
derly removed on a towel and gingerly thrown out
by a hind leg, the thing still returned to its perch,
it became necessary to resort to violent methods.
Throwing it at passing ferry boats proved of no
avail, and attempts to smite buildings a mile distant
with its objectionable presence had a similar result.
The faithful animal was always back on his beloved
washstand. Putting it in a box, the desperate and
haunted tenant took it for a long ride in the train,
a long walk along a strange road, a long row across a
pleasant river, and a quiet stroll to the centre of a
lonely and forsaken peninsula. The man returned
alone. The frog didn’t get back for quite eight
days.
“Warrumbucca” : You meet a decent bloke
or cove in the bush now and again. The other day
I avoided navigating my team through the paddock
of one of these phenomena, though it would have
saved much distance. Next morning I met him.
“Why ther Hell didn’t ver take the team through
me blanky paddick?” he asked kindly. “Well,” I
said, “the horses were a bit weak, and the going
looked soft, so I took the hard track.” “Daman-
blarstit, man,” he said, with true Old World cour-
tesy, “haven’t I got any blank blanky horses to give
ye a pull, curse and blank me?” “Righto!” I
countered shamefacedly, “I’ll know better next
time.” “An’ you oughter know better!” he thun-
dered affectionately. “What in Hell else am I
blanky well here for?”

Parnell or Sydenham has “Tamumu” (B. 8/8/’l2)

the Maoris had a peculiar species of dog the like of

world ( kuri ruarangi). It is improbable that even
the earliest white settlers ever saw one. Abel Tas-

“Jimmie Pannikin ” : Some queer immigrants have reached the North Cowst of N.S.W;
but the limit came to light a few days ago. The boss told him to bog in and learn to milk,
and the new arrival offered no objection. "Buthe said, "I'm afraid of these ere knows.
What abaht gitting me first lessons on a calf.” The boss dropped dead or thereabouts.
something like mongrel sheep dogs, with abnormal
heads, foxy ears, and short, bushy tails. They were
used to hunt birds with, and were great pets. Their
bark was a weak “au, au,” and they would not
bite even a Wowser or a Tamumu. Ilenare enjoyed
the flesh as a delicacy, and the pelts as a motor-
coat ( ihupuni ). Furthermore, they would not go
wild, and could not be driven away from the scent
of the shark stews with a gatling gun. Again,
“kuri” does not mean dog, it means beast. “Kar-
arehe” is the Maori name for dog. Evidently
“Tamumu” is like the people who think that
“poaka” is borrowed from the white man’s “porker.”
Cook found place-names among some of the islands,
such as “pa-puaka” (pig pen) and “puaka ngun-
guru” (grunting pig). He also found stronger evi-
dence than that. He found the pigs themselves.
Perhaps “Tamumu” got his dog evidence in the
same way that he procured his moa information
(B. 20/6/T2). There is no evidence that any
Maori ever saw a moa. Ilenare has no feather mats
from the big chicken, and most of the old shark
consumers believed the moa bones to be the remains
of giant predecessors. Others thought them to have
represented some huge beast. The N.E. coast
Maoris alone recognised them as fowl. Human re-
mains have been found pillowed on moa egg-shells,
but they may have been the frames of the ancient people
(tangata-whenua), who inhabited Moaland before
Henare demonstrated his military-training system
to them. They also had canines, so “Tamumu” will
see that this country has been well dogged even
before Caesar was shortened and christened.
“Eddyson” : Permit me to enter Lai Lai
(Vic.) for your just-inaugurated slowest district
competition. Not for nothing has it won its vaude-
ville reputation as the place where irresistible languor
and immovable lassitude meet and mingle. I have
spent four years in Lai Lai during the past three
months, and I have found everything excessively
gradual there, with the exception of the waterfall,
and there was some hesitancy even about that. Dur-
ing my stay the watch I was carrying had an attack
of backward paralysis, and I employed an afternoon
hunting for the time. None knew the time, nor wanted
to. They knew it was time, and unripe, and that suf-
ficed. My concern in this matter was plainly regarded
as the mark of a restless revolutionary. Amongst
others, I questioned a tired man who was growing
onions—or rather sitting on a mossy bank waiting
for onions to come up. No, he didn’t know what
o’clock it was. “Do you never know the time in
Lai Lai?” I asked pettishly. “Oh, yes,” he replied.
“I got a poplar in me back yard, V when it begins
to bud I know the spring’s cornin’.”
“Tamumu”: In the grey manuka wastes,
north of Auckland, which form the greater part of
the gum country, the cocky who takes up a section
finds that bush suitable for fencing timber is extremely
scarce. On his 100- or 200-acre section there is
not, as far as the eye can see, a single tree or log
from which even the shadow of a post could be got.
But, hidden by perhaps only two or three inches of
soil, are giant kauris, as sound as the day on which
they crashed to earth. They are easily located by
means of the gum spear. Thereafter, it is merely
a question of clearing away enough of the encircling
clay to enable the crosscut to be brought into action,
in order to get fencing timber galore. Why the
good timber is all below ground, and mere scrub
or penny herbage above, I cannot tell. Perhaps
there is a curse on the country.

“Lantana”: I should like the opinion of
the sainted Bruntnell and the sin experts of the
Ohapman-Alexander troupe on the case of William
Jenkins. William was sitting in his home when a
yearning for beer assailed him. Did he fight it
down by means of prayer- and hymn-book? Nope.
He simply put on his hat and hied him to the
nearest pub. As he returned, gratified, if not actu-
ally sozzled, he heard a crash. Entering the house
he discovered that the whole of the plaster ceiling had
fallen on the precise spot where he would have been,
if he hadn’t yielded to the yearning above
mentioned, and fallen a victim to the Demon Alco-
hol. If any professional soul-salver can wrest me
a moral out of this happening, I pledge myself,
here and now, to stand him three (3) milks.
A SUNSET EPISODE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We cursed her —and we pitied her,
The Sunset’s daughter, who,
Up north, where white men seldom were,
Was wed to Kandal Dhu.
He found her at the Quandong Bore,
As fair as he was brown,
Due north five hundred miles, and more,
From quaint old Sunset Town.
Up north she saw him as a King,
When first he came to woo ;
Down south he was a Colored Thing—
Detested through and through.
She saw the white men pass, and gaze
With fierce hate in their eyes;
She heard the thin-lipped bitter phrase
Flung at her sacrifice.
The white girls passed her by, and turned
Their eyes across the way
(White pride decreed that she be spurned,
Though thrice as fair as they).
They did not speak; ’twas understood
That, changeless, on each tongue,
For her who smirched her maidenhood
Contempt unspoken hung.
She saw the great White King who might
Have claimed her as his queen,
Had she pre-seen with wisdom’s sight
The proud, glad, Might-Have-Been.
But no repentance could repay
One half the debt she owed;
And God gives back no yesterday
To her who breaks His Gode.
But came a day when Summer burned
The shrivelled grass to grey,
And Kandal Dhu again returned
Along the Great Lone Way.
Red blew the dust across the plain;
The mad-capped mirage shone;
He steered his hunchies home again—-
But Kandal’s bride was gone!
We asked no questions, and were asked
No questions in return ;
Each face was as the Sphinx’s masked,
Each mouth a silent urn....
The shutters drawn... At whose request ?
Well, let those tell who care,
That we who knew the least knew best
Who paid one white girl’s fare.

“Eryander” : Amongst* all the prevailing
talk about “compulsory service,” I haven’t heard
any reference to the one-time perfect Zulu military
system. At 10 years of'age every lad had, so to
speak, to join the ranks as a kind of squire or
weapon-carrier to some adult relative, and no Zulu
man was allowed to marry until he was 35. Up to
that age he had to nut in six months of every year
in military training—Special kraals being set apart
for that purpose. The men were nearly all of splen-
did physique and dauntless courage, and nothing
but modern diStance-slaughtering-methods could have
prevailed against such a magnificent hand-to-hand
fighting force. The Zulus knew the value of begin-
ning early, ' and even the magazine-rifle couldn’t
stop them, as long- as there were any of them left
to come on.

“Spud”: For long stayers in the scrap-
ping line, I have no hesitation in nominating turkey
gobblers. I am possessed of an Ottoman hen.
I reared her from her bright young egg-
hood, and watched her
grow in beauty day by
day; Until at last she be-
came a maiden turkey in
her prime, and towards
the end of August she
turned lightly to thoughts
of love. Nor were her
pinings vain, for two gob-
blers belonging to my
neighbor got their field
glasses on her, and were
smitten by. her winning
ways and the music of her
voice. They lost no time
in calling, and strutted
before her admiring gaze,
feathers puffed out, wings
trailing, tails spread. But
one young buck grew tired
of displaying his manly
beauty, and attempted to
hold her hand. He was
immediately assailed by
the other aspirant for the
fair damsel’s claw. Then
ensued a fight magnificent
to behold. I was time-
keeper and referee. They
battled chest to chest,
sometimes with necks en-
twined or backs inter-
locked. In spite of iny
cries of “time,” they re-
fused to break, and
scrapped away for lhr.
31min., with never a
smoko. The end came
when the older and more
drinky fowl dropped to
the ground. The success-
ful candidate gave him a
feeble final punch in the
neck. Then he attempted
a strut of triumph, but
succeeded only in an un-
steady wobble, odd feath-
ers puffed out and half a
tail spread. In the mean-
time the sweet cause of
the row had retired to
her virtuous couch on the roost and had blown her
candle out.
