“Billy T.”: Discovered two cement bar-
rels in a secluded part of Albert Park, Melbourne,
when taking an early morning run last week. They
were joined together lengthwise, and in a specula-
tive spirit I put in the boot. They parted and a
small, embittered man wriggled out of the northern
one. His legs remained immersed in the southern
keg. “Blime!” he wailed, “ain’t a Hinglishman’s
’ome his carsle?” It was a doss. The assisted im-
migrant indoors had devised a smart clip to keep the
home from bursting, so to speak. He confided to me,
with many a harsh comment on the worthlessness of
’Orstralians, 1 that he couldn’t go out and look for
work for fear of his abode being stolen.
“Montalex”: “Yalgun” (B. 22/8/T.2) is
right. The blue-tongued lizard is not venomous.
I have been bitten by the brute myself, and suffered
only the ordinary inflammation. And now let me
rise to point out—-for the n-th time—that no Aus-
tralian lizard is venomous. Some of them certainly
“look the part,” but no one of them enters such an
obvious external caveat as the Mexican heloderma,
the only venomous lizard known to zoology. This
creature, which —unlike the snakes —injects its
poison through the lower fangs, has korridum for
an appropriate surname, and has a skin like a bad
case of nodular leprosy. The West. Bornean Dyaks
attribute venomous powers to the “sidak,” a small
lizard of the monitor family (to which belongs our
gohanna), but, after dissecting a couple of dead
ones to make sure, I allowed a live one to bite me
My prompt demise didn’t happen.
* * * *
“Bromide”: Touching this bike-in-war
controversy, it is borne in on me that “Machete” is
but a carpet bushman, or George-street backblocker,
or something similar. Had he had personal traffic
with the remotenesses of this vast land, he would
have met plenty of men who bestride or push bikes,
loaded with swag, billy, water-bag and tucker, over
all sorts of country—roads or no roads —and think
•nothing of it. A shearing-shed at roll-call nowa-
days looks as if a city bike shop had got adrift in
it, so many of the metal steeds are there in evi-
dence. They weren’t carried there on the shearers’
backs, and the roads to the sheds aren’t wood-
blocked either. Granted, that where the country is
absolutely rotten, the bike-proprietor does a bit of
pushing; the fact still remains that he does an
immense deal of riding. The practice—carpers not-
withstanding—is going to have a notable effect in
the defence of Australia some day. The bush biker
travels light. F’riftstans, the men of the Greenhide
Push in N.W. Queensland are quite happy if they
start out on a long prospecting trip with a bag of
flour, some tea and sugar, a 21b. tin of baking 1
powder, tobacco, rifle, 200 cartridges and a lump of
greenhide. So rationed they are capable of main-
taining themselves for months. Now, these chaps
or bloques knock about nearer the place where the
trouble is likely to be than any other crowd, and
they are going to count if trouble comes. And there
are thousands like them elsewhere. It is certain
that they will be right up in front in time for
the first scrap, and they will accomplish the feat by
the aid of their bikes. With horses, water will be
a big trouble up .North unless there has been a
decent rainy season. A couple of dry years may
mean little or no grass. The bike man has no need
to worry over such things. A drop in the bag sees
him to the next water-hole easily. No! you
mightn’t be able to charge a position on bikeback,
but you can do more with a cycle in dry country
than with a horse—and, there is many a dry. stretch
to travel over before Australia is taken.

“Snooker”; The untutored Australian
savage shows himself a subtle reasoner on occasion.
Thus, when “Billy” defeated “Jimmy” for the abori-
ginal , 100yds. championship of Mudanmuck Gully
(first prize a bottle of rum), James claimed half
the prize. “ ’Ow th’ devil can yer claim ’alf th’
rum?” yelled Dungav’s chief backer, “’E won
orright.” James spat vigorously, and replied, “Him
bin win orright, Boss, but if I not bin running who
could ’e win from, Boss?”
“George Street”: The climate of Great
Britain is a sorrowfu 1 fact. To describe as the
true home of a white race a country which can’t
keep decently dry for one Test match out of three,
looks like insulting the white man. to the extent of
suggesting that his true habitat is mud. No one
in Australia who knows the British winter tries to
pretend that it is cheerful, but as winters go it
is decency itself to the awful thing which does duty
there for a summer. Nor can it be argued that the
splash of recent years, and of this year in particular,
is phenomenal. Even the Anglo-Saxon Chronicle
has its seasonal reports spattered with wet clay,
and anyone who likes to look up the weather
records for the 20 or 30 years which closed the
eighteenth century, which are to be found in
White’s “Selborne,” will begin to feel damp and
cold all over with sheer pity for the contemporaries
of Captain Cook and Robert Burns. British poets
sing like mad about the flowers of May and the roses
of June. The vegetables are there all right, but
all evidences point to the sober fact that they must
be picked, and always did have to be, picked,
by persons who held their umbrellas over
the blossoms and got their feet wet in
the grass. The proof that Britain is not even
second-class as a home for the white man is to be
found in the fact that the said white man is not
amphibious, and that he suffers in a sore-souled and
twisted-bodied sort of fashion from all the rheuma-
tism which may happen along. Tropical. Australia
is sometimes warm and sometimes wet, but if a fair
comparison be made between it and the land of the
Ancient Britons, the vagaries of its climate over a
period of years, when all put in a heap don’t
amount to the actual misery often concentrated into
a single day in the Cold Country. Yet that same
Cold Country, as a white man’s home,, is reckoned
worth building enormous fleets to defend, and the
Australian Government doesn’t think enough of the
real white man’s country, called the Northern Ter-
ritory, to go ahead and build a straight line of
railway for its development and protection. By-
and-bye, perhaps, when the' remnant of the white
race is trying to keep warm in the neighborhood of
the South Pole, the place their fathers wouldn’t
keep may come to be regarded as a lost Paradise,
which the flaming sword of Asia guards against
those who shudderingly chafe their toes on the ice-
bergs of despair.
“Ganesha”: Rosie was a very ancient
aboriginal lady. She lived in the Far North, in the
land of pearl and gold, swamp and jungle, sweat
and sunset and silver-lead, ant-hills, coral, finches,
emus, snakes, railways that aren’t there, explorers,
prospectors and cattle duffers, horses and cattle,
absentee politicians, station-managers, sundowners,
lonely pubs and telegraph wires. But she paid no
attention to such trifles. She was old and somewhat
neglected by the tribe. After the feast she was thrown
a kangaroo rib c a bit of gristle, but never any tit-
bit, such as a nice piece of wallaby liver or stewed
worm or baked snake. And Rosie was an epicure.
Luckily she was a gifted woman; a natural ventrilo-
quist. When the food , was too suggestive of a four-
penny restaurant Rosie got struck with a brilliant
notion. She announced sadly to the callous tribe
that the spirits of the bush were calling her, and
one night she wandered away. She found a suitable
hiding place, and kept very still till some men of
the tribe came forth to look for her. They called
and shrieked, but for a long time Rosie preserved a
mysterious silence. Then, at last, her voice came
put of a strange place, high up in the. atmosphere
where there wasn’t anything. It made the statement
that the bush spirits had taken her away because the
tribe was no plurry good, and didn’t know how to

treat a venerable lady. They asked her to come
back, but the voice said it would see them blowed
first. The medicine men were geatly perplexed at
the magic, and when Rosie’s voice broke out in a
new place in the ether, repeating the sorrowful tale
with variations, they left for home in a hurry.
Later on, Rosie arrived, looking shaky and scared,
and very innocent, and repeated the simple legend
how she had been taken away by the spirits of the
bush, and she prophesied how dreadful things would
happen if she were not with the tribe. After much
pressure from the elders she agreed to stay on for a
bit. And the tribe then recognised Rosie at her true
worth, and filled her up with the most ancient ami
valued eggs, and turkey ragout, and dugong oil, and
other delicacies. Rosie was very fat when I chanced
on her, for whenever the tribe had moments of for-
getfulness a far-away look would come into her eyes,
and a casual reference be made to the spirits of the
bush.
STAUNTON’S FARM.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In Staunton’s paddock the stumps are grey,
The logs lie scattered on every side,
At the will of the wind the grasses sway .
From dawn to the quiet evening tide.
’Tis little enough of shade is there.
When the sun shines down from a cloudless sky,
The heat-waves rise on the quivering air—
Twisting the long grey fence awry.
And all day long through the sleepy scene
O’er sand and pebbles a creek runs east;
A few trees dotting the place with green
Give shade and shelter 1 to bird and beast.
It’s better at dawn when the magpies call
And robed in crimson the morning breaks,
Then rosy tints on the landscape fall,
And, stretching its limbs, the land awakes.
A crow sits watchful upon a gate,
His smooth plumes shining like sable silk ;
At the milking-yard the cattle wait
With fresh white tribute of morning milk.
Then Staunton, mounting his horse, rides forth
To view his paddocks, and Staunton’s son,
With axe on shoulder, goes gaily forth,
For work is waiting and must be done.
And Staunton’s wife —has she time to turn
With so ’'much to shape and so much to trim—
With bread to bake and butter to churn,
And the honey to strain and the milk to skim?
And Staunton’s Mary, the tall, the sweet,
With the rounded arms and the peach-like bloom ;
It's little she idles—with hands and feet
Flashing and flying about the room.
Rich beauty, raining from radiant skies,
Has graced her, making her pure and fair,
With the deepest blue of -the sea in her eyes
And the darkest dusk of the night in her hair.
AVith farewell lights on the western steeps
The sun goes down, and with cooling hand
And a touch that soothes —the twilight creeps—
A thing of greyness athwart the land.
Then, all toil over, the man and wife
Sit mutely musing on thirty years,
Filled full of trial and toil and strife,
And love triumphant through sighs and tears.
And Alary’s voice in the twilit room
Soars high like a jewelled bird that flies,
And all is hushed in the scented gloom,
And muteness dreams ’neath the arching skies.
“Come home, come home!” ’tis the last bird calls.
On hills of silence, on creek and plain.
Dew-wet, soft-breathing, the darkness falls,
And golden stars in the heavens reign.

RODERIC QUINN.


DIRGE OP THE VANISHED SYDNEX
COUNTER LUNCH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The corned beef torn as if by dogs.
The slashed and riddled cheese.
The gnawed and nibbled feet of hogs,
Farewell! Farewell to these!
Farewell to the disordered bread!
Batter we’ll see no more!
Strew crumbs upon each mourner's
head.
Strew bones upon the floor!
No longer will a pot of beer
Suffice for every need,
For prog has grown too blessed dear,
Each one must BUY his feed.
The merry days are past and gone
When Bung kept open house.
And spread good food to lure us on
To banquet and carouse.
How many times have we not stayed
And eaten of his grub,
Swelling before him unafraid
To show we liked his pub.
With hands and mouths stuffed full of
lunch.
Our strong jaws working well.
Remember how we used to munch
And how we used to swell"!
Remember what a jolly crew
We used to gather there!
Each with a pot of wholesome brew
Would take a hero’s share.
The little parties that we had!
Aye, there’s the hardest rub!
This saving regulation, lad.
Has broken up our club!
We thought he HIKED to see us eat—
To watch us gaily stuff
With crumbs and bones about our feet,
Until we’d had enough.
But now we know that ev’ry plate
We piled with bread and ham
Was sauced with bitter rage and hate.
And poisoned with a “damn.”
Ah, well! His scorn we never knew;
Our grief he shall not see:
We long enjoyed, O cheerful crew.
His hospitality.

PERSONAL
ITEMS
Alfred Green, the Launceston solicitor who
died recently in his 75th year, was one of
the oldest citizens of Tasmanian’s northern
capital. He was a devoted churchman, and
established a record by attending Holy Trin-
ity parish church for 70 years—ll as a
scholar, 17 as a teacher and 42 as secretary.
He was official scribe at the time of his
death. Launceston was his birthplace, and
he spent all his life there, and saw about as
much of the great outer world as a barnacle.
JIMMY BLAIR, Queensland's new Minister
of Public Instruction.
The death recently of publican Stutt, of
Doncaster, near Melbourne, removed a politi-
cal identity of the early days. In the early
’7o’s he owned a hotel in upper Bourke-
street, which was the rendezvous of every
live politician of the time. There was a
private menagerie in the yard, and the resort
came to be known as “Stutt’s Lions,” several
cages of those animals being included in the
collection. He represented Grant in the
House just before Peter Lalor. Later on, he
acquired the pub at Doncaster and another
at Box Hill, a few miles across country.
He .retired from business recently and was a
notable figure in the pretty picnic region.
Though a very old man, he was remarkably
vigorous until near the end, and his flowing
board was generally sighted fluttering be-
hind a pair of the smartest horses in the
district.
The recent death of a V ictorian land-
owner of vast acreage and tremendous Tory
principles recalls the beginning of the
family. It was founded, many similar
families, by a convict who married a convict
woman. Under Britain’s maternal care
Australia was then a country where any de-
cently-behaved resident could be a large
landowner for the asking. Britain supplied
it with a population of convicts who, in
many cases, grabbed as much territory as
they desired —on ticket-of-leave —and thus
helped to form the fine old Anglo-Australian
aristocracy which has ever blocked the way
to closer • settlement, and is now denouncing
Labor legislation as a tyranny of class
over caste. Apply the X-rays to the back-
bone of the Commonwealth’s Tory party,
and you find the emblem of the broad arrow
embedded in the centre of it. These haughty
notabilities are fond of posing as men who
rose by their own endeavors, and they often
say that if the men of to-day had the same
grit as OTJII fathers had they could rise just
as WE have done. It is too thin. The
country doesn’t nowadays give a man 50,000
acres for being a criminal.
“0.K.” writes
The late J. S. Butters represented Portland in two
Victorian Parliaments prior .to 187 0, and didn’t
leave even a footmark on the shores of politics. It
was after Portland had done with him that he went
to Fiji and gave moral instruction to old King
Thakambau and his dusky courtiers for four years.
He appointed himself Speaker to Thak’s Parliament,
and shook things up. As an entertainer of visiting
Australians he went the whole hog. Tradition says
that on one occasion'he gave a dinner party, at which
each white guest had a colored handmaiden to wait
on him, and then, like a Roman Emperor bidding
each guest take away the golden dish on which he
had eaten his “scran,” he loftily presented each guest
with his particular charmer- —for they were all
charmers in the best Fijian sense. However, the general
tone of the dinner-party was adverse to the host’s
resolution, and some of the older gents went so far
as to accuse Butters of bad taste when they returned
to Melbourne. There were Wowsers, of a sort, even
in those purple days of yore. In fact, I expect there
were Assyrian Wowsers in the glad, mad, bad days of
Asshurbanipal.
“E.C.” : After two years of almost unin-
terrupted suffering, Brother Judkins, of Vic-
toria and Maoriland, has at last met the
death he had looked in the face so long.
Judkins was no brilliant genius, and he was
handicapped by poor health, an undistin-
guished appearance, and an impossible name.
Yet he succeeded by pure energy and bog-
ging-in. He was no Wowser; he fought,
for all he was worth, the drink evil and
social evils of other sorts; but sectarianism
troubled him not. Had he been less of a
social and more of a political reformer, he
would have left a much deeper and more
lasting mark than he did. Yet within his
limitations he was a whole-hearted worker.
Teacher, tutor, missionist, auctioneer, lec-
turer and semi-journalist, he wasted but
few of the short days of his 43 years; and
the public appreciated his energy and hon-
esty of purpose.
A. S. Meek, who collects birds and butter-
flies for Walter Rothschild’s private museum
in England, left Sydney last week for Papua
and other islands to pursue the bloodthirsty
and elephantine beetle. He has just come
back from England, where he signed a new
contract for the obliteration of coleoptera
and such like. He is .also to bring back
stuffed germs and broken-down flies. In the
course of his struggles he will visit 37 differ-
ent islands, and make collections from each,
lie an offer from a second Roths-
child ;to collect fleas for his museum. This
Rothschild has the finest assortment of dead
and bashed and squashed fleas in the world.
Meqk declined the flea engagement. He pre-
to hit something nearer his own size.
The slaying of a flea, especially if it is a
married flea with a large family, is too much
like Jack Johnson hitting a feather-weight
when he isn’t looking.
Albert Dorrington, an Englishman who
spent a few years in Australia (it was un-
derstood by some folk here that Dorrington
was merely a pen-name, and that the real
designation was Bob Smith), has written
an article in the Contemporary Re-
view about the alleged soualor and misery
of the far inland settler. Dorrington was
an electro-plater, or something of the sort,
with a fine turn for literature, and a great
streak of imagination. He was really , a city
product, and, so far as is known, Parramatta
was his western limit by land. Still, lie
once did a sea trip to Eden. One of his
romances was laid on the Palmer Goldfield,
and his hero used to climb a hill in the even-
ing, and look with misty, longing eyes out to
sea. In the present case, Dorrington’s
gift has not failed him. He describes his
modest building allotment on the rail-
way line at Waitara, about nine miles from
Sydney, as if it were a dingo-infested region
out in the boundless wilderness, rather than
a place to which peaceful old commercial
gentlemen plodded home at night with hand-
bags.
“Rope Boy” writes :
Peter Bowling has been nominated as the N.S.W.
South Coast miners’ general secretary. He appears to
be keen on getting the job, an active canvass having-
commenced on his behalf a few hours after the death
of the late secretary. At a meeting of the Delegate
Board, a week or so back, Peter's eloquence induced
the delegates to appoint him acting-secretary until
the election takes place. Some of them are now
asking themselves if they have dealt fairly by the
man who looked after things during the late secre-
tary’s illness. It is current talk throughout the dis-
trict that Peter can talk the head oft most of the
delegates on the Board. His propositions are nearly
alwavs carried; but when they are placed before the
lodges for consideration, they are generally rejected.
Whether he can persuade the Southern men into tak-
ing him on trust as their general secretary remains
to”be seen. 1 guess his actions lately, in not bowing
to the will of the majority of the Miners’ Union, will
have to be explained; and we are told they will be
explained to our perfect satisfaction. In fact,
according to reports, Peter is able to explain any-
thing.
“Vie.”: F. W. Armytage, senior, of our
Western district family, has just passed
away. His father, G. Armytage, landed in
Sydney in 1813, and then went to Tasmania.
In 1836 he crossed to Victoria, in the brig
Henry, with a smattering of sheep, and
landed at Williamstown. He put his flock
with Judge Tedder’s, in charge of a son, T.
Armytage, who had been an articled clerk to
Gellibrand, the lawyer, compiler of Bat-
man’s famous truck with .lika Jika, the
alleged nigger chief. Gellibrand and one
Hesse went out exploring, and are still
missing. T. Armytage and party went to
look for them. Thev followed the Barwon
River west for 30 miles, where the tracks
were lost. Presumably Gellibrand and Hesse
were snatched up to Heaven. A policy of
£ll,OOO on Gellibrand’s life was paid, yet
there were rumors that that legal gentleman
had “gone nigger,” and was living in a nude
state among the Binghis. F. W. Armytage
lived at Woolomanata, near Geelong, till lie
moved to Como, South Yalta. Owned at
various times Mossgiel and Nocoleeehe, in
N.S.W., arid Norley, Thargomindah and
Ealbertie, in Queensland. Much interested
in frozen meat. With G. Fairbairn and H.
Cunningham he sent the first successful
shipment in 1879 by McEacharn’s steamer
Strathleven. Armytage owned most of the
cattle shipped. He married a daughter of
Staughton, of the Werribee. Three sons
survive.

King Richard III.
Last year no less than seven
Australasian Anglican bishops,
who had handed in the gaiters
and . -o. shovel hat with the
rigging on it, were on the re-
tired list. The R.C. bishop
dies at his post, and when he is
buried his remains help to ele-
vate the surface of a continent
which is a great deal too flat in
its natural state. But the Angli-
can prelate mostly leaves his
job in his declining years and
goes to England. Last year
there were 35 retired Anglican gaiters from
various parts of the world, all above ground.
What the church wants in Australia is
bishops’ graves. It has been said before,
but the remark 'Will bear repetition.
“C.A.8.”: Monsignor Carroll, who de-
parted for Paradise on the last day of Aug-
ust, had some ; quaint ways. He disapproved
thoroughly of the purse-of-sovereigns habit,
and never accepted a farewell bean from one
flock to help him on to the next. He was
absolutely Wowserish in his horror of love’s
young dream in dark places. About 30 years
ago, when in charge of Mount Carmel
parish, he arranged to have the evening de-
votions at four instead of seven, so that
the return of the worshippers should be
made in broad, unromantic daylight. On
another occasion lie organised a moonlight
picnic in aid of his church, which (Iho
picnic*) began at four o’clock and ended be-
fore -nightfall. He was a kindly old soul.
An objection was once lodged against an
aged member of his choir, who continued to
bray, though his voice had long departed.
Quoth the Monsignor cheerfully: “Oh, now.
now, we must be indulgent! Why, he sang
here 25 years ago!”

Alex. Peacock, the radiance of whose tail
set in Victorian State politics quite a while
ago, is now spoken of as the rising young
hope of the Liberal crowd for the Senate.
Samuel Mauger, manufacturer, anti-sweater
and strong Protectionist, is regarded as too
dangerous. He wouldn’t let the blessed
tariff alone. Peacock, legal manager and
mining dabbler, doesn’t need to care much
how the country’s manufacturing industries
go. _
Edward Ellis, who must be very
nearly the oldest pressman in Aus-
tralia (his years, are well over 80),
has had to give up work at last.
liis brain is stiil clear, but a pain-
ful and incurable disease makes
labor practically impossible. Mr.
Ellis, who now hangs out in Mel-
bourne, is just as wildly affluent as
might be expected in the case of
an easy-going old Bohemian who,
after the manner of most good
writers, never possessed the com-
mercial instinct, and how affluent
that is anybody may guess. It would
'greatly assist his later years if the
N.S.W. Labor party would pay him
the salary that has been owing to
him since the latter part of last
century as editor of the Sydney
Labor daily, the Post. It was a
fairly big sum, and it has been
due a long while. Ellis did several
men’s work on that ill-fated ven-
ture, and the remuneration he got
was several men’s ill-luck.

By one who knew him: —
Only one thing could be said in serious disparage-
ment of the late Judkins as a plucky enemy .of the
gambling „apd .liquor law-breakers in Melbourne.
.He on one occasion accepted information Grom
Wren’s Tote, party which he used against. Sam
fGHlott, thereby serving the Wren purpose, which was
to intimidate Premier Bent by bringing .about an
.“exposure” of his ministerial colleague. A news-
paper published certain revelations concerning
Gillott and his city property at the same time that
Judkins delivered his sermon on the . same subject,
.the matter for both attacks being supplied to; both
parties by Wren’s agent whilst serving the scheme
of the Tote proprietor. Juddy didn’t diminish his
fury against the Tote, but in thus conniving with his
enemy, as it were, the little man cut a rather • mean
figure. Finally, the Tote was sun pressed, a resmt due
partly to the Judkins agitation. Also, Sunday
trading at the pubs was scotched, if not quite ex-
terminated, by the energetic moralist. The difference
between Jud and the common or prowling wowser
was very considerable. lie was a legitimate cham-
pion of Government moral law, rather than a canting
preacher of the social moral laws which are made
by hypocrites orily to be broken by sinners at large.
Even the genuine Wowsers are unpractical in their
snuffling demands for Reform. Judkins was the
practical reformer who did something, and the hand-
some cash testimonial which came to him on his
death-bed, when he could do no more, was largely
a public gift to a decent sort of Australian.
GENERAL WADE, V.C.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was a source of worry to the Liberal whip that
he could never catch the Caucus napping. Yet, when
the chance came yesterday—when a number of Gov-
ernment supporters had gone away to the races and
there was a possibility of putting the McGowen Min-
istry out of office, the position was blundered stra-
tegically.—Sydney Sun.
He folded his arms in the front of his benches ;
With eyes like the eagle (or was it the bat?)
He frowningly stared at the enemy’s trenches
And watched someone filtering talk through his
hat ;
Alert and alive as a mid-winter adder,
He waited his moment to sound the advance,
And send his brave men with guncotton and ladder.
Good General Wade had left nothing to chance.
Great siege guns of gas in his day-dream were bang-
ing ;
His strategist’s brow was all furrowed with
thought;
While gamely liis leaders were spouting and slanging,
Campaigns of the future were plotted and fought;
He brooded apart like a genius tremendous,
Whose scheming and planning had worked to a
close,
Just waiting around for the finish stupendous—
The abject surrender of hollow-cheeked foes.
And so the long day to its darkening wended ;
The frontal attack never faltered or died ;
But when kindly, Night on the riot descended,
Some private remarked in a frantic aside
That, while the bombardment of Wade’s dogged
forces
Was raising a dust and a wearisome din,
The gallant defenders were off backing horses
And General Wade could have marched his men in !
A knell for the hero expensively sleeping !
Twine cypress and poppies to make him a wreath !
In front of the fortress his vigil he’s keeping,
A mantle of utter abstraction beneath.
A prayer for the fallen, for gfeat is their tally,
And say a few more for the leaders who don’t!
When the last Trump is blown in Jehoshaphat’s
valley,
Mayhap he will wake. But he probably won’t)
N.S.W.
PRIVATE SLEEP.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
At latest advices the Australian Govern-
ment’s note circulation was £9,346.220. In Canada,
thanks to the method of requiring the banks to hold
a fixed proportion of their cash reserves in Govern-
ment paper, it is about £23,000,000, and yet
Canada, unlike Australia, allows the banks to issue
their own notes alongside the Government paper.
Probably if Australia adopted the Canadian idea, it
could raise another £10,000,000, and if it applied
even half of that to a sinking fund, it could start
redeeming the State debts at the rate of about
£175,000 a year, which, with compound interest,
would represent a wonderful amount of good money
made out of nothing before we are all dead.
The Freetrader who scoffs at the idea of
giving Protection to the little industry because it
employs for a start only three men and a boy, ought
to turn up the record of the Broken Hill Prop.
Twenty-six years or so back that concern began with
a small furnace, into which it emptied its surface
ores, mixed with foreign coke and a bit of lime-
stone scraped together from anywhere. The product
was silver-lead bullion, which was sent to America
for treatment. Then somebody got the idea that a
handy market was waiting for silver and lead in
China and India. A refinery was started at Port
Pirie, and W. R. Wilson took a spell from horse-
breeding to open up the trade with Asia. When the
refinery started it was found that the ore contained
enough gold to pay a lot of the expenses. Naturally,
Uncle Sam hadn’t mentioned anything about the
gold ; that sort of thing is one of the blessings of
Foreign-trade. After a while the furnaces were added to
so largely that it became worth while to develop the
coke-making industry in Australia. This is now a
big thing, and the production of valuable by-products
is gradually being developed. As the locally-trained
metallurgists got more experience, they wrestled suc-
cessfully with the underlying sulphide ore. Thus, the
production of zinc concentrates was started, and now
the zinc output is worth three times as much as the
silver output. The treatment of zinc resulted in the
discovery that eucalyptus oil was specially suitable
for the flotation process, and dozens of movable oil
distilleries are at work among the peppermint
scrubs of the baclcbloeks. Incidentally, thousands of
tons of sulphuric acid are produced from the sul-
phides, a portion of which goes in the manufacture
of artificial manures—another new and important
Australian industry. As the co.’s knowledge of zinc
metallurgy increased, the distillation of zinc (spelter)
was undertaken, and that meant the manufacture of
clay retorts in Australia. Now the B. H. Prop, has
two further developments in hand. First it proposes
to extract the zinc which has been run off with the
lead-smelter slags, and when that is done the residue
will be valuable for use as a flux. To feed the fur-
naces after the ironstone capping at the mine had
been exhausted, it became necessary to open up
ironstone quarries, and with the sulphide ore from
those sources it is now proposed to establish the
iron and steel industry. Iron, steel, zinc, gold, oil,
sulphuric acid, coke and by-products, with a valuable
lift to a dozen other industries —all began with a
little 30-ton pot, just over a quarter of a centurv
ago. And the Freetrader reckons that the little in-
dustry isn’t worth encouraging because it is little,
just as the baby isn’t worth bringing up because it
isn’t full-grown.

Like almost every other concern in the stock and
station business, this old-established N.S.W. co.
thought it saw a short cut to fortune by making
advances on pastoral securities. The borrowing
mania of the late ’Bo’s had made money so plentiful in
Australia, and credit was so easily secured, that prices
naturally boomed along just as they are booming
now. Then John Cowshusband and Sandy Mcßull
wanted their money back in a hurry, so the boom
broke and prices tumbled down among the wreckage.
In the struggle to pay interest on extravagant pur-
chases, country was overstocked. Just about that
time the rabbit curse began to cry aloud, and when
even a moderate drought looked in there wasn’t
enough feed for sheep and rabbits, so both lots died by
the million. Thus Pitt, Son and Badgery could not
find some of their securities when they went to look
for them, and a long struggle to make good the
losses ensued. The table shows the latter vears of
the struggle—no dividends and copious writings-off.
The next tahle shows how various items have moved
during the last 12 years:—

As late as 1900 advances, foreclosures, etc., amounted
to £87,087 ; by 1909, these were reduced to
£34,744. As the co. got out of its back country
securities, no doubt at written-down values, it put
the money into office, store, sale-yard and similar
accommodation in and about the city. This is all
lumped now at £129,156. The biggest items doubt-
less are city freehold and Flemington saleyards. The
former are no doubt good property; the latter are
more doubtful, owing to what the chairman referred
to as “the agitation in favor of the removal of the
yards, which is unnecessary, and the absolutely un-
warrantable expenditure by the Government of about
£200,000” —that is on the Homebush abattoirs, etc.
Nowadays the co. devotes its attention almost entirely
to the selling agency, and the slump in profits for
the year is due more or less to the shortage of sup-
plies, including the wool clip, the result of the
drought. Also, wool fell a trifle, and that means a
shrinkage in the commissions, which are on a per-
centage basis. Of the dividend shown for the past
year, Is. per share, or about 6 p.c., is a bonus. This
money is not actually paid out, as a call of Is. per
share is made at the same time. This brings the
shares up to 18s., leaving 2s. to be called. A total
of 6s. has been got in in this way during the past
three years.

The Sydney harbor passenger traffic is catered for by
four companies—two of them all gold, the other two
heavily alloyed. This is one of the other two. Serv-
ing a district getatable by tram from the city, it
some time ago thought it saw an escape from slow
starvation by pushing into the charter and picnic
business. In that connection it launched out into
fairly large purchases of harhor foreshores. The
struggle to pay interest on the big capital cost was
keen enough before the Federal land tax arrived.
After that, the directors began to look around for
buyers. By an extraordinary bit of luck they, like
other subdividers, have been favored by a Sydney
suburban land boom. Hence, as the directors put it,
since the termination of the half-year we have dis-
posed of the Pearl Bay property, at a figure that
will leave a very satisfactorv increase towards t li e
current half-year’s profits.” Pearl Bay is in Middle
Harbor, just inside the Spit. The co.’s other big
property is at Clontarf, where an excited person
tried to spill the Royal Edinburgh’s gore 43 years
f™.-. The co.’s paid capital is £30,000. Its lia-
bilities, £14,374. This money and the reserves
(which include £13,716 of share premiums) are
almost entirely represented by ship properties,
£45,685, and freehold properties, wharves, etc.,
£34,894. The profit and loss account bumps all
expenses, “including depreciation,” into one line.
Whether the amount written off is adequate or other-
wise, this paper can’t even guess. Consequently it
doesn t know whether the profits as shown in the
accounts are under or over the mark. They may
even hit it. Twelve months ago the ship item stood
t j ; now it is £45,685. Meantime the
Lady Denman has been added to the flotilla, though
probably some payments had been made on account
P u nor June, 1911. It seems from those figures
that depreciation is pretty liberally allowed for, so
the profits may be quite as good as they look.

Thomas Elder is dead these many years, and R. Barr
Smith, though happily still among the living, has
been long out of the management of this co., though
he still has by far the biggest individual interest
in it. The directors are, however, men who know as
much about the wool growing and pastoral mortgage
professions as the original partners did, which is to
say that nobody in South Aus. knows more. Elder’s
Wool and Produce Co. used to be in 20,000 shares
paid to £2 10s. each and liable for another £7 10s.
Gradually the vicious habit of shuffling wool, etc.,
into a bale, and sending it to Europe to be auctioned,
was abandoned in favor of local sales. This meant,
in many cases, the financing of the growers; conse-
quently the old Elder co. was metamorphosed, and
80,000 new £lO shares were issued, on which, for
many years, only £1 15s was paid. As the business
grew the prudent old gentlemen who run this con-
cern called up first 15s„ then 10s., and last year
another 10s. This makes the shares paid to £3 10s.
The past year’s 10s. call was got in on March 1; in
the table it is assumed that the whole of the new
£50,000 has earned dividend as from that date. A
good many pastoral cos., bitten by the rabbits,
droughts and other mischievous insects of the early
’9o’s, have been shy ever since of squatting advances.
Apparently E., S. and Co. are prepared to still risk
it. Anyway, as the next table shows, in addition to
“bills receivable and advances on produce shipments,”
the company has £1.898 750 represented by “other
advances, goods, etc.” Under almost any circum-
stances there would he considerable risk for share-
holders in this business, hut the risk in this case
is all the greater because the bulk of the necessary
capital is provided by outsiders, who will want 20s.
in the£, no matter what happens to the eo.’s securi-
ties.

Jan., 1912..8119 9|„ ~ 5729 622 1768 24,184
July, 1912..8817 10 „ „ 6250 1191 1376 25.560
Some of the remarks which The Bulletin made
six months ago about this concern’s dividend badly
want making over again, and the further increase
in the amount of the div. lends them emphasis. In
the first place the mortgage remains just where it did
at July, 1911. It isn’t the monster it once was, but
it still stands at £45,000. The last time a bit was
hewed off the incubus was when the paid-up capital
was raised from £lOO,OOO to £125,000, and there
is but a moderate amount of credit to be got by
paying off liabilities with fresh capital. It is much
more to the point to shift them out of earnings, and
to judge by the Mutual Store's great lack of liquid
assets there is no immediate prospect of doing much
that way. On July 31 the institution owed £1746
to its bankers, and had, to set against that, £l5OB
cash ( £l2OO of it in London). So it really had
no loose cash at all, the debit balance being £238.
Its other more or less liquid assets were accounts
owing by customers and others, £29,819 ; against
which the society owed £21,497 to creditors and
customers', and £329 for unclaimed dividends. It
had absolutely no investments apart from the busi-
ness (and, as already mentioned, the business carries
a £45,000 mortgage) ; and it had no cash except
what it owed to the bank. Also, out of the great
absence of cash at the end of the half-year, there
was the trifle of £6250 to be paid to shareholders.
A concern which is thus trading up to the last penny
of its capital and its borrowings isn’t justified in
paying 10 p.c. Its profits are certainly increasing,
but the dividends have increased so much faster that
the amount laid by this half-year is less than it has
been in any half-year since July, 1906. The re-
serves have grown, but they certainly don't seem to
keep pace with the needs of a growing institution.
It is hereby suggested that it should be made a cast-
iron rule that £2OOO a year be paid off the mortgage
out of earnings, even if the dividend has, for a time,
to be cut down one-half. The saving of interest
would make this method more profitable in the end,
especially as interest rates are on the up-grade.
When a concern which has its shop in pawn, and
which doesn’t seem able to shift a penny of the lia-
bility out of earnings, puts up its dividend eight
times in seven years, it is taking too many chances.
In a business of this sort, the most important
point is the fair valuation of the stock and plant.
As regards that matter, it is impossible to offer an
opinion. Hitherto the store has taken stock every
half-year, and has found the process costly. It now
proposes to take stock only once a year. The matter
is of no real importance; an annual stocktaking by
an experienced pessimist might be much better than
a half-yearly one by an optimist. And if the amount
saved is applied to the reduction of the mortgage,
the new method will almost certainly be an improve-
ment.

This is a company of long standing. It is nominally
aged 12 years, but it is a reconstruction and en-
largement of a previous concern which dated back to
1879, and that was, in its turn, a reconstruction of
a much older fire and life concern, which grew so
large that it split in three. Very rich, very re-
spectable, solid and fat is the Victoria. Business
comes to it by reason of its solid old name, and it
doesn’t need to get up a vulgar perspiration run-
ning after business. And it is a good class of
business, too, which surges in at its doors, hence the
gorgeous dividends. The Victoria doesn’t aim at
mere size with any great intensity, but to some
extent size has come to it, as shown in the following
figures:—

Somewhere about 11 years ago, when we were all a
good deal younger than we are now, this society had
an heroic wash-up, and wrote off half its capital, and
wrote down its assets, and fixed its old depositors for
five years on a 4 p.c. basis. The amount of capital
which disappeared in that calamity was not exactly
shown, but it was not far short of £50,000. Since
then a portion of the lost capital has re-appeared,
because property values have recovered, and proper-
ties have sold for more than their written down
value. These recovered amounts have been placed to
reserve fund ; in fact, out of the £15,133 added to
reserves between January, 1905, and July, 1912, no
less than £11,504 was merely a return of sums
previously written off. The directors were so lavish
with dividends that the amount added to reserves
out of current profits was hardly worth mentioning.
It is now proposed to restore some of the lost capital.
Each share of £2 10s. paid up is to be split into two
£1 shares fully paid, and one £1 share paid to 10s.
By a simultaneous 10s. call and 10s. bonus, the latter
share is to be made fully paid at the expense of
the reserve fund. This will reduce the reserve by
about £9560, and correspondingly increase the
capital.
Some further figures concerning the Standard
follow:
Julv, ’Ol. July, ’ll. Jan., ’l2. July, ’l2.
Liabilities ..£37,015 £35,877 £36,182 £42,244
Advances .. 19,998 80,388 95,486 105,317
Foreclosures . . 72,305 30,973 19,932 17,990
Cash, etc. .. 2,778 234 340 171
The position is very strong in one respect, inasmuch
as the shareholders’ funds represent nearly £79,000,
while the liabilities to the public are only £42,244.
Only the sort of slump that would arise through
an invasion by the Huns could push down values to
a point at which the depositors would be seriously
endangered. In another sense the position is rather
weak, inasmuch as, in the desire to incite business,
all the capital, the reserve fund, the contingency
fund, the deposits and a trifle of £2200 borrowed
from the bank have been shoved into advances.
Liquid assets are nil. So, if the depositors are
struck by the coming depression, and start to with-
draw heavily in order to buy boots for themselves, it
may become a case of realising securities rather more
rapidly than is profitable.

In February a “special dividend” of £4OBO was paid,
and, later, 4080 new shares at par were issued to
present holders, so really the new shares were a gift,
a slice being taken out of reserves for the purpose.
This proceeding raised the capital from £40,800 to
£44,880. In spite of the opposition of the Govern-
ment quarry, the profits continue to forge ahead,
with the boom, of course; but when the building
boom bursts, or fades a trifle, this co.’s profits, like
those of most construction cos., may come down to
earth with something dimly resembling a thud. The
possible horse-power of that thud can, to some extent,
be judged by the figures for 1905. However, even if
the next general slump is worse than the one of 10
years ago, this co. won't feel it nearly so severely,
being in a much better position to stand a knock.
Co. owns quarries at Guildford, Prospect and
Emu Plains, and for each ton of material taken out,
writes off a small sum ; so that, when the quarries
are worked out, the shareholders won’t be left with
a worthless hole in the earth carrying a large book
value. Quarries, plant and machinery stand now in
the books at £42,318, as against £41,823 12
months ago. In the meantime large additions and
improvements have been made, so it looks as though
quite sufficient is being written off. The next table
shows the progress of the co. in another direction:—
June, 1905. June, 1912.
Liabilities.. .. ..£7730 £1,086
Liquid assets .. . . 4076 12,738
Deficit .. ..£3654'
Surplus .. .. £11,652
Sales of sand, gravel and blue metal have taken a
big jump during the half-year. As at December,
1911, the sales were £17,9i0. At June, 1912, they
were £24,054, an increase of £6144. But when the
bad times come, and pater familias isn’t so particu-
lar about the appearance of the garden path, and the
100 or so municipal councils can’t afford to repair
the roads, things won’t be so cheerful.

Bonus and dividends are, as usual, deferred till the
December half-year. The profits for the six months
show a big improvement, though big profits in a
co-operative concern don’t always mean good man-
agement. A co.. which shows a very small surplus
at the end of the year may be better managed than
one whose returns are very fat. In one case the
suppliers are given the highest possible price for their
produce: in the other, prices may be cut low to
swell the dividends. But this co. appears to be pretty
well managed. Commissions show a healthy increase
from £4057 to £4640 for the six months. Liabilities,
mostly amounts owing to suppliers, total £22,268;
and the principal assets are cash in hand £11,017,
and sundry debtors (amounts owing over the last
two weeks of the half-year—payments being made
fortnightly) £20,003. The capital, as is generally
the case in co-op. concerns, is very small, being only
£ 995 ; but the reserves are very solid, so the posi-
tion in that respect is safe.

The average profits for the past three years are a
good deal better than those of the first three in the
table, but not as much as they appear to be,
because the capital has been just doubled since 1900.
It now consists of £123,305 in ordinary shares and
£76,695 in preference, less a few pounds of unpaid
calls. Kaiapoi is one of the most highly reputed
woollen manufacturing concerns in Australasia, and
its products are popular. Yet it had to pay 61 p.c.
to get even preference capital, and after it has paid
that much, and a strictly moderate 7 p.c. to ordinary
shareholders, all that is left over could be comfort-
ably tucked into a vest pocket. Compared with
banking and other branches of pawnbrokery, the
manufacturing business has so far little gilt on its
gingerbread in these parts. Fortunately this co.
doesn’t now have to shoulder any vast amount of
debt, the new capital having been used to reduce
liabilities. The next table shows that aspect of the
matter and a few other things:—

By far the biggest asset is stock, which includes raw
materials. This item cannot be checked in any way,
and the management’s estimate has to be swallowed
whole. There have been such things as errors in
stock-taking, and investors have to risk that. This is
one reason why it is far safer to take a general view
of a few years’ profits than to go wholly on one year s.
The substantial amounts shown as written off have
been in reduction of property and plant book values.
The allowance for the past year was a shade over 4
n.c. —though vou don’t learn much from thnt. seeing
that freeholds, which may depreciate very little, and
nlant, whic'i may denreciate verv much, are tied up
in one bundle. However, the accounts leave a satis-
factorv impression, and manufacturers of this class
of goods have always the country’s growing disposi-
tion to use more of its own products running in
their favor.

WATER LEVELS.—Many painful accidents have
occurred through fracture of water gauge glasses on
steam boilers, and various devices to prevent injury
to boiler attendants have been adopted. These,
however, are more or less objectionable, because
the clear view of the water-level is thereby ob-
scured. At last a practically unbreakable Gauge
Glass has been invented, namely, “BISHOP’S ADA-
MANT,” which will resist considerable pressure as
well as high and varying temperature. To be ob-
tained from Bell’s Asbestos, Ltd., Engineers’ Factors,
315 Kent Street, Sydney.*
From June to September the Bacchus Marsh Shire
Council (Vic.) crushed and spread 3000 cub. yds. of
road metal, at an average cost of 2s. 6d. per cub.
yd., and the cost of the spalls per cub. yd. averaged
2s. 4d., making a total cost of 4s. lOd. per cub. yd.,
broken and spread. Their last contract under the
hand-breaking system amounted to 6s. 3d. per cub.
yd-, slacked on the roadside, and spreading Is.;
total 7s. 3d., as against 4s. lOd. — a saving of 2s. sd.
per cub. yd. This result was obtained* by using a
Bigelow 16in. x 9in. Patent Stone-crusher, and the
Austral Otis Eng. Co., Ltd., of Melbourne, are the
manufacturers of these breakers.* [3

The year’s record by the Pioneer tin mine
at Bradshaw’s Creek (Tas.) reveals that Cecil G.
Evan tills in his solitude well. He always flirts out
some up-and-doing record, and this tune he lets
lie-ht into the reserves of the Pioneer and the new
Argonaut leases. Since 1900 the Pioneer has cut out
4 979 600 cubic yards of drift for 4333 tons of black
tin the average being 19501 b. per cubic yard. This
average compares with 15931 b. won for the past
year. Compare these figures with the reserves, which
total : Stream Tin
Cub. Yds. per Cub. Yd.
In the Pioneer .. 4,298,000 .9801 b.
In the Argonaut .. 1,142,600 .993 „
There is thus 5,440,600 cubic yards, averaging
9861 b. of stream tin per cubic yard to work. The
estimate is based on bores cut, but Cecil G. has
always been lucky in hitting the general average
with his drill. Of course there is a vast difference
in an average of .9801 b. per cubic yard and 1.9501 b.
obtained in the past from the Pioneer; but as
work goes down the lead a lot more drift has to be
handled. It is hardly possible that costs will drop
much below the last year's run of 4.971 d. per cubic
yard, but the installation of the belt conveyer may
help a bit that way. The Argonaut carries shallow
ground, running up to 12ft., and, therefore, should be
a cheap proposition. A lot of the drift is expected
to run to ljlb. of stream tin per cubic yard, and
balance should pan out at about 0.81 b., so that pro-
fits seem to be safe. The beauty of the situation to
the owners is that tin is wanted the world over, and
there isn't any new field rushing out supplies to
break the market. Last year’s work at the Pioneer
gave a gain of £55,510, or, deducting office expenses,
a net profit of £53,114. This, cut up at 4s. 9d. per
share in divs., allowed £5OOO to be written off
plant, £1915 to be paid for taxes, and enabled
£18,551 to be carried forward. Co. has £12,250
of liquid assets, and owes £5158, so that it won’t
perish over the attempt to equip the Argonaut. This
little table explains how the Pioneer has so prospered
of late years:—
Average Price of Tin per Ton.
1910 £142 6 3
1911 173 13 6
1912 190 16 4

Another mining engineer’s views:—
This is how lodide Mineral Hill is loaded by the
formation of the newer and larger company:—
lodide’s quarter interest
2400 fully-paids at £lO ..£24,000
1200 contributing at £2 .. 2,400
£26,400
Balance of payment (either in shares or
cash) to original owner of quarter-in-
terest. . .. . . . , . . . . 6,000
Purchase of remaining three-fourths in-
terest (in cash) .. .. .. .. 50,000
Promoter’s cut for expenses, sale of options
and profit .. .. .. .. 27,000
Total purchase price .. . £109,400
Nominal capital, 15,000 shares at £lO .. 150,000
Working capital .. .. .. £40,600
The quarter-interest of the old lodide Co. is thus
valued at £32,400, mostly in shares, while the three-
quarters interest is valued at £50,000 cash. Pro-
moter's cut is 2300 fully-paids and £4OOO cash.
From the published portion of Fawcett’s report the
following is to be gathered: The mine is shallow,
the bottom level being only 90ft. deep, with the
deepest winze, 20ft. below that. Ore is lead car-
bonate and free oxides of silver, with patchy gold
contents; gangue is silicious ironstone; width of
deposit not proved, as only the hanging-wall is de-
fined. Above the 60ft. level the ore is estimated to
be 12,500 tons, “worth, approximately, £8 per ton
gross value in lead, silver and gold.” Purchase of
the mine recommended as a sound mining venture.
Well, it may be. But there is not one solitary
assay given in the issued report, on which the public
are invited to apply for shares in the new company.
The size of the block of ore reserves isn’t mentioned
either, the only statement being that the 60ft. level
is 100 ft. long. The ore may extend to the grass
roots, or it may not; but even if it does, the block
would need to average 20ft. to 22ft. in width to give
the tonnage stated. Most, if not all, the work has
been done near the hanging-wall only, which appears
to be the richest side. The reserves are admitted to
be not “blocked-out,” i.e., exposed on four sides, but
in one magnificent sweep they are made out as 12,500
tons, averaging £8 per ton ; or, rather to be “worth”
that much, which might be interpreted by the un-
suspecting that the ore reserves are valued at
£lOO,OOO. What is their actual value? Cost of
mining and treatment, etc, is nowhere to be found,
but a line may be taken through the return from a
parcel that has already been sent away to Cockle
Creek. From 4000 tons of lode material 1280 tons
were sorted, and, according to the report, “netted
the sum of £9BOO, after deduction of railway freight
and all smelting charges.” Not a word about the
road freight, or the mining or the development charge.
To put the matter as favorably as possible, deduct
only (say) 15s. (too low to be realised) to mine the
4000 tons = £3OOO from the £9BOO, and the result
works out at a profit of 345. per ton of lode material,
which is the crude ore. Now, it is not at all likely
that the crude ore, yielding the picked parcel, was
any poorer than that left in the mine, and the
chances are that it was a good deal richer. But
allow that it was a representative sample. The block
of 12,500 tons would at that rate be worth £21,250.
It is true that lead has risen since the parcel was
sent, but the rise cannot be depended upon. And
there is nothing to prove that, in valuing the reserves
at £8 per ton, lead was not taken at the present
figure of well over £2O, for the basis of valuation
is also not stated. Anyway, there is a big difference
between £21,250 and the purchase price, leaving the
working capital out of the question. That the mine
is becoming poorer in depth appears to be indicated
by even the present shallow workings, as witness the
indirect testimony of the report. At the 90ft. level
there is a comparatively rich patch of ore, for 150 ft.,
valued at £6 Bs., but even so, it is suggested that
this ore should be concentrated to £9 or £lO per
ton by the rejection of half. This means leaving
£2 16s. to £3 16s. in each ton discarded, and is
pretty abundant proof that costs are exceptionally
high. It takes high-class ore to stand a 42 miles
journey by road and over 400 miles by rail, with a
stiff smelting charge waiting for it at the end of the
trip. The mine may be good, but not, on present de-
velopments, for £150,000.

At Baker’s Creek mine (N.S.W.) stoping
has been started above No. 11 level on Smith’s Reef.
The drive is in 116 ft. The same reef has been driven
46ft. in No. 12 level, and the reef averages from 6in.
to 9in. thick. Forty-four tons of stone were crushed
from No. 3 reef for 50oz. of gold. Tributors made
a final clean-up of 36 tons for 2640 z. The manager
has nothing to say as to whether there is any pros-
pect of the Baker’s Creek co. obtaining any of this
rich stone. The fact that the co. had to make a call
while some of the tributors were crushing small lots
of stone worth from 7oz. to lOoz. per ton was gall-
ing. The sharemarket is anything but strong, and
there are probably some forfeited shares.

Q.E.D.: The Junction North S.M. Co. ap-
pears to be out of order in making the move it has.
In an exactly similar case, Bisgood v. Henderson’s
Transvaal Estates ([l9oB] 1 Ch. 743), Lord Justice
Buckley in the Court of Appeal (Eng.), the highest
court for dealing with such matters, gave this de-
cision: “In my opinion any attempt so to define the
constitution of the'co. as that the members shall in
an event be liable for a large sum, is a breach of
the Statute, and ultra vires. Any clause which Can
be used to maintain a scheme which imposes upon
a member the alternative of accepting liability
for a larger sum, or of being dispossessed of his
status as a shareholder upon terms which he is not
bound to accept, is, I think, ultra vires Also, the
J.N. has not yet gone into voluntary liquidation, nor
can it so long as it is in debt.
Harvey Patterson, of the Broken Hill Pro-
prietary, is an admirer of the big Iron Knob flux
quarry in S. Australia, which has already supplied
the Port Pirie smelting works with 680,709 tons of
ore. Cheap methods of transport have to some ex-
tent exploded the idea that the contiguity of iron
ore to coal is essential to cheap production of pig
iron. But cheap transport went hand in hand with
the steam-shovel mining of friable ore, large 40-ton
trucks, broad-gauge railways, and rapid loading and
unloading of steamboats. Low costs, for Obtaining
the raw material, and assembling the ore, flux and
fuel at the furnace are obviously essential. Assum-
ing that pig iron is to be produced at 50s. per ton,
and that ore from the Iron Knob will give a furnace
extraction of 66 units, it is evident that, if the cost
of carrying the ore from the mine to the furnace were
only 10s. per ton, it would still equal 30 p.c. of the
whole working cost on pig iron at 50s. per ton, for,
under the most favorable conditions, it would require
l'J tons of ore to produce a ton of pig iron. Ore of
50 p.c. grade would make the cost of carriage, at
10s, per ton, exceedingly burdensome. This isn’t
said to belittle the splendid deposit in S.A., but
simply to show that the contiguity of iron ore to
coal in N.S.W. or Queensland is an enormous advan-
tage.
* # * *
The Grafton copper mine (N.S.W.) is
doing some prospecting in the hope of refreshing its
ore reserves. This mine names its levels alphabeti-
cally. “A” level has been driven a total of 1340 ft.
At 1320 ft. solid ore of good quality was passed
through, but very soon afterwards the ore channel
became considerably mixed. At the 1340 ft. mark the
ore commenced to make again, and 9in. of sulphide
was showing in the face. There are other places
that show encouraging prospects. The smelter treated
243 tons for the week, for a return of 34 tons of
matte, containing 14J tons of copper. A new bottom
was smelted into No. 1 furnace, which absorbed some
of the copper. This accounts for the return being
below the usual grade. For the previous week 115
tons were smelted for matte containing 11.3 tons
of copper.
* * * *
Mount David (N.S.W.) is opening the
shoot of stone discovered in its south-east level from
the new shaft. This was driven 75ft. from the
crosscut, and for a distance of 25ft. a good-looking
quartz vein was exposed, carrying gold. A winze
has been started, and the manager estimates the
stone shot up is worth 25dwt. per ton. Some years
since a good shoot of stone was opened from a winze
below the old workings, so there is a chance that a
still deeper shoot of payable stone may be dis-
covered from the new shaft.
Amalgamated Hill End (N.S.W.) had
such a good-looking run of quartz in Phillipson’s
north level that it seemed, while the driving was
going on, highly probable that the good stone would
extend upwards for a considerable distance. But a min-
ing “certainty” very often is, after all, only a sport-
ing chance, and Phillipson’s reef is an instance, for
the rises and stopes Over the level show an erratic
reef of variable size and value. It is quite possible,
though, that the good stone met with in the level
may extend downwards, but that remains to be
proved. The present outlook is overcast because of
the erratic nature of the vein overhead.
Brilliant St. George (Charters Towers)
closed its half-year with a credit of £2538. Stoping
has been carried out on the vein of stone (a cross
reef with a north and south trend) at the 1450 ft.
level. This vein varies from 3in. to 18in. in thick-
ness. Fifty-seven tons crushed at the rate of £9 3s.
per ton, but the profit was only £220. Costs are
very heavy. This co. has recently crushed another
50 tons for £9 11s. 9d. per ton. No. 6 level ap-
pears to have a fair chance of picking up this cross
reef. There are 144,000 shares.

Brilliant Blocks (Charters Towers) has
not succeeded in cutting the footwall reef worked by
its neighbor, the Brilliant Deeps. Its crosscut is in
a northerly direction from the shaft, and is in more
than 600 ft., so is beyond the estimated distance. The
Deeps, next door, found some difficulty in picking up
this footwall formation in their No. 2 level, and it
was only after sinking some distance that the vein
of quartz opened out. The Deeps estimate that they
have 300 ft. of backs above No. 4 (2540 ft.). Brilliant
Blocks is, however, encouraged by the fact that No.
4 level took a south-westerly trend, and has conse-
quently been approaching the Blocks’ boundary at an
angle. The chance of striking the reef at the 2000 ft.
level depends very considerably on the point along
the boundary cut by the crosscut. Shoots of payable
stone alternate with blanks.
* * * *
At Croydon (Q.), Viles’s Block crushed
691 tons for 12020 z. of gold ( £2 14s. 2d. per oz.).
This makes the crushing worth £3255 ( £4 4s. per
ton). A dividend of 3d. per share ( £600) has been
declared. Cuthbert’s Content is crushing 400 tons of
stone.
* * * *
The O.K. (N.Q.) is getting busy in its effort
to find the fortune which it reckons the old improvi-
dent co. didn't look for too earnestly. The water is down
to the 200 ft. level, and is expected to be all out in
a week or two. Trouble is being handed out with
acid water, bad gases and collapsed workings. The
80ft. level is completely blocked with fallen ground.
* * * *
The Mount Oxide Options, after passing
on its large baby to the new company, has another
prospective entrant for the beauty show. An option
has been secured over a tin-bearing area at Stannifer
(y.), reported to be rich as well as large, carrying
21b. of tin to the yard.
* * * *
High lead and zinc prices are responsible
for a re-trial of the Lady Duff, at Stannary Hills
(Q.). A local syndicate is now working the show.
A 50-ton parcel has been sent to the Chillagoe smelters,
and a weekly output of similar tonnage is expected.
The lode is said to be 25ft. to 30ft. wide, where
worked at present, and outcrops a long distance on
the surface. Ancient history says the ore used to
average somewhere about £lO, but it took more than
this to cover the freight alone before the construction
of the Cairns railway.
* * » *
Hampden-Cloncurry (Queensland! main-
tains an average of 12 p.c. copper. It smelted 3750
tons for 454 tons of blister, containing by estimation
450 tons copper, 1640 z. gold and 58740 z. silver.
The ore is mostly silicious at the Duchess, so a tram
line is being pushed out to Mount Sylvia ironstone
quarry to facilitate the hauling of fluxes. Some of
the so-called pyritic ore in the Oloncurry district
contains a lot of silica, so it isn't true to its name.
Calcite (containing lime) occurs in some of the
mines, notably in the Great Australian, but generally
there is an excess of silica, so that large quantities
of barren flux have to be used.
* * * *
Croydon (Q.) is the most northerly gold
field in Australia, and its better half, the Golden
Gate, is so flat and sandy that Mulligan, the veteran
prospector, expressed his surprise at the discovery
of a rich reef in a “swamp.” There are other sur-
prising things about Croydon, for the gold is usually
heavily alloyed with silver. Cuthbert’s Content
mine crushed 1686 tons for 57320 z., but the crush-
ing was only worth £9246, so in this instance the
“gold” was worth under 355. per oz. Costs, includ-
ing development, ran away with £5794, leaving a
balance of £3452.
* * * *
Amalgamated Monkland and Glanmire
(Gympie) continues to sink in search of black slate
floors. To a depth of 'lo7sft. no great thickness of
slate had been met with. The black color is sup-
posed to be due to carboniferous or organic matter,
originally deposited when the slate was formed. This
carbon of black slate appears to enrich any reef that
may pass through it, so at Gympie nothing is ex-
pected unless the dark-colored slate is met with.
The Great Northern, a gold mining con-
cern in the Warning Finger of N.Q., seems to have
lost its teeth and grown to pension age. Its latest
effort was a 48-ton crushing, which took nine weeks
to get out, and the result was 91oz. odd. A few
men—mostly in pairs—are scattered among the levels,
but the results would hardly raise a shout, even of
drinking water. No. 4 north level has a 4ft. reef,
which is as hungry as a Domain beer-hum. Still, it
used to hold its head very high. Its last dividend
was a ss. one on 10,000 shares, which brought its
total dividend score to £1 17s. on £1 shares. But
its last bit of profit dividing was done seven years
ago, since when the concern has grown a “down-at-
the-heels” appearance.
* * * *
The Federal reward lease (160 acres),
situated 25 miles N.W. of Oloncurry (Q.) has been
sold to W. Orr for £6OOO. This lease was men-
tioned in the warden’s report as a hopeful prospect-
ing proposition. While under option considerable
work was done just above water-level, and 30 tons of
ore assaying approximately 40 p.c. copper were ob-
tained. * Some hundreds of tons of high-grade ore
have been shipped from this claim during the past
four years. Oloncurry has not shown any conclusive
sign that large bodies of payable ore exist below
550 ft.
* * * *
Waihi (Maoriland), after producing
£9,997,266, is in trouble over its mine, which has
been idle for 17 weeks, partly because of a labor
dispute and partly because the ore reserves have
been seriously depleted. The hydro-electric power
scheme has been suspended in order not to draw too
heavily on the reserve fund, which, after deducting
£BO,OOO for electric power, stood at £90,000. In
Maoriland, 367 shareholders, holding 30,455 shares,
are in favor of investing money in other mines, while
173 shareholders, representing 15,195 shares, are
against the proposal. Possibly, an amalgamation
with the Waihi Grand Junction is in the air, for the
latter has shown improved form, just when the future
of the Waihi itself became overcast. The hydro-
electric scheme, instead of helping the co., became
a burden. In 1910 Waihi shares were skied up to £lO.
They are now down to 355., and the 2s. dividend
doesn’t help Waihi out of the slough. In 1910, be-
fore the big slump, 450,689 tons of ore returned
£926,100, but since then the returns have gradually
dwindled. The past is glorious, but the prospects
for all the Waihi mines are speculative.
* * * *
Woodlark Island (Papua) is showing bet-
ter form as a reefing field. The Woodlark King, for
six weeks, put through 128 tons for 1198 oz. An
average of over 9oz. per ton isn’t bad from a reef
that measures 3ft. wide in the sink. Another crush-
ing of 60 tons, put through Slater’s mill, averaged
15dwt. per ton. There are other ' good shows on
Woodlark, and the battery at Bushai is at present
crushing good stone. The Rulamadau mine, for a
run of six weeks, crushed 1463 tons and cyanided
1000 tons, for a total return of £lB3O. Nothing is
said about profit, and as a 2d. call has been made,
the co.’s finances probably show considerable lee-
way.
* * * *
Poseidon Alluvial (Vic.) got its new issue
of shares off like hot cakes. Finances are now lev-
elled up, though there will have to be another call
or two before dead work is done with and the lead
tapped from the new shaft. Still, the word goes
round that the right crowd intends to flutter the
flag over the show. What haps it then so long as
dealing is lively and a new market counter pro-
vided. All the Poseidon has done so far has been to
prove a rich, narrow run of wash and a rippling bow
of water. This was in the top part of the lease.
Now the gamble is whether the same values exist
in the main gutter or if the claim is a squib.
Groat Southern, Butherglen (Vic.), is
reminding all and sundry that the field still has a
kick left in it. The day was when the Chiltern
Valley and the Great Southern chased one another
on the dividend list. But the Chiltern Valley is
practically moribund, and so manager Thomas, in
the rival show, has been left to whip his claim into
vitality. A patch of 3090 z., as the result of three
days’ work, has helped him on the job, and, coming
on top of the previous higher average returns, gives
a bit in hand to the Great Southern Board towards
a Christmas box. In the south workings the gutter
makes towards a tributary, and this may be helping
.yields. As the co. has only a short length of the
lead at the end of the mine, enrichment is needed to
the north in order to keep up the returns.
* » * *
Capt. Teddy Aides is off from Melbourne
to Asia again to land another big tin god. He goes
to pick up the Henzai Basin, which has hung heavily
on the hands of a Melbourne crowd for more than
a month or two. The genial Teddy pledges his bank
balance to put up a dredge, to cost a modest
£20,000, should he elect to pouch the property.
One or two Melbourne mining experts tell tall yarns
about the claim, so everyone counts on Teddy clinch-
ing a deal. The ground has a newly-fledged neighbor
with an unpronounceable name. That, too, has been
floated off with the aid of Sydney and Tasmanian
tin men, who are represented by Jammy Pratten
and Palfryman. Teddy is credited with having a
finger in that pie also.
From all accounts, a rude awakening
awaits the Melbourne co. recently formed to make
money out of the old Augusta, at Laverton (W.A.).
The shoot in the property has only the length of
half the width of George-street, Sydney, and as
practically all that can be seen of it has been rooted
out and development neglected, the 10-head mill
now in course of erection is apparently being in-
stalled to treat ore which the co. hasn’t got.
Shareholders are advised to look into things.
* * * *
Hope’s Hill, at Southern Cross (W.A.),
is now earning a bit of money for a party of work-
ing miners. Years ago some £32,000 was won by
a company. Then the shoot of gold began to show
a serious falling off in value, and gave such poor
results that the show was closed down. Trouble-
some sulphides are also said to have hastened the
demise.
