A WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, September 9, 1912.
My Dear Arini.—
The G.-G. Denmans came back from their
Queensland jaunt with healthy shins of pale
tan and ecstatic memories of “cutting out”
beasts with crumpled horns and flaring in-
dignant eyes at Malvern Downs. Ma-
genta, the property of the Fairburn
Pastoral Co. (the Fairburns own land
about the size of a large English county),
where the Denmans also rested the soles
of their riding boots, has a palatial home-
stead and the usual absentee landlords.
Manager Charles Maclean, in charge, and
Mrs. Mac (she was a Miss Hood, of Bris-
bane, with a youngish uncle well known as
Polo Hood) made the welkin ring and the
domestic board groan under the fatted beast
for the vice-regal visit. Magenta, by the
way, is mostly a sheep walk on the fringe
of cattle country. The Gee-Gee and the
Nutting were up by the light of the
morning star to gallop over to Logan
Downs (another Fairburn property) in re-
cord time —a 60-mile sprint. They spent the
remnant of the day swallowing the cham-
pagne air Outback, buying ponies, etc., and
trotted back next morning. If the viceroy
had been born earlier and in a different
station of life, he should have made a good
Mahratta or Pindaree, or a fine recruit for
Sim Bolivar’s cavalry.
During the recent Denman exodus to the
Northern State, Don Walter de Barttelot de
Gee-Gee and his tall, fair lady stayed on
the outer brink of bricks and mortar, some
miles beyond the palm trees of Brisbane.
The Collins homestead was the place. Here
they became so firmly rooted to the soil as
to become quite good imitations of the in-
digenous article. The Barttelots tore them-
selves up by the roots to hurry back, that
they might shed a farewell tear in the sor-
rowful House of Eviction, in Sydney.
From about 200 miles west of Rockhamp-
ton to any spot under the sunset (Q.) the
squattocracy in skirts has now taken seri-
ously to the ride astride. The Denman
lady set the fashion about three weeks ago,
and I hear the old leather-horned offence is
being torn out by the roots, and orders for
the long gentlemanly jacket and etceteras
are falling on local tailors like leaves in a
westerly gale. For rough bush tracks, with
the frequent and treacherous log under their
horses’ hoofs, bushwomen often used to do
by stealth the ride astride, which they now
perform under the public eye, as the correct
balance of power. For some years women
have practically admitted that they have
legs, so that some of Mrs. Grundy’s ancient
horror at the thought of a female form safely
poised in the centre of the saddle, instead
of dangling yards of habit on one side of
her sweating beast, is passing into the place
of forgotten follies.
“You are soon to have in your midst,”
scribbles an earth-tramping friend, “aviator
Busteed as an instructor in the art of flying.
He is most successful, and handles his
machine like a magician. I trusted my only
girleen to his care for one flight, and never
felt nervous for a moment as they floated
into cloudland.”
Mayor Clarke has called a meeting in the
vestibule of Sydney Town Hall for Monday
afternoon, September 16. The cause is the
proposed new institute for shellbacks in New-
castle. Afternoon-tea and musical items will
be used as levers to support the charitable
when they hand out a coin or promissory
note for the new building, through which
the rising tide will not burst into the even-
ing hymn. Governor Chelmsford, Sea Field-
Marshal King-Hall, his Washup of New-
castle and a few important clerical gaiters,
including his Anglican Legs of Sydney, will
heave their sympathy at the meeting - .
A London correspondent remarks : “I saw
Miss Elizabeth Jenkins, formerly of Roslyn
Gardens Kindergarten College" (Sydney),
imbibing deep draughts of learning from
various scholastic conferences here. She
also hovered on the rim of the Eugenics
gathering, which drew together a wonderful
assortment of thinkers who thought in
several languages.”
Invitations have been out for a week or
two for the wedding of Miss Constance
Drummond and Captain Bruen, R.N. Sep-
tember 14 is the date. The Admirable King-
Hall and his lady are in charge of the festive
gathering at Admiralty House. This is to
be the champagne postscript to the legal
sailor’s knot, which is to be tied in the Dock-
yard Church, Garden Island.
For the final farewell hop (October 4),
given by the popular Denmans in October,
guests are requested (unofficially) not to
wear black-edged swords, or cocked hats at
half-mast.
Danish . Consul Marcher, who has been
anchored in this port for many years, is off
to his native Jutlarid, in a fortnight or
thereabouts.
Six feet four inches of elongated humanity,
named Broome P. Smith, F.R.G.S.. recently
blew into this town from West Africa, and
adjacent mosquito-bitten wilds. Smith is
loaded to the Plimsoll mark with lantern
slides, and may lecture to White Austra-
lians on black missions and similar heathen
diversions.
Miss Ilka Marich has decided to wear her
orange blossoms on October 2, in St.
Philip’s Church. The groom-to-be is Mr.
A. E. Forster. His mother, by the way,
has given the bride-elect her blessing and a
dashing motor-car. Ilka is a popular
maiden, and the eldest daughter of tlie well-
known music-warehouse magnate, P. F.
Marich, of this Babylon or hutch.
. A correspondent sends an infuriated scrap
from a remote waterside suburb: “Is it a
fact that the Attorney-General’s wife, Mrs.
Holman, is getting four guineas a day as
expenses for her forthcoming inquiry into
the state of Mrs. Britannia’s cottage loaf?’.’
Better ask Mr. Holman.
The fierce, hard fist of coincidence recently
smote artists Joe Wolinski and Bruce Rob-
ertson in the same place—the doorstep of a
picture show. In 1910 the Australian
brushers bumped into each other near the
front latch of the Royal Academy (Lon-
don). Wolinski, with some of his wares under
one arm. presumably smiled the perky smile
of one who then had a picture hung on the
sacred R.A. walls, the season being in full
bud. A week or so ago they met again—same
kind of bump —before hanging day at the
R.A. Society’s rooms, Sydney. Mutual re-
clamations. Robertson’s etchings mostly
have a Scotch accent, and a warm brown
complexion. The National Gallery has
bought two of these reminiscences of his 15
years of wanderings in foreign climes.
Sydney’s ex-Mayoress Allen Taylor got
away with a diamond fence or tiara for her
hair the other afternoon. Lady Chelmsford
handed over the glittering bauble on the
part of a number of Sydney committee
dames, who took this method of expressing
their burst of gratitude. Though ex-Mayor-
ess Taylor was rough and ready in her rule,
she had a capable grip on the business of
every charity meeting, and she managed the
Orphans’ Smallclothes Association, and such
like, with a mailed fist (a la Kaiser William)
when necessary. Also, when opposition
arose, she could be a foe in shining armor.
(This is also from Kaiser William, and it all
arises from the fact that I incautiously ate
some Frankfurt sausage yesterday.) Her
ability for flogging together the loose ends
of disjointed chatter that dribbled away the
time of a committee meeting was never dis-
puted. The sensitive Squirmed under the
vigorous rounding-up of the large municipal
lady, but the business in hand was pushed
into the right place. Charities of every
brand of religious belief were very glad to
pocket fat cheques as the indirect results
of the City Mother’s stern “Be quiet, you
women!” The ex-Mayoress handsomely
earned a public recognition of her good,
though somewhat loud, works.
Private Sec. Vernon, of the G.-G.’s staff,
is alleged to be an amateur mummer of some
size. He is also suspected of a strong lean-
ing to writing playlets or curtain-raisers by
the stealthy light of the midnight gas-jet.
One Cecil Leys, whose parent edits the
Auckland Star, is snatching a spring holi-
day in this sea-girt city or partial Venice of
N.S.W.
Star-lady Mary Proctor was the guest of
the Pioneer Club on Friday afternoon. In
this exclusive haunt, dedicated to the por-
traits of ancestors who did something for
themselves or for this yriung country, and
did for themselves with considerable cash
success, modern cakelets and tea of five
minutes’ brew, appeared for Mary’s benefit.
(N.B.—A scoffer wants to know why a
pioneer cake or ancestral sandwich didn’t
grace the feast.) The Pioneer Club, having
presented the starry visitor with a waratah
and some yellow-cheeked primroses, retired
into dignified oblivion, and asked the lady
to favor them with a few stellar observa-
tions. After doing her best endeavor to
dodge the heavenly “shop,” Mary got to the
end of her chat, and was asked to get back
to where she started from and side-track the
Milky Way, or something of the kind. So
Mary, under her best hat, pursued the South-
ern Cross before the anxious eye of the
Pioneers and their canvas ancestors.
Mrs. Essie (Jenyns) Wood sends a glow-
ing foreword about her new home in Surrey
(England), and the garden that is going
to bloom under the hand of Kelway. “It
is on the summit of Kingston Hill, 25min.
by car from London.... from my bedroom
window in the moonlight I can see the fal-
low deer feeding in Richmond Park....”
Coming back to mere mundane shows, after
trailing a poetic pen across tall Canterbury
bells, buttercups and daisies in the early
morning dew, Mrs. Essie lets herself down
gently on to the tourney at Earl’s Court:
“It proved to be more of a society function
than anything else.” We know that kind of
entertainment here, at which the ;f alleged
quality of the guests is more swollen than
the cash receipts. “In my party were Marie
Corelli, Mr. Alex. Wilkinson and daughter
Ly 01....”
Mrs. George-Cornwallis West organised
the tourney. Queen Alexandra peeped out
of one of the towers, and serene highnesses
of every rank —solid silver and merely
electro-plate—were perched high above the
blast of trumpets from the vocal tubes of
armor-clad knights. Lady Dudley and her
eldest daughter, the handsome Honor, were
amongst the farthingales and ruffs present.
The Princess I’less, though not the Queen
of Beauty (this golden apple was reserved
for her high and mightiness of Curzon),
dazzled the male vision, riding on a charger
wreathed in crimson roses, with a haughty
train of princes and hereditary blue-blooded
grand dukes prancing on their serene saddles
in the rear. When society had admired
itsplf for some hours, it retired to dance
till the early morning milk foamed on the
Court’s back doorstep, and the early morn-
ing cat came home surreptitiously via the
scullery window from a garish night on the
chimney pot.
The Anglo-Indian, whose health wants
letting out to a temperate climate for repairs,
is said to be getting his foot on to Westralian
sod in considerable numbers. Usually he is
a desirable immigrant, with a pension, a
devilled liver, and a penchant for curried
bones and pith helmets. Something like
200,000 Westralian acres are said to be
passing in small to medium land-hunger lots
to the ex-Indian official.
When the average elderly Anglo-llelmet or
Indo-Pugr-aree (by the way, I stop here to
remark that once, in the long ago, a Hindu
university is said to have held long discus-
sion as to which was the oldest war-cry,
“Erin-go-bragh” or “Indi-go-bloo”) retires
from the Indian sun to the bleak air of, say,
a terrace house at Clapham or Bloomsbury
Square, his death rate is sudden and satis-
factory to the Pension Department. But
ex-sahib Tiffin, with the ingratitude of his
tribe, has discovered that the genial warmth
of Australia prolongs his life to an inde-
terminate old age. Instead of dying in his
native. Cold Land, he basely lives here on his
Imperial pension. Hence the sulky smile of
the Pensions Paymaster on the shores of the
Thames, near Barking and adjacent to Peck-
ham Rye. ,
The long and joyous A.D.C. Burnham
was steeped in private theatricals, till he
bounded off to golf in an adjacent State.
When he rebounds he’ll hunt round for the
name and address of the charity which “The
Importance of Being Earnest” is going to
finance. October 2 is the date. At present
two able-bodied civilians have vainly tried to
drag the inner meaning of his mumming
from the careless Burnham, who only knows
that it’s in aid of lost children, or lost ball,
or lost memory, or lost something or other.
(Lost Government House does get on its
inmates’ nerves.) As a matter of fact, the
show is for “‘The Ministering Children’s
League,” and the place is the Standard
Theatre. A.D.C. Curwen, from the Gov-
ernor Fuller kindergarten (Vic.), is
going to lend his aid to the charity show.
Miss Dorothy Luscombe, the tall and
straight, and Miss Ruth Lumsdaine, another
good-looking damsel, will also support the
Burnham play-acting. Montague Stephen
is reported to be almost professional' in his
part.
The Australian Wild Flower Show, organ-
ised by the Girls’ Realm Guild in this town,
will open its petals on September 26. The
Town Hall rafters will roof exhibits from
every State in the Commonwealth.
Curator Maiden, of the local Botanic
Garden patch, is lending his learned- aid to
the historic vegetation section of the coming
flower show. Australian floral mummies, in
good preservation (gathered by Joseph
Banks and Solander, on the primeval shores
of Botany Bay), will take an airing during
the progress of the event. ’ These ghosts of
the past were ’sent to England, arid after-
wards hunted back to their native land to be
secured under official lock and key. Leich-
hardt’s expedition to the interior will be re-
presented by some botanic wraiths with
phantom leaves. Tassy’s verdant isle sends
along a large consignment of wild flora. Port
Darwin contributes a water-color sketch or
two of its aboriginal blootiis- —a warm blaze
of tropic beauty. Queensland tourist bureau,
with a keen eye to business, is advertising
its luscious native fruits. Westralia is, hur-
riedly rushing into competition with its
amazing wild flowers. Mrs. Ellis Rowan
once stalked these gorgeous blooms with ant-
like industry. N.S.W. has already, through
her medium, a bowing acquaintance with
vivid patches of purple and yellow on beds
of dusty red earth under skies of gold and
amethyst.
The. port of Newcastle in'this State- is
beginning to clamor for a new Seaman’s
Institute. Apparently it has some cause.
The following hair-lifting description culled
from a Newcastle paper, was written in
July last by two officers of the s.s. West-
minster. “After spending weeks in a
Russian port on the confines of civilisation
(etc.)....we attended service at the Sea-
man’s Mission on Sunday evening last.” I
pass over various details of the Rev. Haire’s
cruise.on a wet Sunday in search of a con-
gregation, and boil down the climax. “We
arrived very wet, but glad to see a fine
assemblage of young rrien from the shipping in
the harbor.. . . .The rain was driving con-
tinuously through the doors and windows
and running in streams over the Institute
floor. When the tide rose higher, the floor
commenced to heave up with every sea that
rolled underneath, and fountains of water
spurted up through the holes eaten by rats/’
A harrowing description of the riotous con-
duct of the whole Seaman’s building, which
staggered up and down and round about its
moorings, and was generally so unseaworthy
it ought to have scuttled, completes this
deep-sea wail.
The R.A. Society has just added to its
show (Vickery’s Chambers) a line collection
of medallions and statuettes by a clever Aus-
tralian, named Dora Ohlfsen. This artist
and sculptress has been flitting between
Dome and I’aris for some years, and is
soon on the wing again. Her work is a
whole staircase or so above the average.
Plaster, bronze and white metal are her
materials. The faces, mostly in profile,
stand out in bold outline. They are, in the
majority of cases, foreign faces—stiff, mili-
tary gentlemen, very trim about their beards
and high in the collar, and Italian ladies
with a coronet or other high-class earmark
tucked away in the corner of their medal-
lions. A plaster relief, “The Awakening of
Australian Art,” suggests the idea that
though Art (a graceful nude with a well-
grown sun rising behind her) is rather thin,
she is lithe and full of vital energy. A shep-
herd droving his flock, a bronze medallion
of Mary Anderson and some Roman beauties,
are clever enough to be snapped up on sight
by Those Who Know. A delightful, though
rather sulky flapper, “Marthia” (Rome), is
obviously troubled about many things, but
not about her family crest, which is ram-
pant in one corner of her medallion. The
Dora Ohlfsen style is cold, sharp in detail,
and almost perfect in technique.
The Women Painters’ Show, a young
and hardy annual, will trim the R. A. Soci-
ety’s walls in October. Members are at pre-
sent swathed in working overalls, and pursue
the oily way behind wind-proof studio walls.
I mention the wind, which has been westerly
and prevalent in these parts for some days,
in italics. It has torn the female temper to
shreds by removing spring millinery off the
person of the wearer at the rate of 60 miles
an hour. Like the ancient Hebrews, we have
rent our garments. Worse still, we have
rent our best hat. Sometimes after being
blown off a sivift dog got it, and when last
seen the animal was fleeing with his mouth
full of hat arid barks.
Florence Rodway is ankle-deep in pastel
portraits of young children for the coming
exhibition of petticoat brushes. A small sub-
ject taking a breathless bite out of a piece of
toffee or other toothsome bribe, while the
artist removed her eye for one moment, was
a breezy snapshot for the camera that wasn’t
present the other afternoon. Bernice
Edwell, caged high up against the sky-line,
is hard on the trail of the miniature. The
work of some well-known Swedish women
painters may pass the Wilson’s Promontory
lighthouse in time for the October oil-and-
water rally.
Artist Blamire Young is said to have sold
£6OO worth of mist and moonlight effects at
his recent exhibition in this city. Hans
Heysen, the Adelaide man who gets a golden
sunstroke through his glowing bush idylls,
is reported to be too busy to look another
commission in the eye for at least 12 months.
Art, judging from these and other signs arid
wonders, may be said to be looking up in
this continent. The influx of Longstaff last
year, followed by the Bunny raid on the
cheque-book of the Pelf-made man, may have
excited the parochial market. The local oil-
tube is bulging out to better results, any-
how, whatever the first cause may be.
The British public is turning round to bite
the hand of one of its oldest and most ivy-
clad traditions —a fatted calf of a tradition
which grew up on the premises and played
with the children. It used to believe that
the. more venerable anything was, from a
lyric tenor or a bottle of wine to the heredi-
tary indigestion of the Tweenwayes family,
the better it was bound to be. So long as
the public favorite had enough strength in
his legs to totter to his front strip of plat-
form. the senile decay that, ate up his vocal
chords was regarded as a passing weakness.
To-day new favorites at Covent Garden are
pushed to the front by powerful patrons
every season. This year an Italian tenor,
Martinelli, was the darling of the duchesses.
McCormack, however, had conquered the
press and public. Critics who used to carp
admitted the upward leap of the burly John,
and the favorable influence of the Australian
season on his stage manner. John was
booked for “Tosea” with a young and radiant
soprano, Agostinelli (another new favorite
just chipping the applause off her first sea-
son in London), as the anguished heroine.
The typhoid germ, or whatever wild beast it
was, hit McCormack below the belt (at least
a large and ravenous-looking animal was ob-
served trying to climb on his bed, and though
it fled into the darkness when assailed with
the fireirons, some people, who got a hurried
back view of it, said it was a germ). So
Martinelli had the great opportunity.
The “Ave Maria” (with ’cello and fiddle
obligato), composed by May Summ'erbelle.
with its long-drawn “A —men” at the close,
is an acceptable addition to sacred song
portfolios.
May the Fates be good to you, my Arini,
VANDORIAN.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, September 9, 1912.
My Dear Yarrie, —
Wattle Day painted the town yellow on
Saturday and Monday. Saturday excursion-
ists raided the wattle groves close to Mel-
bourne, and straggled back to the city in
detachments clasping branches of the
national flower. On Monday, matrons in
command of squads of girls held up the com-
mercial world in the name of sweet Charity,
- the youngest sister of the three, but, accord-
ing to Scripture, much more important than
Faith and Hope. Mrs. Forrest, an ardent
lover of Australian flowers, was in the van
of the celebrations. She has a suite of
rooms at the Grand, and «o many helpers
descended on her quarters that the manage-
ment gave them a quarter of the tall build-
ing, and a side door all to themselves. Dinner
was laid in the board-room for the tired
workers.
The Royal Agricultural Society has spent
thousands since last year on its grounds,
ihe paths have been altered, and the grit
that gets into one’s eye is of a distinctly finer
quality than of old. Another large stand
has grown up, and the society promises a
sequence of new stands till, in due course, a
vast amphitheatre will form and help to
block the winds that rage over the grounds
from the north. Though laggard, hitherto,
in regard to tree-planting, something is being
done this year.. Poor little gum trees, two
or three feet high, cower inside wire-netting
cages.
New brick pavilions gladden the Agricul-
tural Show Grounds in place of the unstable
tents of past years, and the indoor exhibits
are, in consequence, better displayed. There
is a special new building expressly for show-
ing women’s work. This year it was filled
with jam jars, and glass cases of pallid-
looking cakes. That cakes were not truly
lepresented, I m sure. The average coun-
try girl can cook well, as a rule, and can
whip up a batch of scones or cakes while a
town maiden murmurs “Fancy.” Possibly
the best cooks were too busy to compete.
There was a really horrible exhibit of
needlework careful, beautiful work wasted
on hideous patterns and worse colors. It
merely illustrated the value of a live Arts
and Crafts Society.
Shop people are agitating for a general
public holiday on Show Day, so that the
man or girl who sells ribbons can have the
same freedom as the bank or office worker,
ihe shop people have only Saturday after-
noon, and reach the showground between two
and three o’clock of the last day, when ex-
hibits and animals are being prepared for a
hurried removal before the five o’clock clos-
ing.
The a^er railway accident, many
ot the Show exhibits added a partly-maimed
assistant to the picture. Men not seriously
enough hurt to warrant losing pay-money
turned up to work with decorations of crossed
plaster strips and bandages, to show where
the aged rolling stock had hit them.
T ,here was no member of the Common-
wealth Parliament at the Show luncheon, on
Thursday. Prime Minister Fisher sent a
letter of apology, which was read out at
toast time. The chairman called upon any
federal member in the gathering to respond
tor the Government. There was no response
from any corner. And no wonder! Last
year. Prime Minister Fisher, whilst speaking
forcibly, was boo-hooed by some highly-
placed but unmannerly squattocrats—a bar-
baric act when one remembers that he was
there as their guest. This year, they would
possibly have emphasised their indignation
by pelting him with chicken bones, if he
had come to remind them that they were
still surviving the ruinous results of Labor
legislation.
Mrs. Edwards and Mrs. Cumming, whose
husbands are persons of the first importance
m the Automobile Club, gave a smart tea-
party on the platform above the lines of
motor-cars. The guests came aloft, sighing
with envy of the cars they passed, and com-
forted themselves with tea and gossip. Mrs
Chester Manifold took her fruit salad and
thought out a new, closed-in car, warranted
to defy the winds of the Western District.
Mrs. Syd. Day, just back from Sydnev.
sported a tricky black and white suit; and
Mrs. Stoddart languished in ermine. Hostess
Mrs. Pvdwards wore lace and orange in a
clouding of mole-colored ninon. Mrs Cum-
ming was in blue and black. Club men
with the long-distance motor look in their
eyes, wandered aloft for tea and talk, and a
bunch of flappers flirted in and out of the
near-by cars.
The Victorian Artists’ Ball happens on
feeptember 20. Some enthusiastic artist-
women are painting a frieze for the balcony
showing early . English people on parad'e.
U is to be a not of costumes from the sth
to the 15th century.
The fine table salts of St. Kilda Yacht
Club came inland last week to the local
lown Hall, and invited the prettiest seaside
maidens to Turkey Trot and Two Step. The
girls stepped and turkeyed with enthusiasm.
-Liie club has not frolicked in this sociable
manner for years; but it has a reputation
for doing things exceedingly well. There
was no sign of vice-royalty. The present
Governors are sadly deficient in water en-
thusiasm. The bounding gee, not the
bounding wave, is their pet weakness.
Last week, the directors of the Melbourne
Steamship Go. invited a# number of people
to come and feed on the Dimboola, the gor-
geous new boat just added to their fleet. The
weather broke down in the fore legs shortly
before the party; but it did not seem to
balk the guests, who sped eagerly through
the wind and rain in cabs and motors, and
clambered up dripping, swaying gangways to
bunting-sheltered decks. David York-Syme
and Captain Roy gripoed the hands of the
brave adventurers on arrival. Mrs. York-
Syme, a sister to our own George Reid,
smiled welcome from the midst of a regal
velvet dress. Wattle blossom and hidden
electric radiators gave a very good imitation
of spring in the enclosure, and a brisk band
kept its spirits up by blowing incessantly
on the. promenade deck. Tea cheered up
the visitors, and exploring parties took obser-
vations of the shining newness of the ship.
Lily Barrett and her pal, Miss Nyulasy,
were lately entertained, unchaperoned and
alone, in the Melbourne Club—the only
ladies. (except the char-peeress) who have
been inside the sacred portals for 20 years.
On Wattle Day, after the two had shivered
valiantly on the mat for an hour, watching
the sheltered members, the secretary invited
them in and entertained them. The im-
promptu invaders bagged a donation from
Governor Fuller, and sundrv tributes in
gold and silver from the Most Expensive, on
their way through the lounge. Their pil-
grimage extended even to the lawns and ivy-
covered walls of the enclosed garden at the
back, whereof the outside world had
almost forgotten the existence. About 20
years ago, the members gave a ball and
erected a marquee in this garden ; but the
weather turned to a flood of rain and nearly
washed the tent away. The experiment was
not repeated, and the calm of the social mon-
astery had not been disturbed by feminine
voices until the Wattle Day girls burst in.
For the moment the privileged pair are the
envy of their sex. They wear the air
which corresponds to that of the plumbers
who have been attending to taps and drain-
pipes at a convent.
What is Art with a capital A? The late
Mr. R. Jenkins (of the Yic. Artists’ So-
ciety) apparently considered that the ulti-
mate aim of Art is to engender an attack of
hay fever. An inspection of his paintings at
Melbourne Athenaeum has enforced this con-
viction. The exhibition is well timed: it
was opened during Show week. The gentle
agriculturist who looked in might easily have
imagined himself back among recently-de-
serted. fields and pastures old. The great
majority of the pictures treat of agricultural
subjects, and Hay is mentioned in the titles
of seven of them. The moderate prices at
which the products of the late Mr. Jenkins
are catalogued give the exhibition some of
the charm of a bargain sale.
The big Poster Ball, in aid of the Girls’
Training Home, rolled successfully last week.
The vice-regals came in full force, and a
Gaud Mayoral party lurked in the offing.
The Maddens and Forrests were also afoot.
The young Grices and Fairbairns and the
young Ryan maiden, who has just “come
out,” helped the show along; but the Staugh-
ton widow could not appear because of the
death of Mr. Armytage. A number of other
people—principally Grices, Armytages and
Staughtons—stayed away for the same rea-
son. Mrs. Cyrus Barlow’s set won a prize
for originality. Her people were dressed
mostly in towels —200 yards of them. (I
refer, of course, to the towels. Any person
who conducted 200 yards of people to a
public dance would be pelted to death with
blanc mange, and buried beneath a cairn of
cream pull’s.)
The original members of the Decimal Club
celebrated joyously on Tuesdav, September
3. A London member of the Club, Mr. Rus-
sell Sheppard, helped in the merrymaking,
which commenced with dinner at the Ori-
ental, continued at “The Quaker Girl,” and
ended hilariously at the Savoy.
Mrs. Robert Harper had songstress Kirkby
Lunn to tea, at Myoora, on Wednesday after-
noon. . Toorak’s Very Best People assembled
to criticise and compliment the melodious
visitor. She displayed perfect amiability
and an enviable sable pelt over ebony crSpe
de Chine. A. power of visiting country
Showites admired her immensely. The
Albert Miller lady sported new draperies, and
Mrs. Hodges trailed in a spring-like silvery
setting. Daughter-in-law Henry Harper
arrived, in grey tones and bright smiles, to
second the efforts of the hostess.
The Peter Mitchells dropped into this dusty
city during Show week, with London and
New York luggage labels all round them. Peter
immediately lost himself in the Show crowds,
leaving Mrs. Peter to unpack and shake out
a brand-new library of foreign clothes, which
had been designed in Paris and built in New
York. She has a particular novelty, to wit,
pleated skirts. {Do I hear the shade of
Grandma remarking: “Tush! Novelty in-
deed! Why I wore them myself!”)
Willie Harper’s motor, dusting homeward
from the Show along the difficult and dirty
Flemington-road, got disastrously involved
with a cyclist, a tram, a fence and a front
door. (It missed a dog, a traction engine, a
parson, a fire brigade, a policeman, the solar
system and a cow, also most of the Cosmos ;
and it just grazed the Past and knocked a
mere splinter off the Future. But it hit every-
thing else real hard.) Whilst trying to avoid a
tram, the motor ran down the unfortunate
cyclist. Then the car became terrified and tried
to escape indoors. It stove in the portal, but
luckily did not injure its crew, and they re-
sumed their homeward way in a taxi.
The cyclist travelled by ambulance to a hos-
pital. And the staring householders won-
dered how the prevailing burglar was to be
foiled until such time as a carpenter repaired
the damage.
Eitel, a member of Dr. Mawson’s Ant-
arctic band, has been in Melbourne energeti-
cally gathering funds. His pretty but rather
sad and anxious-looking wife is with him,
also, a jolly, red-haired, petticoated young-
ster, who sprints all the time after Dad, and
records passing events with a camera.
The widow Chenery’s boy, Augustus, is
to leave mammy for matrimony. The girl
of his choice is Connie McKechnie, daughter
of the Wallaloo squattocrats.
. Dame Best has added twins to the popula-
tion. Knight Robert has been one of the
most energetic battlers for the Baby Bonus,
and now he has lost £lO, because the Baby
Honorarium Bill isn’t yet passed.
J. T. Chia, the dapper official sec. to the
Chinese Consul-General, employs himself
some of these bleak nights in delivering his
lantern-lecture on Old and New China at
suburban halls. Last week the Ilaw-Haw-
thorn branch of the A.N.A. gathered in the
local Town Hall to hear Chia talk of the
native land he escaped from when he was a
youth. Unlike touring foleign devils, who
go only where the population is thickest, the
Consul’s sec. represents China as a countrv
yelling for further development. It has a
“barren North,” like the Commonwealth,
and Chia quotes a Yankee authority,
who argues that the country’s 400 mil-
lion inhabitants could be multiplied by
five, and still find cabbages and breath-
ing room for all. China-night at the
Hawthorn Hall was in the nature of a racial
demonstration. The lecture had a musical
setting, the vocalist being Mrs. Quing Foo
and the pianist Miss Lucy Ming, who sported
the _ A.N.A. gold medal for instrumental
music.
Rumor seems to have definitely decided
that Mr. Chia, of the frock-coat and bell-
topper, will marry Unie Russell, not in
China, but here in our midst. She went to
Europe with her mother on three years’
leave of absence from Dad, but now, after
only six months, they are returning. The
bridegroom-elect proposes to settle here, it
is said. Or to take his blonde Unie to Eng-
land, maybe. He and his brothers , were
educated in England, and, during his subse-
quent sojourn in Pekin, he was director of
the English section of the University. His
culchaw won the maiden’s heart. Moreover,
the family is richly gilded. Chia pere, the
lekin merchant, is understood to be a mil-
lionaire, or even more so, and the young man
who darts. about Collins-street, in immaculate
attire, is in a sure-footed position to support
a wife.
Another local baritone—George Cowley—
is giving a recital, at St. Paul’s Chapter
House HaH, on the 28th. Maggie Stirling
will help the programme, also a basso and a
lady elocutionist. By the wav, a little bird
whispers a doubt as to whether these halls
attached to houses of prayer are duly licen-
sed as places of public entertainment. But
perhaps they don’t need a license. Tickets
are sold at church doors in a quite ordinary
way, and although there is no compulsory
charge for admission to ordained buildings
the collection dish answers the same purpose.
Lalla Miranda, back from Queensland,
stayed in Melbourne for some quiet visiting
amongst her old friends, and a deal of dusty
but informative motoring round the country
bhe gave a dinner party at Menzies’, on
Monday night of last week, and departed on
the Orama, on the 4th. The deck in her
vicinity was fairly heaped with wattle and
violets.
The Glaciarium closed down on Saturday
until next May arrives with a shrill whistle
of draughts. All the energies of the neigh-
boring factory will be devoted, in the interim
to manufacture the stuff that dinks in
glasses, and preserves the ancient fish from
natural decay. The “Glassy” never before
closed so regretfully, and the season ended
with a boom week. On one night, hockey
powdered the ice into a snow field, as thp
Rink and Victorian inter-State skidders sped
«oi'«?i Tuesda y was devoted to sports
calculated to attract country people.. Wednes-
day was made joyous with a fancy dress
carnival, and so on till the end of the week.

of the Scythe—has been grafting hard of late. On Mon-
day he mowed down “Pat” Auld, one of the McDouall
Stuart explorers. Deceased was born near Man-
chester rn 1840, and came to South Australia two
.lears later, when his father began business in Hind-
iey-street as a grog merchant, and commenced the
planting of the Auldana vineyard. When “Pat” left
school he entered the Surveyor-General’s office under
the late G. W. Goyder, and in 1802 he traversed the
continent under Stuart. With B. TV Finnish he did
further exploring in the Territory, and then turned
ms attention to such trivialities as the wine trade,
tne local Hunt Club and various societies and asso-
ciations. Only four of the McDouall Stuart explorers
now remain, and Mr. Auld's last public appearance
was at the McDouall Stuart jubilee banquet, about a
month ago. _ A week ago the State Government pre-
sented to him, through his son, a gold watch, in re-
ness” ’ services as a hustler in the wilder-
Another old identity was reaped at Gumeracha on
the same day, viz., Dr. J. R. Stephens. He was born
in Cornwall m 27, and came to Australia, from
Canada, in 50. Teaching and doctoring he wan-
dered through various parts of the State, till he
finally settled at Gumeracha, 20 years ago. One of
his daughters is the widow of the late Speaker
Holder.
And on Thursday, Mrs. Barlow, whose husband has
been for many years a prominent figure in legal and
educational circles, went under to the untiring: per-
son with the scythe.

POLITICAL
POINTS
A POSSIB LITY OF THE FUTURE.
Mr. D. R. Hall (Minister for Justice) mentioned
that the Discharged Prisoners’ Aid Society had made
several proposals in connection with the matter of
prison reform, and one of these was for a prison
newspaper for the benefit of confinees. — S. M. Herald.
Scene, a prison. Time, any old time ahead.
Business, election of an editor for new lag
meat rag.
Spokesman (to some mute inglorious Chidley):
"Dear brother, in conferring the editorship
of this, our valuable meat-wrapper, upon
you, we have only one request to make, i.e.,
that you keep it like Casar’s wife, beyond
reproach.”
The second week in October is to be Aus-
tralian Manufacturers’ Week. Then a special
effort will be made to fill the shop windows
of Australian cities with Australian goods,
and to convince the person who says We
Can’t Make Things Here, and the individual
who alleges that \ve Can Get Them Cheaper
From Abroad, and the party who declares
that The Time is Not Yet Ripe, that this
country isn’t necessarily born quite dead.
Sydney Labor Council recently had a
spasm of worry over the alleged dear-food
problem, and proposed that the Australian
Government should be asked, among other
things, to suspend the import duty on meat.
Presumably, it had a brilliant idea about
importing beef from London.
When the House of Reps, has nothing
better to do, it potters along at an oft-
adjourned discussion re the proposal to make
Sydney, instead of Yassberra, the Federal
capital. Now, according to the bond, the
capital and at least 100 square miles of
territory must be Federal property. Is it
proposed to hand Sydney over to the Com-
monwealth and start a new State metropolis
at DandalooV
It would seem, from a last week’s police-
court case, that there is a regular trade in
Lascar-smuggling in Sydney. The evidence,
showed that the fee charged for introducing
one of these murky undesirables to N. S. Wales
is £2O. Also, it transpired that the P. and
O. Company alone had lost from 300 to 400
colored seamen and firemen in recent years.
The Customs officers claimed that the two
citizens who played the leading roles in the
court case above mentioned were responsible
for a good proportion of these cases, and
their theory so impressed the magistrate
that he handed them fines of £lOO apeice,
the maximum under the Act. It will do to
go on with, but no punishment that has not
a dash of hard labor in it will really fit this
crime.
THE FACT OF THE MATTER.
Good arguments might be advanced for augment-
ing the outgoing on Defence, the Postal Dept., N.
Territory. Baby Bonus, etc., but can it be done out
of revenue? —D.T. and other Liberals.
The Old Girl (wildly): “Tain't that I
objects t’ yer spendin’ so much; but it's
y’r himpudent an' hindependent hattitood
to'ards a dear ole friend uv th' family's
that hannoys me, so there!"
John Bull’s commissariat lines are
being rapidly, peacefully and auto-
matically cut by the increase of the
world’s population. Replying to the
complaints that Maoriland cheese was
ousting the Canadian product on the
London market, the Kanuck Agricul-
tural Minister stated that, owing to
the rapid increase of home population,
all the- cheese manufactured was re-
quired for home consumption.
N.S.W. still sticks manfully to the
policy of dragging everything to Syd-
ney. Between 1906 and 1912 the num-
ber of electors on the Federal rolls
increased by 262,462 —144,924 in Syd-
ney and suburbs, and only 117,538 in
all the spacious country outside, an
area about half as large again as the
German Empire. It seems hardly
worth while to have a spacious coun-
try when its rulers haven’t spacious
minds to match.
Two items from Sydney Telegraph (Sep-
tember 29) :
Mr. Wade was not present in the Legislative As-
sembly last night, on account of slight indisposition.
During the discussion on the Gas Bill, the member
for Bega (Mr. Wood) came into conflict with the
Speaker, who ordered his removal from the cham-
ber.
Mr. Wade was absent through indisposition ;
Mr. Wood through disposition.
The one cashier at the Customs in Mel-
bourne is an overworked person these Rev-
enue Tariff times. His receipts for the day
are likely to be £30,000 or more, in 600 or
700 different payments, and the checking of
all those accounts is a good day’s work. Evi-
dently something will have to be done in this
little matter of Government business. It Is
a question of higher duties or more cashier.
They are an economical crowd in Victoria.
Some of the railway carriages which per-
ished in the recent accident outside Mel-
bourne were left-offs from the. old Hobson’s
Bay Railway Cos. —things a whole genera-
tion of age, and calculated to crumple up like
a walnut shell eaten by white ants. Appar-
ently Victoria means to run these ancient
vehicles right into the station that is marked
“Judgment Day.”
The wonderful Ordinance by which land in
the Territory was to be let on perpetual
lease with a liability to have the rent raised
almost any day has already burst, and some-
thing new is to be substituted. The original
folly was practically born dead. The most
hopeless feature of it was the proviso that
town lands should not only be re-appraised
every 14 years, but that they should be
liable to re-appraisement at all manner of
intermediate times whenever any public
work raised the value of the soil. To this
there were obvious objections. The estab-
lishment of railway workshops, for instance,
alongside a high-class drapery might en-
hance the value of the land and raise the
rent, yet do great damage to the drapery
business. The arrangement looked too much
like a tenancy at the landlord’s discretion.
The pastoralist might not mind the insta-
bility of things quite so much. If the rent
became impossible, he could drive away a
very large proportion at least of his im-
provements on their own four hoofs. But a
six-storey business blopk is a fixture, and
to put it on top of a bit of land that was
liable to rise up at any moment and hit
the lessee in the eye, so to speak, would be
impossible. In fact, the proposed method of
dealing with N. Territory towns was calcu-
lated to make it dead certain that there
would never be any.
“Trotter” : The monarchs
of England occupy niches in
Canterbury Cathedral. It is
not exactly known when
these effigies began, but only
four niches were left after
William IV. had been
propped up. Queen Victoria
refused to go up,, because, as
anyone may imagine, there
was a legend that the mon-
archy would only last till
the niches were filled. Her
successor did likewise —so
there are the four blank
spaces. The question is
whether their being empty
makes any difference. The
stautes of Brutus and Cas-
sius were more apparent in
the Roman Forum through
being absent.
ONE RACE—ONE LOAN.
[For The Bulletin.]
“We are all one race,” said Mr. Steinberg, with
a smile. 1 —Sydney Evening News item.
Rejoice, ye patriotic scribes —
Australia is becoming known!
United with the Found Two Tribes —
One Race—one IOU—one Loan !
N.s.w. O.C.C.
The Turkish element must have annoyed
all patriotic ice-cream vendors immensely by
the big splash it made recently at Adelaide.
For several years it lias had an “Ottoman
dinner,” but this time it added the unveil-
ing of a picture of the late Federal Minister
Batchelor, who helped a lot of Syrians to
get naturalisation here. Andrew Fisher
went over to perform the ceremony, and
there was a tremendous after-dinner toast-
list, with Governor Bosanquet, Vice-presi-
dent McGregor, Parson Bevan, Butler
(C.P.W.), and Editor Sowden among the
speakers. The appropriate sentiment, “The
Ottoman never will be sat upon!” was unac-
countably omitted.
FARE SCALING IN GENOA.
In an encounter between three men who refused to
pay tram fares at Genoa, and the police and tram
officials, two policemen, one fugitive and the con-
ductor were shot dead.
Perci Da'counti da' Jump saves his fare again.
Apropos of the recent Federal squabble
re day labor, it is just 50 years since Queens-
land gave up that particular system in favor
of contract methods. The latter continued
in vogue until 1902, when financial exigencies
compelled the Philp Government to cut down
expenditure and curtail the profits made by
private contractors. The late John Leahy,
a Private Enterprise apostle to the core, was
the man who re-introduced day-labor into
Bananaland. And it worked well while
John had his eye on it. Yet though John
was a good sort of business man, his eye
wasn’t an unprecedented organ. It was
just a plain ordinary eye.

South Aus. has gaily authorised another
£2,000,000 worth or so of railways and sun-
dries to be built out of loans. So Cohen,
the weary Titan with the hooked nose, is
expected to shoulder the burden again.
The Argus says of a recent Women’s Na-
tional League meeting that the speakers were
all “of the feminine gander.” A goose con-
vention.
“Lex” writes
Notice has been given by a N.S.W. Labor member
of a Bill to restore E. D. Meagher to his old job as
a solicitor and proctor of the Supreme Court.
Meagher was struck off by the Court (rightly or
wrongly) just as many other Gentlemen One have
been. If he alone is restored by Act of Parliament,
while the cases of other men are not even inquired
into, it can only be because he is a Labor member
and his party is in power. There is surely a weak
point in the logic somewhere!
S.A. Works Minister Butler has declared
in a casual wav that under the present Gov-
ernment navvies do 20 per cent, more work
than they did when Verran was Premier,
and the Verran party foams considerably and
demands evidence, which isn’t forthcoming.
Still, there are possibilities in the case.
Verran’s ganger was driving a Government
supporter,, who could always withdraw his
support if requested to shovel an excess of
dirt in a given time, The present Govern-
ment is mostly driving an Opposition sup-
porter, and having no hope of getting his
vote it doesn’t care how much dirt he
shovels, provided he doesn’t shovel too little
dirt.
The absurdity of Party Government looks
as if it were at last beginning to be recog-
nised. In N.S-W. it was necessary to invent
Speaker Willis to save the situation. In
Tasmania one man holds absolutely the bal-
ance of power. In Maoriland things are so
divided that any hour may bring forth one
of those political crises which, when they
arise, make a big hole in the public pocket.
The most economical sort of general election
costs the general public in hard cash more
than on an average it pays for a Sunday din-
ner, while the temporary dislocation of bus-
iness must indirectly add enormously to the
bill. Yet, while Party Government remains
there is always a general election in sight,
and J. Smith may at any time, and to serve
no earthly good purpose, be asked to plump
down his hard earned cash to run a show
which at best isn’t equal to the poorest
sort of flickergram. And the cash cost of
frequent and useless elections isn’t the worst
feature of Party Government. The facts of
the case were brought out strongly by
N.S.W. Minister Beeby, in his recent speech
at Nyngan, when he declared that —“So
acute has become the division in party poli-
tics to-day that in cases where a non-party
vote would record 90 per cent, in favor of a
certain thing, because there happens to be
a party system, one party, while believing in
the thing the other party is advocating, re-
gards it as an accepted duty nevertheless to
prevent it being done, for the simple reason
that the other party has brought it forward.”
If the Holman-McGowen crowd was cap-
able of rising to the height of its plain public
duty, the fact that one of its number has dis-
covered the comic-opera nature of the game
now being played ought to offer good pro-
mise of reform. Labor parties used to talk
about Elective Executives and a Referendum
with Initiative while they were in opposi-
tion, but their memories have a way of fail-
ing when once they reach office.
THE CYCLONE.
[FO RTHE BULLETIN.]
Mr, R. O. Blackwood, president of the Vic. Em-
ployers’ Federation, in his address at the annual
meeting, went so far as to say that we are nearing
the edge of an industrial cyclone, which must bring
about general devastation. One particular section
Of the body politic seems determined to destroy the
others. If he were in a position to advise the
workers, he would urge that they should not make
the conditions so hard that Brains and Capital will
be forced to go elsewhere. —News item.
The cyclone that is coming,
Saith the Profit fat and hearty,
Will bescatter the illusions
Of the Socialistic Party.
When he talketli in a high tone
Of the vague, uncertain cyclone
Our eyeballs bulge with horror and our flesh begins
to. creep ;
When he thumps the Tory tabor
And predicts the doom of Labor
We can feel ourselves already lying prostrate in a
heap.
Brains and Capital will leave us,
Stewing slowly in our graA'y;
All our industries will perish,
And our means to build a Navy
Will be swept away and sky blown
By the fury of the cyclone
Which is coming to this country as a judgment from
the Lord ;
Yet it might have been prevented,
If the workers had consented
To be governed by the wisdom of a gentle Wages
Board.
No tongue can tell exactly
Just the date of that to-morrow
When the hungry working classes,
Crushed by unavailing sorrow,
Will be talking in a dry tone
Of the devastating cyclone
That swept the fields of Labor and brought Unions
all to smash ;
But the dismal time is coming
When the Industries now humming
Will be finally abandoned by their parents, Brains
and Cash.
Then, like orphans on a doorstep,
They will stare at one another —
Yea, like little ones bereaved
Of their father and their mother;
And the hopes so vain and high-flown,
Of the days before the cyclone,
Will haunt them whilst they babble bitter curses on
the names
Of the fatal, old-time leaders,
Pertinacious mischief breeders,
Who wrought ruin to the workers by their Socialistic
Aims.
VICTORIA,
O. K.

SPORTING
NOITIONS
At time of writing, the life of Australia’s
clever and daring aviator, Hart, of Parramatta, is
hanging by a thread. He was making one ot a
series of trial flights with anew type of mono-
plane designed and built by Mr. D. K. Sandiord
and himself. It flew' beautifully, and they were
both delighted with the model, which promised a
speed of over 70 miles an hour with ease. The
engine was not new, it was the same well-proved
and trusty Gnome that he had used on all his other
aerial craft. On Wednesday evening last week he
was returning from a successful flight, and was
about 200 ft. from the ground when the engine seemed
to stop. The machine gave a dangerous dip, and
Hart apparently tried to use the planes, but failed.
Then tiie aeroplane plunged madly, head foremost
for the ground. It was thought at first that he must
be dead, for the front of the aeroplane w'as buried
in the earth with the force of that terrific dive. But,
fortunately, he was not. Both legs were broken, his
skull was fractured, and he was covered with cuts
and w'ounds. Considering the ghastly nature of his
injuries, it is a marvel that he is alive, but he
made excellent progress, and though his condition
is most serious, there are strong hopes that he will
pull through. Yet if he does recover, he will
almost assuredly be lame, and in all probability his
flying days are over. A man whose skull has been
fractured must confine his aviation to the theoretical
and terrestrial sides of the business.
If anybody has experienced any difficulty
in finding Mclntosh of late it is not owing to rush
to the head over his latest and greatest plunge. The
solemn truth is that he is hiding, with most of the
Australian and foreign teams for the great tug-of-
war. The trouble is that the rest of the teams have
objected to the Parramatta Pygmies, and the sport-
ing firmament is full of angry Heckenberg. Nine
international teams went on strike when they heard
the Pygmies were going to put the family into the
tussle. They held a solemn meeting, and sent a
most emphatic and strongly worded “NO,” but never
a word of “Why Not.” When this was made public
50ft. of Heckenberg leaned over Sydney and hollered:
“Whaffor?” And then old Parramatta awoke from
the dead, and with an alderman and an ex-Mayor
striding magnificently in the van, swooped down on
Mclntosh, with the Parramatta Pygmies in the rear ;
and all day long the noise of battle roared amongst
the joists of the Stadium’s new roof and fled city-
wards on the wintry wind. So Mclntosh and his
minions fled and hid themselves; and nothing but a
ruling from Willis, the Mighty One, the Dread, will
bring them back from the vasty deep.
“Bloggs” : It would be interesting to know
the point of view of referee Scott, of the Stadium,
as regards fouls. Does he consider them meritorious
actions? Or does he think that a little fouling now
and then is good for the boxing game? Or does he
consider that every pug should be allowed his first
foul —not to mention ms second and third —in every
contest that jie wages ? All these hypotheses are
arguable on the form displayed _b.y Scott in the last
three fights witnessed by this writer, at the Stadium.
In the jxeileher-O’Donnell scrap the Yank found him-
self losing. He accordingly, with the utmost delib-
eration, smote O’Donnell below the belt, the U.S.
theory being that it is more honorable, as well as
less painful, to lose on a foul than a knock-out.
O’Donnell yelled with anguish; the referee cau-
tioned the offender; the mill proceeded. In the
next round Kelleher fouled his adversary twice more,
and with obtrusive frankness at that. On his smiting
below the belt a fourth time, Scott did what he
ought, by the rules, to have done a round earlier,
and wiped Kelleher out. In the McCoy-Til fight, one
may fairly say that Til’s chances of winning were
rendered hopeless by nothing more nor less than his
opponent’s foul tactics. His eye was laid open by
McCoy’s head at an early stage, and he was roughed
illegally right through the disturbance. Jack Read
is ordinarily a fair fighter. Encouraged, possibly,
by the license allowed in the foregoing contests (a
license, by the way, that Snowy Baker would never
have tolerated), he mishandled Hock Iveys, last
Saturday night,- in an outrageous fashion. After
Keys had been hit low three times, the following
occurred (vide S. M. Herald, 9/9/’l2) :
“Read’s corner sent him in to make up leeway.
He started roughing, and in the breakaway struck
Keys low in the groin. The veteran bent beneath
the blow, and then, straightening himself up, with an
effort he went after Read. Keys never seemed to get
his full strength back again, and weakened gradually
as the round progressed.”
Yet, at the end of a match in which, by the ad-
mission of a vast majority of the local sporting
writers, and practically the whole of the Stadium
audience, Keys outpointed Read in all but three or
four rounds, referee Scott indicated the latter as
winner. The question arises: Do fouls count for
points to the fouler in Scott’s opinion? To the
writer it seems that the opposite should be the rule.
If I couldn’t bring myself to do the right thing, and
starkly disqualify a boxer the moment he earned
such punishment, under the laws of boxing, I would,
at least, register against him as manv points for
each illegal action, as though his adversary had
scored a knock-down. The present laxity which re-
sults in a fair, chivalrous boxer, like Keys, losing
to one who has fought him foully is going, if it is
persisted in, to afflict Australia with the manners and
customs of the U.S. prize ring. And once that hap-
pens, the Wowser will pass local boxing the slumber-
stroke amid general applause.
* * * *
Referee Arthur Scott made a mess of
things again at Sydney Stadium when he gave Read
the verdict over the elusive and polished Ilock Keys.
For fully 14 rounds Keys won handsomely; but as
everybody knows, Keys is far from robust and lacks
stamina. Towards the end he tired visibly, and the
stronger Read did get some of his own back. But
the old master of Australian boxers made him look a
perfect novice, hitting him when, where and just how
he darned well pleased. Read, aware that as a boxer
he had no chance, tried roughing tactics, and well
earned the warnings he got. The wonder is he
didn't get more than warnings. Of course, had it
been a fight to a finish Read would have eventually
won through sheer attrition of the enemy; but it was
not a fight to a finish. It was a scrap of 20 rounds,
for 14 of which Keys absolutely outpointed his
younger and stronger rival, only to find that in the
opinion of the referee the six last rounds in which
he did not have altogether the best of it made the other
14 kick the beam. The referee’s billet is a putrid
position to hold. It’s hard to watch for breaches of
the rules and keep a tally of the points he mentally
awards each bruiser, and have the account all made
up ready to deliver to the fighters and the public
immediately the last gong goes. It is no wonder
referees do make mistakes, but rather that they don’t
make more. But. there seems to be a general con-
census among fight fans and scribes that Mr. Scott
made an out-size bloomer on this occasion.
The brief, brilliant, sudden-death rush in
and around Sydney for the Rugby League’s City Cup
has already reached the final stage, and the decisive
battle will be played off next Saturday, between the
two unbeaten teams, Glebe and South Sydney, at the
Agricultural Ground. The two teams qualified for
the final when South Sydney overwhelmed North
Sydney by 16 points to 11, and Glebe downed Bal-
main by 11 points to three. Curiously enough, the
Glebe-Balmain match was regarded as such a fore-
gone conclusion that it drew only about 6000 people,
while over 10,000 rolled in to the Sports Ground to
see South and North Sydney decide which should go
overboard in a game which popular fancy regarded
as anybody’s. Asa matter of fact, North Sydney
put up a better losing game than Balmain, or ooutn
Sydney put up a worse winning one than Glebe —
whichever way the 'ball follower likes to take it. The
Glebe-Balmain struggle started with a see saw action
out of which Glebe generally came off best, and, at
any rate, always succeeded in preventing its enemy
scoring. So the first half ended in favor of Glebe
with five points, while Balmain had not scored. After
the resumption Glebe put on three more and led by
eight to nil. Things looked hopeless, and then more
hopeless still, when Glebe soared to 11 without Bal-
main breaking its egg. It was not till the shadows
were lengthening that Balmain made its first real
effort, and went howling through the ranks of Glebe
as though they were men of straw, and scored a try.
The kick for goal was a failure. Going again, Bal-
main secured and let loose another passing charge
that seemed to have the Glebe defence flabbergasted—•
and then the whistle went, and Balmain was out of
the City Cup by 11 points to three. South Sydney
accomplished what Balmain failed to and hauled
the game out of the fire at the tail end of things.
South started well, and rolled over the Northmen
till its score stood eight points to North’s two.
Then North, which, with the wind in its favor, had
failed to do anything to write home about, came up,
like a giant full of tonic, to face a gale that blew
right down its neck. Before long North was leading
with nine points to eight, and the flabbergasted
South began to wilt visibly. Hereabouts the cheery,
not to say tremendous, remarks of the captain had
the same effect on the team as cuss words on bul-
locks, and the Southern men gave a great heave, and
tried to drag the bogged game out of the mire.
Some of these football captains have a fine flow of
rhetoric. But North continued to progress, and lifted
the score to 11, with South still stuck at eight.
Here the South’s captain made an extra effort, and
tore the heavens like a garment with his shrieks of
encouragement. The South team gave an extra heave
and the dray began to move. Presently South was
leading with 13 to 11. Another desperate charge
through the shadows, just as the hand trembled on
to the hour, a try secured, and a hurried shot at
goal which missed; and South had won with 16
points to 11.
* * * *
The final of the Rugby Union competi-
tion between Western Suburbs and the runner-up,
Glebe, saw the Western crowd lose its collective
head. It had Glebe beaten again and again, but
over-eagerness caused it to kick too hard and with
bad aim. It charged wild ; it lost its judgment and
mulled its defence by clawing frantically when it
should have, been polished and serene and looked
carefully before it passed. Still, for quite a long
while, the ferocious hunger of its front division for
the leather fairly smothered the Glebe back machine,
and reduced it to impotence and gibber. Glebe,
having won the toss, took advantage of the wind
that was raging; but all the same did not make
much apparent headway. It was a whipped team if
the Western crowd could only recover its thinking
apparatus. West couldn’t, so it half-time Fate
wrote the score Glebe three to nil. It was a magni-
ficent piece of work scoring that try. A charge, a
punt, a recovery, and then a two-man pass. When
Western Suburbs got the wind behind them the hearts
of the Glebe barrackers sank. But Glebe played better
than ever, and made some fine dribbling rushes. West-
ern Suburbs saved the situation several times by forc-
ing, and then Glebe secured another try, and stood six
to nil. It was close on time when a lucky accident
gave West a try, which was converted. This put
fresh hope into the Western heart, and valiant
attempts were made at field goals, any one of which
would have saved the situation; but they failed.
The whistle blew on Glebe as the premier team for
1912 by six to five.
* * * *
The wild struggle of the Soccer ’haliers
for the Gardiner Cup is drawing to its close. Only Bal-
main A and Sydney A remain in, and by Saturday
night one of them will be dead. These two knocked out
Cessnock and Garden Island respectively on Saturday
last, and cleared the field for their Armageddon,
from which the conqueror will emerge champion of
the State. After that there will remain only the
Association and Rawson Cups to be decided. Owing
to the Gardiner Cup semi-finals there were no matches
in the other two Soccer competitions on Saturday.
Balmain’s stamping of Cessnock was severe and de-
cisive, but not brutal, although the actual play was
considerably marred by rough work, which resulted
in one man, Marshall, of Cessnock, being carried off
with a broken leg, and two men being pitched off
for mistaking the game. They took it to be Red Indian
instead of Soccer. Cessnock had all the worst of the
opening, playing in the teeth of a stiff breeze. But
it made no difference when ends were changed, and
Balmain won by four goals to nil. The tussle be-
tween Sydney and Garden Island was a nearer thing,
for although the Island could not score, it managed
to prevent Sydney getting home more than twice.
Although not so wild and woolly as the Balmain-
Cessnock riot, it was as willing as.anyone could wish;
and was played with a disregard for the conventions
that would have made even Chidley blush about the
leCT V *
The Sydney Messengers, it seems, are not
the only goal kickers in Australia. Dr. A. C. Wilton,
of Adelaide University Club, holds the S.A. record
this season, with 49 kicked to his own cheek. A
very creditable performance.
* * * *
A beantifnl crowd, tallied at 40.133, of
whom 24,451 “parted” £1540 3s. 6d., witnessed
the downfall of Geelong and the recrudescence of
Carlton on Saturday, in the Vic. League’s first semi-
final, on the Metropolitan Cricket Ground, Melbourne.
The episode promised a joyful and exciting con-
clusion, but in the second quarter driving showers
of rain and hail swept away all hones of first-class
play. The first quarter ended evenly enough—Carl-
ton 21 to Geelong’s 16 points—and in the second
term the Geelongites put in a sustained attack that
should have won the game outright; but erratic
kicking, somewhat excused by the disconcerting rain,
gave them only nine behinds, in as many minutes,
when they should have had at least six more goals.
That was, in effect, the Seasiders’ last effort, for
Carlton perceived that it had miraculously escaped
a great disaster, and thereafter bogged in with suffi-
cient vigor to notch point for point, while in the
last term the Old Blues put on goal after goal with
such apparent ease and precision that the final score
—Carlton 71, Geelong 43 points—by no means dis-
closes the relative strength of the teams. Briefly,
Geelong failed because it had no system and
because it couldn’t kick, the lofty punts of
its “backs” being pleasing to inspect, but
of as little use to the side as the erratic shots of its
forwards. Carlton, on the other hand, has but little
more organisation than Geelong, and its ’bailers lack
vim, but its forwards know how, to make the best
use of their chances, and its backs understood the
art of clearing their goal effectively.
On the North Melbourne ground. North
and ’Wick, re-playing their indecisive semi-final of
two weeks before, perpetrated a feat unique in noot-
ball records, as far as this writer knows, and which
sent the barrackers wild with delirium Wicks
team was exactly the one that “drew on the tormei
occasion ; North made two changes. I* rom the start
North took the lead, and by half-time had an ad-
vantage of 14 points; but after the interval Wick
set to in earnest, and steadily accumulated goals
and behinds, till, at lemon time, it had five goals 11
behinds to North’s five goals nine behinds. it
wasn’t enough to swear by, and when, on resuming,
North gained a goal, ’Wick had to bog into the
battle afresh. It got a goal and a behind, then, with
a three-point lead, set down to stalling off North s
attacks. But North couldn’t be successfully stalled ;
it broke through three times, and though it couldn t
commandeer the earnestly-desired goal, it got the
necessary three points to draw once more —and then
the bell rang. Scores: Six goals 12 behinds all, as
compared with six goals nine behinds at the first
draw. There are rumors of a protest, one of ’Wick s
behinds being claimed as a goal; but the Ass. will
probably ignore the matter and order the boggers to
rush to battle once more. There are still two Satur-
days to spare before the cricketers seize the ground,
and as last week's 20,000 crowd paid out £268, the
Ass. will view the third trial of strength with a
friendly eye.

The N.S.W. Rugby League will, after all
the skite about it, have to face only a deficit of
£lB5 6s. Bd. (wonder what that lawyer charged the
6s. Bd. for) to make up on the cost of the wander-
ing Kangaroos least year. Of the expenditure of
£6597, fares (12300) and “allowances’’ to players
( £2108) swallowed the bulk of the boodle. The
League has immense moral satisfaction, though, _in
the thought that the deficiency was caused by paying
the team a decent allowance. And verily, I say unto
you, that the howl of a footballer’s wife in the
night, complaining of insufficient allowance, is
mightier than a bad season to bust a league’s mana
with the public. The League has played the better
part, for of a truth £lB5 6s. Bd. is a bagatelle, and
not much of that.
* * * *
The Victorian Cricket Association scored
heavily last Saturday when, without warning, its
emissaries appeared at the Melbourne Cricket Ground
when the football semi-final was in full blast, and
distributed 25,000 copies of a pamphlet entitled “The
1912 Australian Eleven in England. A Few Facts
for Fair Minded Sportsmen.” The other side soon
got wind of the nature of the publication which was
being distributed, and frantic efforts were made to
stop it, some remarkable persons appealing to the
police and park rangers to heave the distributors
out. The pamphlet is brief and to the point. It
shows, for instance, that of the nine matches played
before the deluge set in the Australian XI. won
six, lost one and drew two. Which compares with
the doings of preceding Xl.’s as follows: —
Year. Won. Lost. Drawn.
1912 ..6 1 2
1909 2 3 4
1905 5 1 3
1902 .. 4 1 4
Comment would be superfluous.
The results of the Test matches in England in 1012
are as follows:
Batting Bowling
Won. Lost. Drawn. Average. Average.
England ..4 0 2 25.19 14.26
Australia .. 2 1 3 27.66 23.65
S’Africa ..0 5 1 17.21 30r17
And yet that marvellous organ of public opinion, the
Age, said : “The batting averages make poor ‘reading’
(they were only the highest of the lot), and the
bowiers have proved very uneven.” (Their average
was better than Africa’s.) Some of the individual
performances are shown to beat the band. Bardsley
is the first Australian to score 2000 runs on two
occasions in England. Macartney scored his 1000
runs in record time. Matthews is the only bowler
in the world who has achieved the “hat trick” twice
in one test match. And Bardsley’s batting average
is the highest ever secured by an Australian willow
wielder. It is sad to see a paper like the Age de-
scend to hawking stale fish and trying to push trade
by misrepresentation.
* * * *
G. R. Hazlitt, one of the best bowlers and
most startling fieldsman in the Australian XI., will
on his return from England remove to Adelaide,
where he will be a master at St. Peter’s College.
He turned 30 the other day, but is only seven years
old at cricket. He was picked as a bowler
against Jones’s team in 1907. He failed with the
ball, but otherwise covered himself with glory in
his first Test, when he dislocated his thumb in a
marvellous one-hand catch in the slips. When Aus-
tralia wanted over 70 to win Hazlitt, with a not-
out innings of 30, did a lot towards it. When he
left he was a master at King’s School, Parramatta,
and a mighty tower of strength to Central Cumber-
land. He will not be available for the South Aus-
tralian XI .till after a few months residence, but as
it is the law it must be obeyed. A son of veteran
actor Hazlitt, he is naturally a rover.

wound up the list of matches that really

class countv), and “An” England Eleven. Concern-

Minnett played only in the final match, and bowled

the attack in all three matches, and the Greasy
Leather medal goes to him, while Macartney dons
the dripping willow wreath. As Minnett was laid
up earlier in the tour with strained thigh muscles,
he shall have a soft cushion. In the general averages
for matches of any importance (against England,
S’Africa, first-class counties and the M.C.C.), Macart-

though all did well some time or other. Macartney

18.10. Then come the two who did all the hardest

At Caulfield last Saturday, a few of the
unready cracks of the Cup betting market had an
outing in the Memsie Stakes (nine furlongs), a w.f.a.
event which is worth £4OO to the winner. The
Parisian, Bobadea and Broadsword went out un-
backed, and ran accordingly, which suggests that
there is no fear of either of them being overwound-
up for their big engagements. Wolawa and the im-
ported Eudorus appeared to be genuinely fancied, and
may have disappointed their trainers The Derby
colt, though backed down to two to one, was beaten
by Captain White and Rathlea, finishing not many
inches ahead of Eudorus. The winner’s brilliant
performance gives him a chance for Caulfield Cup,
presuming that he can stay the distance; and, in
any case, the Captain should be a reliable trial
horse for his stalwart stable companion, Uncle Sam.
Sydney lacrosse semi-finals for premier-
ship honors were apples of Sodom for Eastern
Suburbs, which, through the season, had only once
smelt defeat in 16 matches. The chance of Sydney
Club against the Suburbs was snuffe'd out—even as
the King-street girl snuffs out the attentions of the
horny-handed wharf-laborer. All of which goaded
Sydney to frenzy. So the men who are fined if they
throw a tram-ticket or a bumper on the pave tucked
their ears into their sweaters and bogged in mightily,
and tore the greatness from Eastern Suburbs by
seven goals to five. In the other section of the
semi-final realm, Petersham romped over Balmain by
10 goals to four. So Sydney and Petersham will
take charge of the earth next Saturday for the glory
of the onion-net pastime.
Leichhardt and University clashed in the
final for Sydney Baseball premiership. The students
were alleged to have forsaken all other studies for
the week in favor of glorified rounders. But evi-
dently baseball is more than a theory, and the worthy
burghers of Leichhardt arose and chewed up the
budding lawyers and immature surgeons by 13 runs
to six in a nine-innings game. It was a very convinc-
ing judgment—the sort from which there is no
appeal.
* * * *
The race follower who likes to take
his and her pleasure amidst garden-lilce sur-
roundings is reminded that the Victoria Park Racing
Club’s meeting will transpire on Wednesday (18th).
The course is in perfect order, and, as everybody
knows, Victoria Park is the garden of N.S.W. ; a
green, flower-strewn paradise amidst the drifting
sand of the desert of Zetland. It is always beautiful,
but now in its spring verdure of young grass and
fresh flowers, it is superb. Owners and trainers are
informed that entries close on the stroke of five this
afternoon (Thursday), and the weights will be.
declared at 6 p.m. on Saturday, which will give the
scientific punter plentv of time to practise the baked
roll method of divination. The programme holds
seven races and the added money is £500.*
* * * *
The 18 cyclists who faced the howling
breeze with icebergs on its bosom (one couldn’t
honestly call it a zephyr), and the roadway of float-
ing dust in the N.S.W. Cyclists’ Union individual
road championship at Homebush, Sydney, on Satur-
day, earned a leg-in for the next set of
V.C. medals which may be imported. At the end
of 19 miles the strong head wind had caused nine
weary ones to fall by the roadside. At 30 miles only-
three strong whirlers in their agony were sticking to
the game and showing their teeth against the wind.
The final rush was a struggle between C. Hooper
and S. Edworthy (last year’s winner). Hooper won
with a few lengths to spare. D. Kirk, the only other
plugger to weather the gale, filled third place about
100yds. to rearward.
Time has moved quickly in the motor
manufacturing trade. An interesting antique exhibit
at the Melbourne motor show last week was a trio
of buggy-cars and a petrolised tricycle, dating back
to 1900, or thereabouts. The Vacuum Oil Cos. had
collected these curios. Dr. Syme, of Lilydale, brought
out the tricycle. Thomson, of'Armadale (Vic.),
built one of the four-wheelers, and drove it from
Sydney to Melbourne a dozen years ago, and Dr.
Syme again was the exploiter of a four-cylinder
Mors car in 1900.

“Messenger”: The other day I listened
furiously while the deputation from the
Council of Churches protested to Prime Min-
ister Fisher against the so-called “bonus for
babies” being granted to mothers of illegiti-
mate infants, on the ground that it would
“encourage immorality.” Are these church-
men really so simple as to suppose that any
woman will undergo the pain and danger of
maternity for the sake of a fiver? And do
they really imagine that girls will yield
more readily and frequently through the
knowledge that, if the infant arrives, they
will collect one hundred shillings wherewith
to bid the “respectable” world defiance? I
guess that half the Church feeds regularly
on thistles, and that the curate with the
cold in his head was quite right when he
cried out “Let us bray.”
“Chasuble”: The Sydney Church of Eng-
land Synod, in common with all the others
of Australia, has again been dinging it in
about the shortage of clerics. Here is the sim-
ple remedy. Announce that the Archbishoprics
of Sydney, Melbourne, Brisbane, Adelaide
and all the fat bishoprics and church livings,
will be strictly reserved for Australians.
Then the clergy won’t care if they start on
poor wages, for there will be possibilities
ahead. Young men will come forward,
as do the barristers and medicoes. What a
roar there would be if the Chief Jus-
tices, or any kind of Justices, in Australia
were fetched from England! And if the
office boy understood that he would never
be more than an office boy, what inducement
would he have to lick the stamps with en-
thusiasm and conscientiousness?


WAS A COMPLETE CRIPPLE.
Mr. Charles Lincoln, of Richmond (Tas.), was a
complete cripple with muscular rheumatism, and
could not get about. tie started using Chamber-
lain’s Pain Balm, and after a few applications the
pains were easier and soon left him altogether. He
is now well, is 52 years of age, and earning his liv-
ing as a general laborer. We hope that sufferers
from rheumatism will not need further evidence in
regard to this liniment *
