ROBERT WINGAD, DECEASED.
P URSUANT to an Order of the Chancery Division
1 of the High Court of Justice made in the Mattel
of the Estate of ROBERT WINGAD, deceased. And
in an action HOPKINSON and another against
WINGAD and others (1912, W. No. 1713), and dated
the 15th day of July, 1912. WHEREBY IT IS
ORDERED that, the following inquiries be made:—-
1. Whether David Wingad, one of the sons of the
above-named Robert Wingad, deceased, is living or
dead, and if dead when he died.
2. What children the said David Wingad has had
and when they respectively were born, and whether
any and which of them have died, and, if so, when
and who are the legal personal representatives of such
of them (if any) as died after attaining the age of
21 years.
3. Whether any and which of the children of the
said David Wingad have in any and what way
assigned, mortgaged or otherwise dealt with then-
respective shares in the sum of £l4OO by the Will
of the said Robert Wingad, deceased, bequeathed unto
and _ equally between all the children of the said
David Wingad who had attained or should attain the
age of 21 years.
NOTICE IS HEREBY GIVEN that all persons
claiming to be entitled to the said legacy of £l4OO
as children of the said David Wingad or otherwise
under the said inquiries, are personally or by their
Solicitors on or before the 30th day of November,
1912, to come in and enter their names and addresses
in the book kept for that purpose, and prove their
claims at the Chambers of Mr. Justice Swinfen Eady
and Mr. Justice Neville, Royal Courts of Justice,
Strand, London, England, or in default thereof they
will be peremptorily excluded from the benefit of the
tsaid Order.
Thursday, the 12th day of December, 1912, at
11.30 of the clock in the forenoon, at the said
Chambers (Room No. 704), is appointed for hearing
and adjudicating upon the claims.
Dated this 26th day of July, 1912.
J. C. FOX, Master.
Note.—lf David Wingad, the son of the above-
named Robert Wingad, be still living, he is requested
to communicate with Mr. R. J. Gribble, Solicitor,
Furnival’s Chambers, Lvdiard Street, Ballarat, Vic-
toria, Australia; and if the said David Wingad be
dead, anyone giving to the said R. J. Gribble, at the
above address, such information as will enable him to
obtain a certificate of the death of the said David
Wingad will receive One pound ( £1) reward.
It is alleged that the said David Wingad was a
carpenter, and resided at Ballarat, Victoria, Aus-
tralia, and that he disappeared from his home in or
about the year 1898, and has not since been heard of.
STEVENS, SON & PARIvES,
22 Bedford Row, London, England,
Solicitors for the Plaintiffs.

GUEST.
DITRSUANT to an Order of the High Court of
1 Justice, Chancery Division, made in the matter
of Re ANN HAINES, Deceased, PEACEY v.
ROBERTS (1910, H. 2483), whereby the following
inquiry was directed, namely:—
Who are the persons entitled by virtue of or ac-
cording to the Statutes of Distribution to the estate
of the intestate Ann Haines, Deceased, living at the
time of her death, and whether any of them are
since dead, and, if so, who are their respective legal
personal representatives?
NOTICE IS HEREBY GIVEN, that William
Haines and William Roberts, the Younger, and all
persons claiming under the intestacy of the said Ann
Haines, and the children (if any) of Jane Splain
(n£e Haines), if she died before the 22nd March,
1909, or the issue or legal personal representatives
of the said William Haines and William Roberts, if
they or either of them died after the 22nd March,
1909, and the brothers or sisters of Ann Haines
(formerly Guest), or their children, if living on the

benefit of the said order.

of the clock in the afternoon at the said Chambers

claims.
Dated the 26th day of July, 1912.
A. KEEN, Master.
PEACOCK and GODDARD, 3 South Square,
Gray’s Inn, London, W.C., for Rickerby and
Son, Cheltenham.

died a spinster on the 22nd March, 1909.

Haines and Mary (Cove), his wife, of Cirencester,
Gloucester, and is believed to have married —•
Splain, a schoolmaster, of Minety, Wilts, and to
have gone to Ireland, and is believed to be dead.

Haines and Ann (Guest), his wife, of Cirencester,
Gloucester, and went to the United States of America
in 1851, and was in Ohio in the years 1861 or 1862,
since when he has not been heard of.

ried to James Haines at Circencester, Gloucester, in
the year 1825.

Roberts and Ann (Haines), his wife, and was born
at Charlton Kings, Cheltenham, in the county of
Gloucester. He went to New South Wales about 40
years ago. sinee when he has not been heard of.
“Prop-tick’’: It was smart work to get “Ben Hur”
on the Melbourne stage the first night (Saturday).
The whole box of dice only arrived from Invercar-
gill at two o'clock the same afternoon. A dozen
horses aboard, to be rehearsed, hundreds of tons of
scenery and props. The Williamson management
had arranged with the Union Steamship Co. to push
the s.s. Maunganui. Ten hours were saved on the
trip, despite adverse weather, but it took 150 extra
tons of coal. Work from the wharf was done with
clockwork rapidity. Same on Royal stage. AH
ready at 6.45, just an hour before up-curtain.
“Ami’’: G. R. Ireland, recently alluded to, was
the favorite amateur Melbourne star, in parts like
Claude Melnotte, with the Garrick Club. Becoming
a professional, he was succeeeded by Batten, a credit-
able Hamlet, while the popular low comedian was
Bagster. Afterwards the Histrionic Club sprang up,
with such as Levey, Bruce, Philp and Fox. Ireland’s
first professional show was as Sir Eglamour, in “Two
Gentlemen of Verona,” at the Royal, under Brooke’s
management. The cast included Brooke, Heir, Laip-
bert, a comedian named Wigan, Ellen Mortyne and
Sirs. Heir. Miss Mortyn had first appeared in “The
Unequal Match,” and then in a piece called “The
Daughter of the Stars.” Ireland at once established
a good position. He was in the glorious company
engaged by Simmonds for Jefferson, including Heir.
Lambert, Holt, Stewart, Fitzgerald, Dunn, Mrs.
Heir, Rosa Dunn and Mrs. Phillips. What fine pro-
ductions were “Our American Cousin,” “The Octo-
roon” and “Cricket on the Hearth.” Better still
for Ireland was his association with Barry Sullivan,
at the Royal, for about two years. Ireland had the
misfortune of not being so supple as men like Tither-
adge, Cathcart and Harwood.
The matinge performance at Her Majesty’s (Melb.),
engineered by the new Association of Ac-tors on be-
half of their unborn benefit and pension funds, was
not verj' attractive in the programme, but it got a
lovely advt. through being boycotted by the com-
bined theatrical trades unions. Melbourne Argus
gave the boycott a full column report, the burden
whereof was commendation of the free, noble and in-
dependent anti-unionists who had come to the rescue
of the Cause, and would “assure a splendid
success for the matinee.” The announcement that
Grace Palotta, Andrew Higginson, Bantock and co.,
would act as ushers, ticket-sellers, gallery packers,
etc., was almost pathetic in its tacit suggestion that
such “larky” mummery on a matinee occasion
amounted to a checkmating of the Theatrical In-
dustries Association. The combined forces include
the Theatrical Employees Association, the Musicians’
Union (to which all the principal theatre orchestras
belong), the Supernumeraries Union, the Billposters’
Union, and two organisations of stage performers.
Already it is in the power of the T.I.A. to smash
the Association of Ac-tors by threatening to boycott
the managements which employ them. This first
move was directed merely against the honorary man-
agement of the A.A.’s first benefit performance. It
was not expected to prevent the matinSe show from
happening, but it was bound to prove to the Com-
bine the futility of offering direct opposition to the
interests and principles of trades unionism. In pro-
moting the anti-trades union Association of Ac-tors,
theatrical managers were looking for trouble. If
they persist, they will surely find what they are
looking for.
“Wonga”: “Get-Rich-Quick Wallingford” affords a
good example of the sacrifice of verisimilitude to
the exigencies of the stage—with special reference to
the matinge girl. In the book, on which the play
now running at the Sydney Cri. was founded, AVali-
ingford is a typical Fatman, an unromantic citizen
of vast vest-space and stolidly impressive manner.
On the boards he is a slender beauty-man, whose
glib impudence vastly allures the inexperienced
maiden, but would inspire a U.S. business man, how-
ever bucolic, with a desire to ring up the police
and intimate the discovery of a new Fiji uncle.
Wallingford, as he appears at the Cri., is charming,
but Wallingford as he might have appeared (i.e.,
as the personification of Fat), would have been in-
structive. Also, he would have been a novelty. Come
to think of it, the fat, respectable, urbane confidence
man has not been exploited behind the footlights
in the way he deserves.
Charles Frohman’s revival of Pinero’s “Amazons,”
at the London Duke of York’s, is playing to such
big business that it could easily run to the end of
the year if only he could keep a grip of his three
Amazonian beauties, which he can’t. Marie Lohr
is under engagement to join Gerald du Maurier’s
company at the Wyndham Theatre in October;
Phyllis Neilson-Terry has an important part in Beer-
bohm Tree’s new spectacular pageant, dealing with
F. Drake and the Spanish Armada; and Pauline
Chase has to re-visit America, before once more re-
suming the r6le of Peter Pan in London at Christ-
mas.
Chung Ling Soo, the Scotch-Chinaman conjurer,
must have an industrious press agent in his employ,
judging from the number of paragraphs about him in
the papers. Here is a simple one:—
“Chung Ling Soo, whose magic and mystery are
one of the leading attractions at the Hippodrome,
makes his profession the absorbing interest of his
life. At Barnes he has his own engineering* works,
where all the appliances connected with his illusions
are made. When ifi London he is at the bench by
n,r )c in the morning, working and experimenting
until the time for his show. ‘Work with me,’ he
said, ‘is a monomania. I can’t be easiful.’ On a
voyage to Australia, nearly four years ao-o, after two
days of enforced inactivity, he appealed to the cap-
tain to find him work. For the rest of the six weeks
he was stoker, artificer, cook, steward, carnenter
scenic-painter and entertainer. At Fremantle the
passengers were so pleased that they presented him
with a silver cigar-box.”
And this country entertained Donald Chung un-
awares, with no idea that he was a marvel who had
cooked for a big liner all the way from London.
Some theatre items from London: Madge Tither-
adge is to have a big chance in New York (starting
this week) as Juliet to Lewis Waller's Romeo. Mrs.
Forbes-Robertson (whom Australia knew as Gertrude
Elliott) is Cleopatra in Bernard Shaw’s revised play
of that name. Beerbohm Tree, staging a iew play
about Drake, has declined to collar the hero’s part
himself; “my reticence is due to a modesty which
has been forced upon me by wisdom.” “Every-
woman” is being given an English setting, for pro-
duction in London. “The Third Degree” has been
rechristened “Find the Woman.”- “Bunty Pulls the
Strings” has run for a year, is being played nine
times a week, is to go on indefinitely, and has four
touring companies out in Britain and eight in the
U.S.A.
The latest freak of the “Rehearsal Co.” in London
was a performance of seven scenes from “Twelfth
Night,” with all the characters played by women.
Among the male characters the lady Malvolio was,
seemingly, less successful than the Lady Toby Belch,
who made-up remarkably well and hiccupped and
roared rather like a real Toby. Doubtless a less un-
convincing performance could have been perpetrated
by a company of men—but such an assertion of
“equality of the sexes” would be resented by Suffra-
gettes. Since lovely Woman stooped to emancipation,
it isn’t advisable to tell her that any sort of her
sex can be more naturally imitated by a man than
any sort, however drunken, of his sex, can be mim-
icked by a shemale.
“S.T.S.”: I am beginning to be rather sorry for
Carey, sen., in “On Our Selection” (the play). What
has he done to qualify as a villain, anyway ? True,
he wanted his own money back, which is the un-
pardonable sin in melodrama. But old Rudd was
stony-broke, and ought to have filed his schedule
long ago. In putting in an execution Carey was
only getting ahead of the other creditors. Further,
Carey had applied for a nice bit of local land which
he could make good use of; and Dad, who couldn’t,
put in for it and got it—out of mere spite, to all ap-
pearances. Further still, young Carey was inclined
to make a fool of himself with the penniless Miss
Rudd. However, he got killed in her house, and the
family, firmly believing that her other lover had
done it, committed flat perjury to screen him. They
get great applause for this. Carey, sen., gets hisses
for being anxious to see his son’s murderer arrested.
Being (as I suggest) a simple and rather amiable
old gentleman, he carries the warrant about in his
pocket, and is induced by a regular uncle-from-Fiji
trick, to tear it up himself. Then it turns out that
someone else committed the murder, so, apparently,
it didn’t count and becomes a humorous matter; and
old Carey is finally kicked out, literally kicked over
the doorstep, off the ground, by a comic Irishman.
I submit that he is the most innocent, maligned
sufferer, miscalled Villain, in the history of the
stage.
“Busk”: A few tags. Pinero is anxious over fit-
ting casts. For the small part of a dissipated young
fellow m “The Benefit of the Doubt,” he found the
exact individual, with a face that had been leading
the pimpled life. Call him Pharaoh Tompkins. But
Tompkins, on the strength of such a beautiful en-
gagement, turned teetotaller, and became beautiful
to look upon. ‘“Oh. where are your pimples ?” asked
Pinero, like the artist who invited the ragged, dirty
little crossing-sweeper to his studio. The boy’s
mother fetched him along nicely dressed, and cleaned
up, and the artist tore his hair. Pope Pio Nono
was appealed to by a Knight of the Order of St.
Gregory the Great for leave to subscribe to a
golden crown, for Fanny Elssler, the danseuse.
Pope: “Oh, you may, but, I say, is a crown a proper
for the feet?” A gag introduced in Sheridan’s
Rivals was Bob Acres trying in vain to cram upon
his head a fashionable cocked hat. Says a friend:
That’s only to carry under your arm.” Bob: “A
hat for the arm! Damme, that’s rum.” Crummies,
acting King Lear, made two shies, in vain, to get at
one sentence. First: “How sharper than a serpent’s
child it is to have a thankless tooth.” Second: “How
sharper than a serpent’s thanks it is to have a
toothless child.” Charles Kean alwavs substituted
b by m. As Shylock, he said: “You take
away the beans by which I live.” Dampier had to
struggle against this tendency.
REVEALED.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Upon the stage the other night
He strode in garments rather fine,
His constant cigarette alight—
Itself a most suspicious sign.
But he was really good to see,
And in the gods’ department we
Were wondering who the man might be,
Until with curling, cynic lips
He dared to sneer at fish and chips!
The sad-eyed heroine was there,
Deserted by her love, alas!
She nibbled poor but honest fare
Provided by the comic lass.
With high silk hat upon his head,
He turned to where the lady fed.
And bitter were the words he said.
We knew him vilest of the rips,
For he had sneered at fish and chips !
The gallery with yells of hate
Beset him, for the foe was found.
He stood revealed, a profligate,
A murderer and lying hound,
A man who’d burn, or steal, or kill
To work his own malicious will.
I hated then, I hate him still,
The blasted toff whose loathly quips
Were coldly cast at fish and chips !
VICTORIA.
SILAS SNELL.

The Nearest Railway Point
to the Jenolan Caves.
Described as “the wonder Caverns of the World/’ the Jenolan Caves
have greatly popularised the Blue Mountains with Tourists from
every part of the World. The wonderful formation of Nature—a
source of interest and delight to lovers of beauty—consists of a
cavern chain, littered with huge, fantastic stalactites in glittering
limestone. Each section presents a new and unique formation, and
a visit to these Caves will fully compensate the Tourist for his time
and expense. Connected by road to the Railway, the Caves are
easily visited in one day from Mount Victoria.
Mount Victoria, 77 miles from Sydney, is the radiating point for a
number of Beauty Spots, and possesses an unlimited field of interest
to the seeker of pleasure or health. The Climate is exceptionally
fine; the air fresh and exhilarating, and extremely bracing after the
enervating effects of the Summer heat of the Metropolis.
“ THE IMPERIAL ” —Mount Victoria’s most up-to-date Hotel—is
an ideal home for the Tourist. Purchased by the present Proprietary
in October, 1911, over £7OOO in improvements have been spent upon
the property up to the present. The “Imperial” has its own Light-
ing Plant; its own self-contained Sanitary arrangements and Water
Supply, and the finest Motor Garage outside Sydney.
Although not the largest Hotel on the Mountains, it stands second
to none for comfort and convenience. There are 50 beautifully
furnished Bedrooms; a magnificent Dining Hall, excellent service
and first-class cuisine. Summer Tariff —from November to April:
10/- per day, or £3/3/- per week. Winter Tariff—from May to
October: 10/- per day, £2/10/- per week.
MOTOR TO THE CAVES. A regular Daily Service is run from
the “Imperial” Hotel to the Caves, returning the same afternoon.
The famous “Ttala” Cars are used by the Management, thus making
the trip especially comfortable and interesting. Return fare to
the Caves, 30/-.

particulars from

HOW “ANTINEURASTHIN”
REMEDIES NERVOUS BREAKDOWN.
It is, in the first place, essentially a food —not a
drug stimulant.
It is a food that feeds the whole of the nervous
system with those elements the lack of which in the
structural tissues constitutes the neurasthenic con-
dition.
It is so easily assimilated that in one hour it is
fully absorbed, thus creating quickly a renewed
mental and physical vigor. This is not mere stimu-
lation, but is really the sensation produced by the
actual assimilation and storage of those refined ele-
ments necessary to nerve comfort and vigor. This
is not —as in the case of tonics and stimulants—a
mere transient whipping up, but an actual addition

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will be
taken of communications insufficiently stamped. Name
and address must appear on MSS. No liability is
undertaken re contributions voluntarily submitted,
whether sent by post or handed in; and no MS. will
be returned unless stamps accompany.
Britisher (Westralia) : (11 You seem
rather to approve of Freetrade, yet you applaud
Canada, which is much more Protectionist than Aus-
tralia. (2) If the U.S. “high tariff” admits a small
quantity of manufactured goods, it may be because
there are certain goods that it doesn t want to keep
out. The U.S. has a free list, like other places. (3)
You say that Canada has borrowed abroad
£275,000,000 in :-x years (1906-1911), and its
boom shows that borrowing is a good thing. Dear
friend, every place booms while the borrowing mad-
ness is on. That was why Charles Surface, the
spendthrift and borrower, was so much better dressed,
while the loans lasted, than his brother Joseph. (4)
You ask, “Has any State a sinking fund?” Certainly.
The one you are in has a big sinking fund. (5) You
proudlv quote Chiozza Money as a sort of final court
of appeal, and enclose his remarks on the stagnation
of Australia, and sav that the great C. Money “should
require no introduction to us.” He doesn’t. He is
the ass who mistook one year’s increase of population
for ten years’ increase, and not having sense enough
to verify his impressions by looking up a book of
reference, he said things which made him a subject of
mirth. Didn’t you recognise the blunder?... .Billo:
What is wrong with The Bulletin’s statement that
the average school history (the only one that many
people read) is a fraud? It always strains facts in
the interests of patriotism, and it is full of lies or
half-truths inserted to save space, because the facts
would require too much room and too much elabora-
tion. Take three popular school book items at
random: (1) The Roman troops were not withdrawn
bodily from Britain and the island abandoned ; (2)
It wasn't the imposition of a new tea-tax, but the re-
duction of an old tax from Is. to 3d. per lb. that
brought about the Boston tea riots; (3) In the
Armada scrap the English fleet was the stronger of
the two in almost every way. The third represents
the patriotic lie, while the others are the half-truths
that save space and explanation. And there are
hundreds more. ... O’Regan: What are you complain-
ing about? The Bulletin said that it “didn’t
especially blame the Professor of Music for publish-
ing your song. If it resembled ‘Adoration,’ he
might be able to make sense of bits of it.” Have
you the front to say that “Adoration” is so
poor that nobody can make sense of any of it ?. .. .
H.McD.: In the opinion of this office you are a
horror. If you can’t improve your handwriting 700
per cent., your style of writing 900 per cent., and
the ideas on which you base your pars. 20,000 per
cent., in the name of the holy w.p.b. take to some
more lucrative hobby. . . .Madge S.: As you are a
lady we can hardly call you what we might call you
if we were less civilised than we are. In fact, we
rather like you, but do try and write a little bit
legibly. . .liinkabillian: “Plainly and brutally,” then,
the verses are bad, and the alleged ideas have not
yet come to hand. There is no bread-and-butter in
them. Look at this in print:—
Wattle we do,
’Neath Austral’s sky blue
In the hours of our Natal day
We crown right as might
By day and by night,
To reign on for ever and aye.
. ...L.G.G.: Pretty bad verse even of its kind, and
the kind is not high in the lyric scale. .. .R.-H.M.L.:
We like the closing lines of your attack on “Woman”
She’s mother, or she’s sister,
Aunty, cousin or niece
To every son or mister
Of whate’r creed or race.
She’s been the grace an’ love
Of all the sons of men
Since God said Adam, here’s your dove,
She’s our guiding star since then.
....The Sawdust King: Not up....Longy: Verse
awful. One par might do. .. . Epacris: “Red Heath”
is too indecent for this paper. Only religious jour-
nals can afford to publish information like that....
J.L.8.: We admire “Thoughts under a Gum Tree”
more than we can say. Don’t know what’s it about,
but this stanza nearly killed the Religious Editor:—•
Drugged by the Marine Magician,
Tasted we of Love’s fruition,
Dreamed we of supreme ambition,
Walking on a wave-washed shore.
Now, my fevered invocation
Brings, of love, no incarnation,
And alass 1 my avocation
Unimportant, as before:
Deeper I in aberration
Since those love-blest days of yore.
. ...H.G.: “Man and His Environment” is a very
elementary effort. Anyhow, this isn’t a physics class,
although, if you can make physics interesting we
might throw out the latest camel yarn to make room.
But judging by this effort you’re not able Imma-
ture Moll: The venerable age of the first of your
“Sundry Cynicisms” which caught our eye (re the
wages of gin being breath) filled us with suspicion
and misgivings, so that we dropped the rest hurriedly
into the wicker oubliette. .. .N. 8.: The “gin breath”
jest was invented in this office. The venerable man
of the staff did it when he was a junior sub-editor
long, long ag0....J. Aw: Nicely rhymed and beau-
tifully written, but not the sort of stuff that appeals
to us. It is too Chapzanderish, for one thing....
J.R.F.: A sad, bleak day, cheered only by two purple
features, viz. (1) the intimation of an infuriated
poet, whose ballad had been severely sub-edited, that,
in revenge, he would never darken our doors again ;
(2) the dropping of your illegible and carelessly flung-
together and in every respect maddening contribution
into the w.p.b... ~.Ivevea: By your own showing the
bogus New Hebrides planter is too palpable a Fiji
uncle to be worth exposing. .. .E.L.: We print the
opening sentence as some faint tribute to the splen-
did opulence, dignity, rotundity, etc., of your style:
“Your esteemed correspondent Randolph Bedford in
one of your issues last year remarked that the Wil-
low Trees in Tasmania were bare of foliage for
several months, as this appeared to me not to be in
line with the usual accuracy of his statements I have
taken careful notes this year with regard to the loss
of verdure on 10 trees within a radius of eight miles
with the following conclusions, six trees had / lost
their leaves (not by any means all but you could see
the branches peeping through) by the 4 July, and
four by the 7th, while seven had regained their
coats on the 23 August, and three on the 28th, the
day of writing”... .Alok (or again it might be
Anak or Maud or George Y.) : An invitation to eye-
strain, pure and simple. Wherefore, since we have
only two eyes, and they but weaklings, your inebriated
looking screed was flung aside, practically unread.
TO ARTISTIC (AND INARTISTIC)
CONTRIBUTORS.
K.U.F.: The picture would hardly have crawled
through even with a new idea attached. What ceme-
tery did you dig that joke out of ? It amounts to
body-snatching. .. .Laurence: The point has escaped
us.... Dunn: It is possible to overdo the low-cut
lady and the high-heeled woman. Your figures are
shrieking impossibilities.... A.W.: The jest is almost
a record for futility... .D.P.: Bear-skin proposition
turned down for obvious reasons, besides its age. The
yarn of the two stutterers is an early PuNCH-ism....
F.G.: The trouble lies in the painful obviousness of
the gag. And the picture arouses no boundless emo-
tion.... Mary G.: Too amateurish. If you are a
young school girl then they show enough early pro-
mise to suggest that, with good tuition, you may
do something. If you have your hair up and are on
the electoral roll, they show a sufficient absence of
late promise to justify you in leaving the job alone.
If you are in the midway or flapper stage, you will
have to toss up.... J.A.E.: There is neither vice nor
virtue in them... .E.H.: The gag is feeble, and the
aged husbandman, with the voluminous tailed coat
made of wood, has got his beard on crooked. The
rest of the picture isn’t very annoying, but the cross-
eyed man who stands head and shoulders over the
bashed and dinted crowd seems to need some explana-
tion... .A. W.: The senseless quip, of course, accounts
for Maud’s mad expression, but where is her other
leg?

THE DAGO MENACE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In 1915 there would bV seven Dreadnoughts be-
longing to various South American States 1
—Cheerful news item.
Oh, the Dago’s partly white,
And sometimes he can fight,
And sometimes he lives on garlic, fish and sago ;
His drink is lemon-squash,
Now and then he’s known to wash—
And 4/ou’ll have to speak respectful of the Dago 1
Oh, the Dago’s often cheap,
And he sweats while we’re asleep—
I don’t think he goes to bed, although he may go !
'Neath the counter he will snooze,
’Mid the fish- or fruit-refuse—
Still, you’ll have to how politeful to the Dago!
He’s both ignorant and slow,
And we often tell him so!
And his anger is the rage of a virago—
For he’ll maim, or take a life,
With his swift and sudden knife!
But you’ll have to take your hat off to the Dago!
lie’s a liar, it may be,
And a fool, perchance, is he,
Who thinks that images of wood and clay go !
But he bristles up his fur,
At the sight of Death astir
And you’ll have to act obsequious to the Dago!
Yes ! —it’s not a pleasant thought
For a Race that dreadeth nought—-
To think St. Jingo second to St. Jago !
But, unless you make things hum
That’s about what’s going to come,
And —Australia will be ivaiting on the Dago!
Jf.S.W.
R. McGEDDON.
Like the Commonwealth, the Cold Country
is afflicted with those dreary things that are
called “conferences”; but from recent files
of the London papers it looks as if the de-
plorable state of Bull’s alleged land defences
has shocked even the leathery “Nonconform-
ist Conscience.” At the AVesleyan assem-
blage, held in July last, the report of the
committee on the (more or less) “Religious
Observance of the Lord’s Day” (just as if
the Lord didn’t own all the days!) urged the
conference: —
To omit from its resolutions the paragraph
calling upon the young men of Methodism to hold
aloof from the Territorial Force until the regula-
tions regarding Sunday rifle practice were altered.
Ten or 15 years ago the said conference
would have shouted at the top of its monkey
shave for nine hours, and then hurled forth
the carnal recommedation to the joyful
sound of “Washed in the Blood!” But,
after nine minutes of discussion, the badly-
scared Methodies “unanimously agreed to
this course /” The fear of a future German
edict that would compel it to sing. “Ha
welche Lust, Soldat zu sein!” (“Oh! it’s
grand to be a Soldier!”) at strenuously un-
pleasant Sunday Afternoons, worked this
miraculous metamorphosis. Consequently,
the good young Methody will carry his gun
on the Sabbath, uncursed, in merry England.
But I should like Jimmy McGowen, Our
Mister ’Olman, and all other peace-and-
propriety, anti-Sabbath-ci(bbage heroes to
mourn with me awhile oyier the passing of
another Grand Old Institution. Also,
Pearce might chew on the hymn-book for a
time, and then tell us that, as our father
John Bull is now -following after strange
gods, we, too, may imitate J. 8., and the,
Swiss, and the Germans, and the French, and:
a few more without being prosecuted
for fracturing the blessed Sawbath ! I don’t
suppose that the Lord will offer any Per-
sonal objection.
The Bulletin’s “Old Soldier” re-enters
the lists : *
“Miles” (B. 22/8/T2) misunderstands me. I did
not suggest that .the bayonet is useless in modern
warfare. My sole contention was, and is, that it is
less useful than a number of other things. And it is
these things that the cadets must be taught in the
scant time at their disposal. Nowadays, a line
of infantry must get very close before it can
charge. As far back as half a century ago it was
proved that steady infantry, armed with muzzle-
loading rifles, would stop an advance, even in ex-
tended order, at 300vds. I have seen it done more
than once. Now, steady infantry with magazine
rifles could not be approached within 600yds. over
open ground. A great deal of the fighting in Man-
churia was among high crops, where both sides were
hidden] It must also be remembered that the Japs
were pressed for time, especially after the invest-
ment of Port Arthur, and they no doubt sacrificed
their men recklessly to save time. In the days of
Brown Bess, infantry would engage at 60yds. apart,
fire one or two volieys, and then the line that was
least shaken would charge with the bayonet, a-nd
sweep the other away. Now the infantry will engage
at 800yds. or 1000yds. Tange, the extended lilies 'ap-
proaching each other by rushes, or creeping frpm
cover to cover, firing continuously, and protected by
their artillery. We have it oh the authority of the
commander of the artillery of the Prussian Guard,
that at Gravelotte he was unable to get his 60 guns
within 2500yds. of the French position on the ridge
of Bazeilles: “I was forbidden by the imperative
voices of 6000 chassepots,” he said. As an indica-
tion of what happens during the storming of en-
trenched positions, Nanshan, cited by “Miles,” is a
very good example. The works were manned by
3000 Russians, and the line of works was 4000yds.
long. It was attacked by Oku with 39,000 Japs.
Says Major Maurice in the “Cambridge Mod.
Hist.” :
“As the sun was setting, the infantry of the 4th
Japanese division, wading breast-high through the
ebbing tide of Kinchow Bay, turned the Russian left,
and at last secured a foothold in the position.”
The works had been pounded all day by some 70
or 80 guns, and, presumably, the 3000 Russians had
not much strength remaining in them, since they
left 700 dead behind in the works.
What I question is not whether the bayonet is
worth while, but whether there is time, in the 64
hours annually allowed for drill, for the lads to
learn bayonet drill in addition to the three essential
M’s—Marching, Musketry and Manoeuvre., By all
means teach not only bayonet drill, but fencing and
single-stick. They are excellent in their way. Butt
add another 36 hours to the drill required, so that
the essentials shall not suffer. You cannot teach a
soldier anything that may not be useful to him some-
times on service. The difficulty is to find time to
teach him everything.
“Adelaide”: A rustic tragedy in Shak-
speare’s day. (Its only connection with
Australia is that it appears in the chronicles
of Theberton, a Suffolk village whence
came Light, the founder of Adelaide, which
now has a flourishing suburb of the same
name.) It appears in the diary of the local
park-keeper :
Item, the Mundaye before Mvchaelmes da.ye cam in
a dogge of Johnson’s, the schoemaker, and kyllyd ij
dooes, and then the dogge was take up, and I sende,
to hym to wete wether he wold have the dogge
agayne, and he send word naye, and then I hynge
him upon a tree.
Poor old dogge! These were cold days for
the bowe-wowwe animal.

HILL-CLIMBING
is only one Fine Feature of Dalg'ety Cars
In the Austrian Alpine Contest, the Press Car was a 38-h.p. DAIMLER (one of the Dalgety
Agencies), ancl the Special Correspondent writes, in the Autocar, as follows: —
“We had to cross the two steepest passes in the Alps, viz., Tauern and Katschberg. The Tauern is
nearly 6000 ft. high, and the Katschberg well over 5000 ft., but it was the gradients rather than the altitude
that we found to be the major obstacles.
“Our 38-h.p. DAIMLER made light of its task, and ascended with ease, but chiefly on the bottom speed,
which of itself indicates the severity of the ascent. Some 30 miles from the start, we struck the Loibl Pass.
The height is well under 5000 ft., but as an ascent it is as stiff a proposition as could be conceived. In the
first place, the gradients themselves are probably from one in five to one in six. My fellow-passengers,
indeed, averred that the rise was worse than that of the Katschberg. In the second place, hairpin bends
were numerous and all extremely rough, whilst, in the third, the road abounded in humps and gullies. The
climb was extended oyer kilometres, and of this distance the major portion was appallingly steep. After
our experience on Tauern and the Katschberg, however, we had no fears whatever for our SILENT
KNIGIIT DAIMLER.
“As regards steepness, however, the Loibl may not be in actual figures auite as high as our friend
the Katschberg—l cannot pledge myself that it is not—it is certainly not child’s play, and I should be sorry
to tackle it with any car in which I had not the completest confidence. This we fortunately possessed, and
were amply justified. The car sailed by in triumph, passing every other that was within measureable
distance, and we reached the summit without a falter. We turned to the descent with the certainty of
having something equally steep in the way of gradient, as the valley lay visibly below us at an enormous
depth. The road nroverl just as rough and badly graded as on the ascent, and both by reason of the
caniveaux and the steepness of the valley, we went down against compression with one or two others, and
the lower gears engaged and the clutch in. The DAIMLER SILENT KNIGHT engine, by the way, confers
exceptional advantages in this respect.”

To Kill Hair.
ELECTROLYSIS AT HOME. APPARATUS
i Returnable when done with.
SuMtfuous hairs on the face or . arms can only be
got rid of in one way. The roots must be dissolved
by electrolysis.
The well-known Mme. Tensfeldt, one of the most
noted beauty experts in Britain, has for some years
been, supplying to ladies, unwilling to pay the ex-
pense of repeated treatment for superfluous hair, a
portable and simple apparatus, for home use. Thanks
to the clear explanation which Mme. Tensfeldt sends
with it, ladies can use it for themselves before a
looking-glass, or a servant or freind can apply it.
The apparatus is charged for one year, and it works
perfectly, without pain or shock, while absolutely
destroying the root of each individual hair. Success
is guaranteed.
Home electrolysis is not like the pastes and fluids
with' which ladies have, until now, had to be con-
tent. These substances burn the hair but do not
touch the root; the hair drops out—dead. The root
is completely dissolved, and it is impossible that
hair should ever grow again from the same root.
The Tensfeldt Home Electrolysis outfit will be sent,
carriage paid, to any part of the world for £5 55.,’
international money order or banker’s draft, payable
in the United Kingdom ; and when the apparatus is
done with it can be returned, carriage paid—pro-
vided it is sent within six months—and an allowance
of £1 6s. in cash will be sent immediately in ex-
change for it. The address is Mme. Tensfeldt, 120 a,
I'rinees Street, Edinburgh, Scotland,*

BRISBANE.
IN BANANA LAND
David Bowman, like the sons of Geo. 111.,
didn’t last long enough to come into his king-
dom. Asthma and other miseries have
broken him up, and he retires to the position
of a private member, leaving his mantle to
T. J. Ryan, of Barcoo. Even his worst
enemies wish the retiring leader well.
•‘Don Q.”: “Suspending the operation of
the Act” is a chronic habit with Digby Den-
ham’s crowd. Last year a Bill was passed
to deal with the vendor of adulterated boots
and shoes. It was ordained that pieces of
old cardboard and decayed newspapers
should no longer masquerade as leather, and
there was a general air about the ordain-
ment or ordination that bespoke real earn-
estness of purpose. But Denham wanted to
be as kind as possible, and gave the sellers
of shoddy six months in which to get rid of
their existing stocks. Then, in reply to an
influential deputation of importers, he pro-
mised, to extend the term for unloading the
rubbish for another six months. Finally,
whpn faced by a second and more determined
mob of foreign traders, he agreed to con-
sider the question of repealing the Act alto-
gether. Similarly, in the matter of the
Wages Boards and Factories Act, Minister
Barnes boasts that his Department has com-
pelled 59 sweaters of industry to refund a
sum of £l7OO which had been filched from
employees by illegal retention of wages. By
the look of things, 59 persons have to thank
the “suspension of the operation of the Act”
that they are not where some other people,
who steal £l7OO or even 3s. sid., so often
find themselves.
“Jup” speaks his mind :
The sacrifice of Ken Grant marks the beginning;
of the end of the Kidston influence in Queensland
politics. Grant was a bad man to lose and Blair is
a poor one to have acquired. The newcomer has the
place-and-power hunger to a marked extent, and the
Ministry is divided enough without the introduction
of fresh trouble. In spite of a paper majority for
the Government of 22, the malcontents reduced it to
two oh a division on the Industrial Peace Bill.
Appel, whose handling of the general strike foolish-
ness excited general derision, is understood to be
marked for slaughter. Meanwhile, Blair and Macart-
ney are palpably wondering why Denham hangs on
to a job that they could fill so much better them-
selves.
“Kobbi”: One result of the Macgregor’s
sojourn in Bananaland’s gem country, a
while back, was the sparkling exhibit at
Brisbane’s recent show. The value of
stones shown in the rough, cut and mounted
stage is estimated at £6500. Every color
was there. One exhibitor alone had 200 oz.
of gems, cut and uncut, while most of the
“biggest to date” stones on the field were on
view, including the one that Macgregor got
in the course of his alleged toil. Tracking
back on the long-dried pages of the inky way,
I found that gems were discovered in Queens-
land as far off as ’75, and that some of
the earlier shows were in full going order in
’SI. An attempt, 10 years later, to estab-
lish a European market fell as flat as a mess
of pottage thrown at a distant telegraph pole.
In 1900 there were only 12 miners at work
on the field, but now about 550 grub out a
living there. Then this slab of truth fell
on my intellect and made me leap. Most
of the stones are sent to Germany to
be cut. Isn’t there anybody in Australia
cannMe of making a lapidary’s wheel go
round?
APPEL ,
Queensland 's
Home
Secretary.
William Clarke, of Brisbane (Q.), bas
been raking in sundry odd and anci-
ent corners, with a view to classifying
the yarns re the end of Ludwig Leichhardt,
the explorer who tackled a job that was
much, too big for him. A scrap or flinder
of evidence torn off the tail of the garment
of Possibility comes from one Walsh, now of
Broomehill (W-A.). Walsh avers that once
when he was camped on Slashers Creek, a
stream which trickles or dries into the
Thompson (W.Q.), an old fatherly nigger
told him that when a boy he had seen three
dead white men on the bank of Lake
Buchanan. They had come from the west,
evidently making back, and Buchanan is
salt. The nigger had never been on horse-
back, and when it was suggested that he
should mount a gallant steed and show
Walsh the scefie of the find, he dispersed in
dismay. Still, all dead white men are not
Leichhardt, any more than all old cannon
found on the Barrier reef were thrown over-
board by Cook, or all saplings near Cook-
town are the tree that Cook’s old ship was
tied to. Another point of interest men-
tioned in Clark’s memoirs, published in the
Courier, deals with Colin Archer, one of
that ilk who knew good squatting countrv
when they saw it. Colin Archer, after
taking a hand at the squatting business,
turned his tracks back to Norway, where he
designed and built the Fram, of . Nansen
fame and failure. So, after all, Australia
had a hand, though a very remote one, in the
discovery by Amundsen of the South Pole
(since Amundsen aforesaid used Nansen’s
Fram).
, The letter of “F. 8.” :
In the opinion of Governor Macgregor, the recent
General Booth was the greatest man of the nineteenth
century. Said he: “According to historians, the
two greatest men of last century were Napoleon and
Bismarck. One deluged Europe with blood; the
other cursed civilisation with armaments. Mr.
Booth relieved as much misery as the other two
men caused. ’ My humble idea is that Macgregor
ought to have more sense. Booth was a religious
parasite. He dared -not denounce the real causes of
the evils he saw around him for fear of offending
the rich people from whom he derived so many sub-
scriptions. So he banged-the big drum, and pounded
the loud tambourine, and put a sor.t .of porous
plaster of piety on the external symptoms, and made
the outside of the platter clean, and whitewashed
the sepulchre. Pauperism, hunger, misery and in-
sanity grew around him, and he did nothing to
diminish them. He tried to establish a servile race
of religious fanatics ; and he believed, not in making
the masses independent and self-reliant, so that they
wouldn t need Booth, but in a vast system of what
he regarded as charity, with Booth as the glorified
centre thereof. A completely successful Booth would
have made the world a gigantic workhouse, with a
rug drum at the front door and a big snuffle at the
back. Napoleon I. and Bismarck certainly, by their
military devices, led to the killing’ of a lot of people
who would have died in any case. But, so far as
their social policy went, they aimed at making
the masstes independent, solvent and upstanding.
Booth was only great on benevolence, which is a
device by which rich sweaters! and monopolists give
a trifle of ill-gotten money to a hook-nosed middle-
man and gam credit thereby. And the hook-nosed
middleman gives the money to the poor, if they are
sufficiently pious and humble and obedient to the
hook-nosed circumstance. And thereby the poor are
taught habits of dependence and pauperism, and
there is much applause for the hook-nosed intervener,
and for the rich loafers and sweaters and rack-
renters who handed him the funds. The verdict is
that one Napoleon nr Bismarck, or even Robespierre,
is worth many Booths, T '
The Recipe.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“If you want to be beautiful do your own wash-
ing,” is the theme of Dr. David Paulson's American
lectures. There has been quite a rush of Society
women to Lake Winona, Indiana, and wash-tub
classes have been formed in numbers.- — Age.
Duchess and dowager, forty and fat.
Crusty old spinster, a-nursing your cat,
Wary old widow, alert as a lynx,
Chaperon weary who once was a minx,
Would you be lissome and youthful and gay.
Would you get rid of those stragglers in grey,
Would you be fragrant and blush like the
rose.
Would you be beautiful? Wash your own
clo’es.
Ladies who grieve for a tint over-red,
Ladies who sigh for a curve that is dead,
Ladies who wish they could trippingly twirl
In the gay mazes that call for the Girl,
Elderly warriors who’ve got off the stage,
Out of respect to the gibes of the Age,
Wrinkled of forehead and ruddy of nose,
Would you be beautiful? Wash your own
clo’es.
Ask that good lady who washes them now
Doesn’t she smile, with the sweat on her
brow ?
Isn’t she youthful through soaking in suds?
Doesn’t she. revel in wringing out duds?
Isn’t she frightened she’d soon become stale
If she were left with no boiler to bale?
Wouldn’t she count it the crown of her woes
If her employers would wash their own
clo’es?
Rub ’em and scrub ’em (the doctor-inan
saith) ;
Ppund and confound ’em as long as you’ve
breath ;
Bring back your color by belting the skirt,
Knock oil your adipose hunting the dirt;
Wringing will give a gay twist to your
t tongue,
Cleansing your stockings you’ll learn to be
young.
Steaming and stewing will bring back your
pose—
Would you be beautiful? Go and Wash
Clo’es.
QUEENSLAND.
F. BORN.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, September 7, 1912.
My Dear Thargomindah,—
It was a rejuvenated and invigorated
.Viceroy who returned to us on Wednesday
morning from the wilds of Central Queens-
land. From all accounts he had been having
the glad time of his vice-regal life, chasing
the panting kangaroo, and pursuihg with
the sinuous whip the bounding bull and the
aerial and spiral cow. Evidently such peace-
ful occupations are 'good for Governors who
begin to feel the strain of wearing a cocked
crown, and ought to be regularly prescribed
as a tonic for weary greatness. Lady Den-
man also looked much better for her sojourn
amid green pastures and still waterlioles.
Lady Barttelot was motored to Pinkenba by
the Collins lady, under whose roof she had
for some days spread her blankets.
The Denmans and their suite scattered
away from our city per Lucinda, and in-
stead of the whole awed and solemnly happy
population turning out to speed the parting
guest, the only witnesses of their departure
were one aide, two soldiers, a lonely Premier
and a silent Speaker. Someone had, as
usual, blundered, and the chill of the proceed-
ings was rendered the more Antarctic by the
fact that things were meant to be quite
otherwise. The G.-G. had himself expressed
a wish to shake the entire population by
what it lifts its tea-cup with before leaving,
and no one had remembered to tell the public
to come along and be shaken. It was a
splendid opportunity missed, but six noisome
rubbish carts, lined up along the route to the
wharf, tried their utmost to make things
cheerful. In spite of everything, however,
George Five’s understudy said he was
pleased with us, and wanted to visit our big
State again, and have another look at his
friend, the prickly pear.
The Wee Macgregors have only twice
shown us the light of their countenances
during this week. They attended the Mater
Misericordise ball on Wednesday evening,
and visited the United Service Institution
on Friday afternoon. On the latter occasion
the Wee solemnly removed the duster, towel
or other drapery that hid from view the
classic countenance of George V. The effigy,
bust or ornament thus released from obscur-
ity is the gift of Mr. Guelph himself.
The Mater ball was well attended, though
it lost a number of its usual supporters
owing to the fact that the medical staff' of
the Goodna Asylum had a dance arranged
for the same night. The latter was an hilari-
ous affair, and a number of enthusiasts from
town who helped it to hilare brought their
weariness of eye and limb home by special
train in the younger hours of the morning.
The Denham lady and Mrs. Arthur Pierson
were the chaperones.
On Monday night, Mrs. Arthur Earl and
daughter Ruby entertained a clump of the
Younger Set at a “small and early” in honor
of Miss Mary Coates, a Sydney girl who has
been wintering with them. The only other
leap which happened this week was the
Arcadians’ ball on Friday. It was a thrill-
ing affair, and the garments worn carried a
cheerful suggestion of the world, the flesh
and the Beelzebub.
A local “holy Willie” has been working up
a feeling of shocked unhappiness over the
alleged indelicacy of the representations of
well-known paintings and statues at the
Empire by Henriette de Serris and her clever
company. Whether on account of his stric-
tures, or in spite of them. Henriette is getting
crowded and appreciative houses.
The scent of the wattle which filled all
Adelaide-street with sweetness on Tuesday
night proclaimed to a wondering universe
that the Women’s Club was on the rampage,
and meant to do its little best to promote
the observance of an annual Wattle Day. A
committee was appointed. Mrs. Lou Bond
and Mrs. Frank Little spoke pleasantly on
the national flower theme. A youthful male
contributed a very long and extremely de-
tailed paper on the various forms of the
bloomy vegetable, and then everybody got to
work seriously on the supper. Some songs
helped the programme through to a quiet
finish.
The Toombul branch of the Q.W.E.L. met
at Eschenhagen’s on Tuesday afternoon to
compel Miss Elsie Munro to become the
owner of a gold wristlet watch. The lady
certainly deserved all she got, since for nine
long years she has served the branch, either
as its treasurer or secretary. On the occa-
sion of the presentation, cups foamed or
sizzied with fragrant tea, and Mrs. Hutton,
Mrs. Stirrat and Andrew Petrie said plea-
sant things to the recipient of the gift.
Mrs. W. T. Robertson gave a big At Home
at her pretty Indooroopilly home, lona, oh
Thursday. Her garden was a joy to heart
and eye, and an excellent band was coyly
hidden under the branches of a sheltering
palm. The hostess, a lady whose dress sense,
or instinct, or feeling is highly developed,
wore a white lingerie frock, a large Tuscan
hat and a cloud of veiling garlanded with
fuchsia flowers. About 200 of our Very
Vest People drank tea and nibbled cake, and
then the whole mass of guest left in a hurry
and a cloud of dust to catch the train to
town.
Very few of the usual devotees were at
the races, and as far as frocking is con-
cerned, it was a coat and skirt day. The
weather was dusty and gusty. Among the
ardent racegoers whom I saw blown over the
lawn were Mrs. Jack Fihelly, Mrs. Peter
Murphy, Mrs. Buchanan, Mrs. Charlie
Buchanan, Mrs. Frank Wilson and a small
assortment of other female persons.
Dr. Harold Bourne and Nurse Brown
(lately of England, but at present reading
temperatures at the General Hospital here)
have conspired together to tempt matri-
monial fortune, and there is a matter of the
same kind reported between Muriel Duff and
one Norman Anderson, a feeder of sheep or
oxen in the Gladstone district.
One of our weddings of the week was that
in which Ethel Kennedy, the eldest daughter
of A. S. Kennedy, of the Education Depart-
ment, swore her plighted curse to Clement
VVethered, of Glenugie Station (N.S.W.).
and a similar nuptial bargain was struck
between little Miss Russell, of Bowen Hills,
and Burrell Gray, who successfully combines
the avocations of chemist and all-roi'inc’
sportsman. _ A pestle-and-mortar, a bicycle
and a full-rigged yacht occupied front seats
on the Wedding cake.
Anyone who owned, or hoped to acquire,
any sort of interest in a Scotch ancestor,
hurried to the Exhibition Hall to-night and
endeavored to obtain the best seat for his
money. It was a braw Caledonian nicht,
and every item on the programme was thrift-
ily applauded. Miss Bessie Fraser, from
your aged and untidy city, was the star
performer. The boy and girl members
of the noted dancing tribe of King are
hereby awarded generous bouquets of
thistle and wild haggis for the part
they took in the uproar. A pipe band
also contributed to the gaiety of that por-
tion of the Scotch nation assembled, and a
small dog, which might have had a Gordon
setter among his distant relatives, tried hard
to assist the melody. He was ejected with
violence.
Yours imperishably,
Australia Jane.
The inclusion of James Blair in the Den-
ham Ministry, and the departure of K. M.
Grant therefrom, is by no means a com-
fortable change for the Q. Premier. It con-
firms the oft-stated charge of the undue
potency in the Ministry of the South, and it
leaves the Centre, North and West, about
75 per cent, of Bananaland, with only one
representative in the Cabinet. In fact,
Denham’s Government becomes mostly a
Queen-street Ministry, which is the old
trouble over again. It would not be sur-
prising, under the circumstances, if Towns-
ville and Rockhamption again joined forces,
as in the days of yore, to combat the southern
influence. What the ambitious and able
men of the Liberal party (of whom Forsyth
os the foremost) will say to the elevation of
Blair, who is only a recent recruit, may well
be imagined. Grant’s exit deprives Denham
of his most popular Minister. The clouds
are thickening round the Q. Premier’s head.
Alas ! for Denham !
Doomed to the hardest task of man alive —
To make three billets do the work of five.
David Hay Dalrymple is now humping his
swag among the stars. He died at the age
of 72. His early career doesn’t matter much,
for he was just the usual kind of business
man, mayor, etc. The day of his greatness
commenced when Plugh Nelson made him
Secretary for Public Ignorance in 1895. He
was also in the Byrnes Ministry, the Dick-
son Ministry, and the first Bob Philp Min-
istry. .As a speaker he was good; as a con-
versationalist he was excellent; and as a
humorist he wasn’t too horrid. Regarded as
a Minister,, some people said in the bitter-
ness of their hearts that he might have been
improved upon, without wrecking the Con-
stitution or knocking the bottom out of the
country’s prospects. He took little or no
interest in his work. There is still told a
vile and mouldered tale about a time when
he held the two portfolios of Agriculture and
Public Instruction, and had to receive two
deputations in one day. To the first, which
dealt with the bucolic industry, he gave a
vague and general assent. That is to say,
he looked at the ceiling and said “Oh, yes.”
The Public Instruction deputation came on
afterooon, when disgust at the whole
Ministerial job had eaten into Dalrymple’s
soul. He listened t 6 it as one buried in
great abstraction, thep sat up suddenly, and
looked at it with the . sort of eye that 'Solo-
mon used, when he said that all was vanity*
and told it in a general way that he would
endeavor to get the duty taken off guano,'
and that he recommended forest conservation
and ringbarking. Then he left by a back
door, and the deputation fainted standing,-
and walked out like a, teacher in a dream.

A FASCINATING AUTHOR.
The Complete
Works of Guy
De Maupassant
Issued to Subscribers only
. . by
The Standard Publishing Co. Pty., Ltd.,
Publishers of the Complete Works of
Shakespeare, Balzac, Dickens, Scott,
Thackeray, etc.
IT is a literary event of more than ordinary im-
portance that The Standard Publishing Co. are
able to announce the issue to subscribers of a
complete and admirable translation of the entire
Works of Guy De Maupassant. 1
Henri Rene Guy De Maupassant is by well|nigh
universal consent the greatest short story writer in
all literature. It was when Baudelaire translated
the short stories of Edgar Allan Poe that the French
attempted to achieve mastery in one of the most
difficult forms of art. < , H .
Maupassant and Gautier alone improved upon their
teacher. But Gautier's chief fame rests Upon his
poems and his romances. Maupassant produced a
series of little masterpieces, which are unsurpassed
for ingenuity of conception, for perfection of detail,
and..fpr an almost miraculous adroitness in .the' pre-
sentation of character and incident within the' 1 tipiest
limits. The unwinding of one of his stories .igf one
of the fascinations of literature.
His stories deal with innumerable details of the
human comedy as it presented itself to Maupassant’s
ever-observant eye. He has given us strange tales of
travel and adventure of weird mystery and aw.esome
dread, and of the pure comedy that iS'. never; far
absent from human circles. ~ ;
Only a few of these masterpieces have found t Heir
way into English print. Strangely enough,, this is
the first collected edition which has ever appeared in
England. It has also been left for the Publishers to
make this edition more complete than the authorised
French edition. V.
This Standard . Edition, in fact, contain? .-many
stories and sketches which have actually neVer been
republ shed in France, and are now for the first time
rescued from the oblivion of the magazines, in vrtiich
they originally appeared. /
THE GENERAL FORMAT. —The Standard Edition
is an endeavor to produce a set of the vi Complete
Works of De Maupassant, which will conform ip the
best dictates of style and art.
the ILLUSTRATIONS. —There are thirty through-
out the set, from original drawings by 1 noted French
and American artists. A -number were executed by
De Maupassant's friends —who declined compensation
—contending that it was an honor for them to show
their admiration in this way for his works.
THE PAPER. —The paper used in this edition was
specially made, and is, therefore, of one color and
quality throughout. It is a pure antique, with
deckled edges. ') j;
THE PRINTING. —The printing was ,-done . f|om
original type and not from plates. This (ensures a
special clearness. A new font of French Elzevir
type, bold and elegant in design and to r«ad,
was specially cast for and used in this edition.
There are good margins to every page.
THE BINDING. —It has been our to secure
simplicity and quiet elegance in the bindings. .The
books are handsomely bound in Blue Vellum Cloth,
With distinctive brown and gold title labels.
ANALYSIS OF THE SEVENTEEN
VOLUMES.
IN THE FIRST FIVE VOLUMES the 'ahtlior pic-
tures, with marvellous skill, the virile noyijl tv: of
country scenes, and the comedy and tragedWpnder-
lying the whirl of Parisian life ,in fashiohhßfc: ,draw-
ing-roonis, in studios of art, in greenrooms ojtyppera
and theatre, and in the Quartier Latiii of students
and grisettes. 1 j
THE SECOND SECTION OF FIVE VOLUMES
contains the Romances which caused Tolstoi to hail
De Maupassant as the supreme realis,t ahd romance
writer of his century. Here are found, the' daring
explorations of his subtly intelligent .mind into; the
emotions of the heart of womankind.
THE THIRD SECTION OF FIVE VOLUMES em-
braces the Dramas, Poems and Travels, the _ last-
named sparkling with gems of description-—Meisson-
nier-like pictures in words —of Spanish, Italian,
siean and Oriental life. The shorter Romances fare
also included. t
THE FOURTH SECTION contains stories belong-
ing to no one particular phase of life, unless it piay
be human passion. Love and laughter, tragedy and
tears run side by side in these two volumes, the last
of the series. f
AN APPRECIATION.
Elbert Hubbard writes: — j
“This man never wrote to please the rabble. He
wrote because he had a message for the world,, and
because Divine Energy forced it through his
De Maupassant wrote with the conviction • that: in
life there could be no phase so noble or so mean, so
honorable or so contemptible, so lofty or so low, as
to be unworthy of chronicling ; no groove of hinjjan
virtue or fault, success or failure, wisdom or Jolly,
that did not possess its own peculiar psychological
aspect, and, therefore, demanded analysis. If occas-
ionally some intellectual pigmy, after reading,
screams in falsetto, please remember, spontaneity
and mediocrity are never kin. A WARNING: ’Those
of you who find enjoyment in blue-milk, brain-wander-
ings should never seek De Maupassant..-' You, will
be disappointed. You will not be able, to-digest nor
assimilate the strong elixir of his mint!, so it will
curdle in your gullet—-then it’s you for mental con-
stipation.” V;, .' C'.y ;

10,000 FREE TRIAL BOXES.
Those who are troubled with falling hair, dandruff,
baldness, itching scalp, etc., know full well the
humiliation that they cause. There is no need, how-
ever, of such conditions. These troubles can be
quickly overcome by my True Hair Grower.
1 am not an advertising quack, but am a business
man. My hair began to grow thin about the time
I reached the age of thirty. When I had reached the
age of thirty-five the top of my head was quite bald.
1 had considerable pride, and sought for something
to cause renewed growth. I invested in every pre-
paration that I could see or hear of, and here let me
say that not only did I try all the English so-called
restorers, but those of Continental Europe. I also
visited specialists. My experimenting cost me many
sovereigns and considerable time —yet the hairs came
not. I had given up the idea of ever growing hair
again. One afternoon, while enjoying a brief busi-
ness respite by an Alpine trip, I fell in with an old
collegiate chemist, whose hair was particularly plen-
tiful. I jokingly remarked that I wished he could
spare some of his hair for my benefit. This lead him
to tell me that he understood the human hair as well
as a good dentist understands teeth. He wrote out a
formula, and suffErested that T have a quantity of the
compound prepared. I did this a few days later. I
was astonished to see the hair coming forth after
several applications, and within six weeks my head
was covered by a firm, yet soft, black growth of hair.
Since then the hair on my head has been as luxuriant
as when in my school days. Having arranged for
the legal right to do so, I have for some time been
privately selling this marvellous hair-growing com-
pound. I hold over one thousand letters from people
who have actually grown new hair through the
medium of my true hair grower. I do not pretend
to say it is the only hair grower in the world —I am
not interested in decrying others —but I do assert
that it is the most reliable, and in the end by far the
cheapest.

Business, Bobbery, etc.
The big steamers still continue to shout warnings
at Australian ports that they are coming, and likely
to continue doing so. Word has been sent along of
the new Union S.S. Co.’s Niagara, which has been
launched at Clydebank, and will probably steam into
its stall, ready to pull out on the first Canada-Aus-
tralia trip about the beginning of next year. It has
a length of 522 ft. and a depth of 374 ft., and its
'gross tonnage will, it is reckoned, figure out some-
where round 13,000 to 14,000 tons. Four of its
boilers are being built to eat coal, but bunkers and
ballast tanks are being constructed so that it can
use them for oil stowage if the oil boilers prove up
to expectations, and if liquid grease can be obtained
at a fair price hereabouts. Its sea speed will be
about 17 knots, so it will be right up amongst the
fliers of these waters. Of course, this 13 or 14 thou-
sand-tonner is small compared to the White Star’s
new 18-thousander, expected here presently, and it
is a Lane Cove ferry alongside the Cunard’s Aqui-
tania and the German lmperator, which will run to
45,000 and 50,000 tons. But the Niagara is a large
■size in ships for the Union Cos. It goes to show the
sure tendency to build bigger boats every year, and
the day is fast approaching when even Sydney will be
turning over plans for deepening its harbor (always
•regarded by the Sydneysider as “The Finest Harbor
in the World”). And then Melbourne will be sitting
out on the deserted pierhead watching the Ships that
Pass in the Night to some of the real deep water
ports that at present are left out in the cold.
* * * *
“Barney O’Hea”: How is this for an up-to-date
sample of wedding notice advertising?—
“SNOOKE-SIIORE.—On the 21st inst., at the resi-
dence of the bride’s parents, by the Rev. Silas
Slim, James, eldest son of John Snooke, to Maggie,
second and dearly beloved daughter of John Shore,
undertaker, corner Thomas and Nede streets;
telephone 93.”
Out of consideration for the bride I have slightly
altered the names, but the notice is otherwise just as
it fluttered from the press in one of the most teetotal
regions of Maoriland.
* * * *
“Rubberneck”: These Australians will insist on
going to the fringe of civilisation at the call of gold.
J. S. Hartrick, a Victorian mining engineer, has got
home from a trip through Mongolia, where he went
to look at a quartz reef for a Hong Kong sliindykit.
He went to Ho-char-di, 650 miles nor’-west of
Pekin, travelling in a Chinese cart without springs
and drawn by mules, and the track he travelled upon
was worn down to bedrock by the traffic of cen-
turies, and hadn’t been repaired since the days of
Confucius. The gold occurs in a dyke. formation,
which has been worked for untold ages in the most
primitive fashion. The ore is carefully chipped, and
only the chips that show gold are crushed —in a
mill made of two hard stones, upper and nether
like an old flour mill. After this struggle the stuff
is washed in wooden dishes, and 5 per cent, of the
gold is recovered, while 95 per cent, goes into the
tailings. The gold is put into quills and sent for
sale to Pekin, where it fetches 30s. an ounce
although it is worth 80s. The auriferous belt is said
to be nearly 300 miles long, and is very rich in
places. Yet the pig-tailed incompetent has never,
since the dawn of history, got far below the surface,
and he has never scratched much more than a bare
living and burial expenses out of the business.
* * * *
“Searchlight”: On Dorrigo (N.S.W.) there is over
£5,000,000 worth of valuable timber easy of access.
The promised railway should reach it, and any
amount more, but every year of delay sees the poten-
tial £ s. and. vanishing in smoke. In other words, it
is burned off. At ieast, an amount equal to the
whole cost of the railway has gone skyward in the
last dozen years. One pine tree, recently cut, pro-
vided timber and to spare for a five-roomed cottage.
“The Pariah”: McClusky, cow-owner and Liberal,
was a passionate believer in Private Enterprise. He
spurned the great co-operative Butter Cos. in his
vicinity, and sent all his grease to a private agent in
Melbourne. His returns were small, and, when he
wrote complaining, he was told that the butter had
arrived in a soft condition. He worked his chilling
plant harder than ever. In vain. After four y ears
he took a holiday, and attended the market. There
was his butter, looking surpassingly good. He
bought it at Is. 2d. Next day he called on the
agent. “Your butter was rather soft this week,”
said the latter brazenly. “It only brought Bsd.”
Then was a torrent of Scotch wrath emptied upon
P. Enterprise, who eventually got out of the fuss by
agreeing to pay top market price over the whole four
years. Mac took his butter to another nrivate agent,
curiously enough. I’m not sure I don’t pity that
agent.
* * * *
“John Dishragge”: Some weeks back I made some
observations on this page re the subtly degrading
influence of washing-up on manners and conversa-
tion. I also mentioned that myself and wife were
inserting cries for help (at the usual rates), in the
helpful morning paper. Well, they were answered at
last by a fresh-faced daughter of old John Bull, and
we are not going to wash-up our dishes any more.
Not the same dishes, anyhow. Miss Bull, who, we
guess, got her washing-up training in an iron
foundry, is retiring next week in good order, leaving
our little collection of china in ruins. Last night
I drank in care-free, picnic fashion out of a
billy lid, after coloring it with milk out of a pickle
bottle. I don’t exactly know how she managed it;
but I know the last vegetable dish, the big meat
dish and five large plates succumbed during the ava-
lanche, which drowned the doctor’s best story last
Friday night. A dropping fire of isolated crashes
kept up till the remains of the last cup had been
rattled on to the dust-pan, and reverently interred in
the dustbox. Then an uncanny silence descended on
the house, and we ceased to jump and cry out. Yes-
terday the dresser contained only a pannikin and a
tin plate; and she dropped both of them in a sad,
experimental way, before she came in, and told us
in an injured tone that she’d be leaving.
AUSTRALIAN OR IMPORTED?
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
At a meeting of the Prahran City Council, the
finance committee recommended that the tender of an
English firm for the supply of a steam road-roller be
accepted.—Carried.
Before all else we must be cheap,
This gospel let us scatter
O’er land and sea, with steady sweep —
What do our workmen matter!
For why should local industries
Be foolishly supported?
I'm tired of hearing words like these,
Australian, or Imported ?
We always love a pantomime
Which English wit produces,
We knqw the worth of Yankee rhyme
And Garvice has his uses;
An aproned Bishop spreads the Word,
From Houndsditch straight escorted,
To doubt his value were absurd —
The tr-r-uth must be imported.
A councillor in old Prahran,
Of local funds controller,
A sound, commercial, whiskered man,
Must hire an English roller.
“Why should we spend a golden pound,”
In righteous wrath he snorted,
“To thump this quite, unhallowed ground ?
I move we vote ‘lmported’.”
And so our weary way we take
In bondage to the alien;
How should we ever learn to make
A good thing marked “Australian”?
Our very Councils bear the brand
That shows their views distorted;
We want our Local Bodies manned
With solid weight, imported.
VICTORIA.
GANESHA.

THE PICK OF THE FAMOUS DARLING DOWNS.
IMPORTANT SUBDIVISION AUCTION SALE.
At Warwick, Queensland, Wednesday, 30th October, 1912.
On Account of the Scottish-Australian Investment Co. Ltd.
Talgai West Estate
22,500 Acres. 4000 Acres Under Lucerne
Subdivided into 102 Farms of from 100 to 300 Acres, all Admirably Adapted
for Dairying, Wheat Growing and Mixed Farming.
Splendid Climate—Elevation, 1500 Feet.
EXCELLENT TERMS EXTENDING OVER 17 YEARS.
RAINFALL, 29 y 2 inches.
REAL PROPERTY TITLE.
Situated between the important towns of Warwick and Toowoomba, on the main
Brisbane to Sydney Railway line, with 3 Sidings practically on the property. Connected
by Telephone with Brisbane and all the towns on the Darling Downs. TALGAI YVLSI
is lti miles from Warwick, one of the most prosperous towns on the Downs, and affording
excellent market facilities.
The Land consists of rich alluvial flats, sloping into chocolate ridges, well sheltered
and nicely timbered.
The Homestead Block is highly improved—two-storey Stone House, etc.
Fourteen Dairy Farms on the estate in full working order. Splendidly watered.
The Dairy Herd (recognised as absolutely the best in Queensland), consisting of
2400 Milking Shorthorns, will be sold by auction before the end of the year. The
January, 1912, return per cow was £1 Is. 4d. for 1203 head, BEING A WORLD S
RECORD.
The Allora Butter Factory is 6 miles from the subdivision by road or rail. There
is a creamery on the property, and railway facilities enable cream to be forwarded to
factories at Clifton, Warwick and Toowoomba.
Terms. —s% cash deposit on day of sale, 5% when possession is given,_ no further
payments of principal for two years, then the balance in fifteen equal annual instalments.
Interest at the rate of 5% per annum, payable half-yearly. On the Homestead Block the
deposit will be one-quarter cash.
Lithos and full particulars on apnlication.

LONDON and MELBOURNE.
When food prices go up it is very profit-
able in the aggregate to a country like Aus-
tralia with a surplus of food to sell, but it is
a cold time for one like Britain, which buys
most of its victuals abroad. Thus the Brit-
ish Board of Trade has scratched its dome-
like forehead, and estimated that Bull paid
£55,564,000 more for his imports (most of
the rise being on victuals) in 1911 than he
would have paid if the prices of 1900 had
been maintained, and received £19,177,000
more for his exports. Consequent dead loss
to Bull over £36,000,000.
The S. M. Herald, talking about the
proposed Newcastle ‘(N.S.W.) steel works,
threw a brick at its Joss: —
To take steel rails alone, the quantity of these
which will be required all over Australia is enorm-
ous. Already we have to import most of those we
use from abroad, and we are forced to pay an
exorbitant price for them, in addition to the heavy
item of freight...It is ridiculous to suppose that we
cannot make steel as well as others, or, when every-
thing is taken into consideration, as cheaply.
It is “ridiculous to suppose,” etc. Still,
hasn’t the S. M. Herald been doing just
that kind of “suppose” ever since it was
born?

THE NEW

No. 597
f
SI
THE NEW FEATURES OF THIS REGISTER.
Nine separate totals In pounds, shillings and
pence of different kinds of transactions and of the sales of different
assistants, in addition to grand total of all cash sales.
Amount of each cash sale made by each assistant is
automatically added into a separate total. Every time a sale is made
the amount is added to the total previously shown for that assistant.
Every penny received for cash sales is recorded twice by
this register—on the adding wheels of the assistant making the sale
and on the “secret” or grand total adder. A printed record giving full
particulars is made at the fame time.
Amount in pounds, shillings and pence received in payment of
accounts is shown on another set of adding wheels. This obviates the
necessity of going through dockets or cash book to find how much money *
has been received on account—the total is ready at all times.
Total value of all goods sold on credit is recorded separately. A
glance will show the amount of credit business for the day or to date
from any stated time. This record enables the shopkeeper to see at once
what his total turnover has been.
Every penny paid out is added to previous total amount of
disbursements.
Think of the advantages of having all these separate totals and of
being able to obtain such valuable information by merely turning a key
and glancing at the records.
THESE ARE SOME OF THE IMPROVEMENTS.

DEAF?
It you are deaf, or going deaf, von will be glad
to hear of a simple and scientific device which will
restore your hearing, no matter how old you are.
there is no mystery or secrecy about it ; we describe
it tully and explain just how it acts.
You use spectacles as sight magnifiers, and in the
same way you may use this sound magnifier as an
Sl*? weak hearing. But, unlike spectacles, the
ivilson Common-sense Ear-Drums are invisible and
comfortable. You can’t tell you are wearing them.
No one else can, either, because they are not seen.
They rest the ear nerves by taking the strain off
them—the strain of trying to hear dim sounds.
What a blessed relief to have done with that
tiring strain!
Being made of the purest and softest Para rubber
they cannot be felt, even when the head rests on a
pillow. For the same reason, a blow on the side of
the head will not hurt the ear. In fact, they pro-
tect the ear, the tender inner parts that are so
senstive, from cold winds, dust, sudden and piercing
sounds.
So that you may clearly understand why these
facts are true, and why the Sound Magnifier restores
the hearing, we give an illustration of one as it lies
in the ear. You will notice that the Sound Magni-

IT MAKES YOU SHUDDER!
No matter how dainty food may be, or how carefully prepared, it ;s
simply repugnant to you if your stomach is out of order. That dull pain
in your stomach causes you to simply loathe the sight of food—you cannot
eat, and yet you want something and don’t know what. When you do eat,
you suffer tortures afterwards. Do you realise what will happen to you
if you continue in this way? Your body will become depleted and any;
sickness is apt to take hold of you. If you escape such evils, you will do
as lots of other dyspeptics have done—die of exhaustion.
YOU POSITIVELY CAN AVOID ALL THESE TROUBLES BY TAKING
DR. SHELDON’S DIGESTIVE TABULES.
The reason why any wholesome food is not properly digested is
because the stomach lacks some important element of digestion. Some
stomachs lack peptone, others are deficient in gastric juice or hydrochloric
acid. The one thing necessary in any case of poor digestion is to supply
those digestive elements which the stomach lacks, and nothing does this so
thoroughly and safely as DR. SHELDON’S DIGESTIVE TABULES.
They digest what you eat, thus giving the stomach a rest and assistance
until it is restored to its normal action and vigor.
