Buck Up!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I AM growing bald and ancient, old
desires are fading fast;
When “the boys” get on the warpath
I have got to up and scud;
For while Youth may be Time’s master
there’s a day that dawns at last
When Time ranges up beside you and re-
marks : “All Flesh is Mud.”
But to me it doesn’t follow that because we
drop the pace
That we need to be downhearted and to cut
joy from the bill.
There is no place for the geezer with the
all-is-over face
Save a cold and cheerless section in the bone-
yard on the hill.
There’s a little cosy corner in a tucked-in
pub I know,
Where the racket never finds me when I
want to steal away;
There’s a flash of eyes and liquor that re-
flects the long ago;
And the girls are in the smoke-wreaths —
dear, lost girls of yesterday.
I am back again with Mollie on the star-
track by the sea;
I can smell the ti-tree’s blossom; I can see
the true-love star;
I can hear the crooning water —all the
thrills come back to me,
Where I dream with pipe and whisky in the
corner of the bar.
All the gay and careless spirits of the festive
days of old,
They have scattered to the four-ways and
the stranger fills their place—
Some are lost and some arp married, some
are sleeping in the mould,
While their tankards iie unneeded where the
spider spins his lace.
But I keen mine brightly polished, and I
pour the golden beer
(Though I can’t pour what I’d like to for
the morning means a head),
And I drink the old toast standing: “To the
joy of being here” —
For I’d rather go on crutches than be posted
with the dead!
Don’t go crawling to the graveyard with a
tear-drop in your eye—-
What you’ve done you’ll have to pay for
when you pass beneath the sod —
Better see life out in comfort; you can bet
that by-and-bye
A tale of all the joys you missed will cut no
ice with God.
N.S.W.
BILLY B.
Down for the Farmers’ Con-
ference.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ terrible lucky. Three weeks more,
and it would have been all u-p, as the saying
is. I was in the township, and Sarah telly-
phoned in: ‘Ninety points and still going
strong. Mine’s a trip to Sydney.’
“Sarah hadn’t been one to go much on
politics, but after that when she knew that
the Farmers’ Conference was coming, I
never saw so dreadful anxious a girl to save
the country. And Dad was always the man
to give ’em a few points. I kin talk, you
know. Once when Holman was touring, he
called in for a bit of 'tucker. Of course, I
chipped him, and when he was pushing his
plate away, he says: ‘Sir, you’re a terrible
hard man to argue with.’ That’s me. So
we’re down to the conference.
“Terrible handy things tellyphones is
in the country. The Government runs out a
main trunk, and then drops spurs two miles
each side. The cockies hitch on, dozens of
them. There isn’t what you’d call much
privacy, but yer get used to that. ’Most,
any time you can pick ’er up and listen to
’em gigging all round. We have tellyphone
concerts in the evenings. Somebody has a
new piano-piece, and there’s a bloke 30 miles
away who’s a daisy on the bushman’s bag-
pipe, and there’s phonygraphs all ways. We
do things all right.
“But it’s a terrible starved service. The
tellyphone is the tellygraph as well. There’s
seven or eight stations on our trunk line.
When you want to send a tellygram, you
take it in to a station which is most likely
a store. Perhaps somebody else will be gig-
ging along the line, so your wire has to be
hung up on the tellyphone. _ It’s the same
if, say, you were sending a wire to me. The
operator at the end of a trunk would talk
it over the tellyphone up to our store, and
the storekeeper would holler it back to him
so’s the township wouldn’t miss anything.
After that, it’d be hung up on a rail or
dropped on the floor, waiting for somebody
to call for it. There’s mostly hobnail boot-
marks on our tellygrams.
“I’m not going much on the conference.
Most of them seems to be just opening their
heads, and letting the wind blow their
tongues about. Still, we’ve all got them set
about the freehold. Nobody can kid us with
their perpetual leasehold. Lu Triffles, the
Minister for Agriculture, comes round with
his tongue in his cheek. He starts chippin’
about his department. Terrible handi-
capped, he says, because there’s one officer
working here, and another officer working
in some other place. ‘And the worst of it
is,’ he says, ‘we are paying rent for these
offices.’ Upsets Patten, our boss-cocky. ‘Oh,
payin’ rent is good for you,’ says ’e. ‘Keep
on doing it in perpetuity,’ ’e says, ‘and it’s
surprisin’ how you’ll come to like it.’ He’s
a hard seed.
“Not that there’s much straight-out rent-
ing in the wheat country. The share system
has choused the other clean off the premises.
It’s a fairer go, too. Say you’re the land-
lord, and I’m the share cocky. You throw
in the land, the seed and the fertiliser; I
chucks in the labor and the working plant.
Of course, there are a few extras either way.
F’rinstance, you supply the bluestone for
‘pickling.’ We whack.the harvest, whatever
it is, grain or hay. Mostly we put up half
the wheat bags each, and the boss cocky is
supposed to provide shareman’s quarters and
a horse paddock, with water for the stock.
“It pans out just about middling for the
share cocky if things are middling. If he s
a goer he can bung in 300 acres easy. Sup-
posing it’s a 10-bushel harvest; that means
eight bushels for the share cocky, or 275. an
acre. So his little cheque’ll be £405. Ot
course, that isn’t all bunce. There’s chan
for the working stock, and there’s a few
chips taken off the plant. Still, a man can
make a pretty good start on not much capi-
tal ; and if a bad season hits him, it only
hits him for his labor and his horse feed.
“If the Government wants to do a bit foi
us hayseeds, it might find out what’s the
matter with wheat sacks. We’ve been pay-
ing 7d. and Sd. apiece for the little three-
bushel Chapmans. You just sit down and
figure it out, and vou’ll find the cocky is
paying about Is. 3d. in the £ for the mere
envelope to wrap his harvest in. I know plenty
of men, ‘hard doers,’ whose bag bill last year
was 3s. 6d. an acre —just about as much as
they’d be expected to pay a fatted landlord
if they had one. Somebody wants to say
something about this _ robbery. At present
we’re not dead certain who the thief is.
Here is a job for Lu Triffles.
“I still hear ’em telling the pale young
man at the typewriter to go on the land.
They seem to reckon he can go on the land
with a hoe and a wheelbarrer. It ain’t
so easy. If a young bloke wants to make a
decent living out of wheat, he’s got to bring
in not far short of 250 acres. He can’t do
that with a hoe and a barrer. He’ll want
a plough, harrows, cultivator and drill to get
the crop in, and he’ll want a harvester to
get it off. These will run him into close up
£3OO. And they won’t shift themselves.
He’ll come a mucker if he starts in on stiff
land with less than seven horses, and when
he’s bought their harness, they’ll run him
into close up another £3OO. By that time
he’ll be finding that he wants a dray, most
likely a waggon, and when they talk waggons
they mean anything from £75 to £lO5, and
don’t blush about it. It’s very good to tell
the young ribbon salesman to go out and
be a hardy yeoman, but. unless the young
man has a father that the bank manager
is polite to, they might almost as well tell
him to be a fried egg.
“But it’s all right when you can get
going. There’s not the profit in many things
that there is in a good season. It’s hard
tack, but we’re getting into the way of
mixing it with other things, and there’s
plenty we talk about besides wheat and the
price of corn sacks. When she got me fairly
on the string, Sarah comes up to me and
says, ‘Dad, didn’t yer give that yearling
Jersey heifer to me?’ As like as not I did,
so I couldn’t argue with her. A few days
later she says, ‘Dad, I sold the heifer. Cousin
offered £4 for her, but I said five. Then we
agreed to split the difference, as long as he
made it £4 17s. 6d.’ That’s why I’m hang-
ing around here. Sarah reckoned she could
do 20 grand ooeras on the money, and if
you looked somewheres in the e-allery you’d
find her, with a packet of sandwitches in one
pocket, and what’s left of Molly’s heifer in
the other. Terrible fond”—
P. X.
Sunday.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I WONDER what devil invented Sun-
day? Now I come to think of it, it
wasn’t a devil, but no matter. Any-
way, I’d like to tell him what I think of it!
Sunday’s the hardest day in my week, and
I’m tired for six days after it. I’d like to
meet a week that hadn’t any Saturdays,
Sundays or Mondays in it. I’m always
sleepier on Sunday morning than any other
morning; so is everyone else. It’s almost
as hard to get the rest of the family up as
it is to get a new dress. I don’t know about
my temper, but I do know about the tempers
of the rest of the family—my word, I do.
Before breakfast is over the mailman
comes with the weekly mail. Everyone
fights for letters and papers, and the house
begins to look as if a paper-chase had been
held in it. I skim hurriedly through my
letters while Himself keeps up a running
commentary :
“Um —wool’s down! Got the returns of
those weaners. Struck a beastly bad mar-
ket. Where’s Friday’s paper? Dash my
skin, where have my glasses gone? I wish
someone wouldn’t put papers over every-
thing.”
He goes and plumps into a verandah chair
while I tidy letters away and screw up
papers in a frenzy. There’s the house to
tidy, the dinner to cook, quite a million
little things to see to, and only a few hours
to do them in. I want four hands and 120
minutes in each hour on Sundays.
Whilst I am hurling the beds into day
attire and banging brooms a voice calls out
from the verandah. I have been well
brought up, so I fly out and snap:—
“What d’you want?”
An injured smoky voice answers: “No-
thing. I only called out to say the King has
had toothache.” I don’t say anything, but
I aim a kick at the cat. However, the cat
has learnt that Sunday morning is no time
for foolery, and leaps hurriedly.
As I am scurrying to put the bread in
tins I feverishly wonder what pudding I can
make with no butter and one egg. Then the
voice comes :
“What’s the date?”
“Don’t know,” in a frayed manner.
“Why the devil don’t you? Bless my
soul, anyone ought to know the date. This
ink’s nearly dry—just fill it, will you? I’m
busy! Who’s been tidying this table. My
block’s gone. Oh, here it is. Wish you’d
leave things alone.”
I feel I want to hurt something, so I slam
the butter between the pats and scrunch it.
“The mailman’ll be along soon. My let-
ter’s not written, and I can’t find that
cheque of Brown’s to send to the bank.
Where the blazes is the cheque?” So the
voice bawls with a jerk.
“It’s on the file,” I bawl, also with a jerk.
“Damn it, why didn’t you leave it where
I’d put it in the drawer,” he yells, and a
drawer shuts violently. I am too tired to
say that I found it stuffed in the pocket of
the trousers that were put out to be washed.
Just as I am making the inevitable bread
and butter pudding Himself demands string.
Then I have to keep my thumb on the string
while he ties it, and, of course, I move my
thumb, and there is an explosion.
He has a long conversation with the mail-

man. Then he comes back for morning tea.
Why hasn’t a morning more hours? He
drinks his tea at leisure, but Igo about
doing odds and ends, and carry the cup with
me and gulp at intervals. Oh, the odds and
ends that eat up so much time!
Then I put the bluish leg of mutton in the
tin with islands of fat, and shut it up to
sizzle and roast. And I wish it was the
universe I was cooking.
We’re expecting people in the afternoon.
Himself likes people, lie says they make a
break. They do! I slap up scones for the
sacrificial feast, and, in between basting the
spluttering leg, try to make our one living
room look less sordid. I ram purple petunias
and mignonette into the vases, and put the
shabbiest things in the darkest corners.. The
world wouldn’t be such a bad place if. we
didn’t have to put a good face on things
until the face becomes a fixed and ghastly
grin.
Dinner time finds me hot and tired, with
the red of the fire and the heat of a simmer-
ing temper in my cheeks. "Himself nas a
good appetite. It never balks nor shies, but
takes its fences flying. It can take one
variety of meat and three vegetables in its
stride. He can’t understand why I don’t
wolf greedily, too, after spending a morning
rushing about in a heated kitchen full of
smells. Then he takes his third cup of tea
out on to the verandah that he may drink it
leisurely.
Lightning is a snail to me on Sunday
afternoons. The plates are stacked half-
dried. The kitchen is hurled into a sort of
tidiness. I rush off to my room to wash off
the surface dirt. When lam scrabbling up
my hair Himself bawls out that the Browns
are coming. My heart goes down to the
bowels of the earth, but my fingers run
races.
I manage to meet the Browns on the ver-
andah with a stiff, tired smile. lam vilely
conscious of a hastilv-flunv on, awry dress,
and hands still red from abluting. We say
it’s hot or it’s cold, and how’s Billy and
Tommy and father, and everything Brown
that grows. Oh, these Sundays, for there’s
nothing ever to talk about, and one gets
tired of inventing lies about one’s neighbors!
I’m almost glad when it’s time to get
afternoon tea, because I can say “damn” in
the kitchen, and only the cats can hear, and
they’re used to it. I grin at the little
Browns, and give them weak tea to spill in
their saucers and cake to drop on the floor,
and I say how sweet they are, and pray for
bed.
By the time my head is splitting and I’m
on the rim of hysterics they go, and it seems
to me that most of me goes, too. As lam
gathering up the fragments of the feast,
Himself comes in to say that Smith is
coming!
I say it’s hot or it’s cold over again to
Smith, and how are his sheep and his grass
and any other Smiths. Then I feed the
fowls, and throw some water over the few
struggling plants. Then I get tea. Then
I wash up!
While Smith and Himself smoke and talk
on the verandah I try to look at the paper,
but I’m too tired to take it in. No wonder
women haven’t much “grasp.” Later on,
Smith comes in, and I try to talk while Him-
self has brief and spasmodic slumbers.
“Have you been to town lately?” I ask
Smith.
“Not lately,” he says.
Pause.
“Are you going into the Show?”
“I don’t think so.”
Pause.
“Did you see about that railway acci-
dent?”
“Noticed somethin’ about it?”
Pause.
Himself having an interval of wakeful-
ness.
“Has Downs struck water yet?”
“Not yet. He expects to in another 20ft.”
Pause.
Nearly desperate, I get supper.
Then at last Smith goes— goes —he goes.
Himself yawns loudly and goes off to bed.
I clean up. wash everything, set the table for
the morning (’cause it’s Monday), skim
pans, fill up kettles, wind clocks, sort out
clothes for the first copper. Then I also go
to bed.
And that ends Sunday. Washing day
follows.
B. MERINO.
The Tale of ’Enery.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THIS story, supplied by “Mossy Camp-
bell,” is so touching in its ill-spelled
and unpunctuated beauty that it is
shoved right in :
Mr. “Enery” Bingham, “pushite,” factory worker,
walked down the old familiar alley for the first
time in six months, to tell the truth “Enery” had
been “High and Dry” for that enforced, period at
the expense «f an impartial goverment. he chanced
upon a member of a rival talent, who was a par-
ticular foe of his. for a time the hatchet was
buried. Wot-o, Cocker, Greeted enery. ow is it.
Cocker was taken aback, he had expected er bit uv
stouch. Just came out—he queried trying to look
unconcerned, enery Nodded in the Affirmative this
morning, he said, me first stretch V ther last I
hope, he added. Cocker regarded him with the
wisdom of experience. I thort that ther first lagging
I got. he said, ’n’ I think differently now. “enery’s”
gazed at a row of rookery old cottages, one was
empty with a placard in the window which signified
it was to be let. the Door of a neighboring house
opened and a woman appeared, she beckoned enery
over, see yer back frum the country, she began dis-
creetly out of respect for his feelings. yes. re-
plied “enery” flashing a grateful look upon her the
whole street as if by magic suddenly sprang into life.
People appeared at doors, frowsy heads popped out
of windows, sundry urchins apparently engrossed in
playing marbles, rushed pell mell into their re-
spective. homes to spread the news of enery’s return,
the wireless telegraphy of the alley had begun, arms
were working in semaphore fashion Thumbs were
turned down as in the days of Ancient Rome, by the
occupants of the top windows as they indicated to
one and another the presence of “enery” _ matrons
suddenly discovered that their allotted Portion of the
footpath needed immediate sweeping notwithstanding
the fact at that hour of day they were committing
a breach of a council bylaw careful for the con-
veniences of pedestrians, enery was galvanised into
verbal retort. Huh, er lot of gapers he said with
infinite scorn, as if a bloke wuz er cheap freak in
er curiosity mooseem. one of the doorstep gossipers
apnroached. Did. yer. see. my. Bill “up above” she
said in a husky whisper, yes, replied enery shaking
his head mournfully, one uv ther “screws” gave im
er bashing Just, before I came out the woman de-
parted to be first in the field to break the news to
the other energetic sweepers. I’m. sorry fer yer
trnbble sonny said the woman who had beckoned
enerv over. Did they tell yer in there. enery
nodded he was too full for words “who Buried her”
he said in a low voice repressing his emotion,
we got up a euscrispshun list explained the
woman ’n’ we got the money frum this street,
enery’s feelings underwent a change, thank gawd,
pore mother was’nt buried a pauper, he said. I
wuz with her when she died, continued the woman
’n’ her last words were fer you to lead a better
life. what became of the furniture, said enery.
sold up by the landlord replied the woman, well,
said enery preparing to depart I must get along,
ave yer got a place to stay at said the kind hearted
neighbour. enery lied bravely he would make a
fresh start. I’m staying down ther road with a
cobber, he said indicating cocker who with his
hands in his pockets was balancing himself with
great skill on the edge of the kerb. well, Good
bye, said the woman, so long said enery.
The Freetrade Rabbit Pie.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It (Australia) has endeavored to put down sweat-
ing and long hours and poor pay and black and yel-
low labor on its own premises, while keeping the
door open, or at least more than half open, to the
•products of the people who practise all the cheap
sins that the Australian manufacturer isn’t allowed
to practise.— Bulletin (6/6/’l2).
SES CUEEEN, the cookie, he ses to me:
“Now, I puts it to you in this way:
If a feller... . (Woah, Ginger ! Come
over, yeh cow !)....
If a feller sets out fer to say
Where he happens to stand in this politics
game,
And to reason the why and the how,
He has got to have sometliink to back up
the same,
As the sayin’ i 5.... (Woah there, yeh
cow !)
“Ses Cullen, the cockie, he ses to me: ‘Well,
I reasons it out fer yeh, so:
There’s this ’ere Pertection an’ this ’ere....
(Woah, Nell!
Come over there, Ginger! Way! Woah!)....
There’s this ’ere Pertection an’ this ’ere
Freetrade,
Which I never ’ave quite understood,
Till I figures it out be the blunders I made
While I scratched fer me own livelihood.
“When first I took up me selection out ’ere
I was votin’ Freetrade pretty strong!
An’ to live on the cheap was me centril idear,
An’ I couldn’t see anythink wrong
With livin’ on rabbits, fer rabbits was
cheap,
As you’ll probably quite understand,
Fer, back in that time we was breedin’ a
heap
On me own, an’ the neighborin’ land.
“There wus Sanderson had the next section
to me ;
He bred mostly rabbits an’ debts ;
An’ Jones, an’ McPherson, an’ Sandy McGee
Had heaps of the dear little pets.
So I figgers ut out to meself, an’ I owns
That this puttin’ up fences is rot;
I’d be wantin’ the rabbits of Sandy an’
Jones
When I’d et un me own little lot.
“So I cleared off a bit an’ I sowed down me
grass,
An’ I lived upon rabbits an’ duff,
Which was cheap, you’ll allow, but as
months came to pass,
I was just about gettin’ enough.
I was pinin’ fer change, so I reckoned I'd
breed
Some sheep an’ a bullock or two ;
But them dash Freetrade rabbits kep’ eatin’
the feed
Just as fast as the bloomin’ stuff grew!
“It was here that I give up me Freetrade
fer good ;
An’ I set about riggin’ a fence—
Jest a sort o’ low tariff of barbwire en’ wood.
(I admits I was long gettin’ sense.)
‘Now this,’ reckons I, ‘will keep most of ’em
out,
While ut lets just a few of ’em in!’
You will note, all along, I ’ave not the Past
doubt,
I was keen upon savin’ me tin.
“But I’d come to icknowledge that breedin’
the pests
Was a false an’ mistaken idear;
So I poisons me own, an’ I digs out the
nests
Till I got me place pretty well clear.
But me chock-an’-log fence was a sight fer
sore eyes!
It was far frum Pertection, I owns;
Fer I still hugged the notion, of eatin’ cheap
pies
That was bred be McPherson an’ Jones.
“An’ them rabbits was cheap —you might
think I was dense,
But I couldn’t deny they was cheap.
So still they come in through the gaps in
the fence,
An’ took ’alf o’ the grass from me sheep.. ..
Then sudden, one mornin’, it struck roe —
fair whack !
An’ I seen ut as plain as could be:
Wot about all the grass that them rabbits
took back
To McPherson an ’ Jone% an ’ McGee?
“There was Sanderson’s rabbits consumin’
the feed
That belonged to me bullicks an’ sheep!
An’ here was me helpin’ ’em ! Thinkin’ I’d
need
Of a bunny or two on the cheap !. ...
I was solid Pertection in less than a week,
An’ me nettin’ was up in quick time;
An’ now, you will notice, me bullicks is
sleek,
An’ me mutton, I tell yeh, is prime.
“So you take ut frum me.... (Woah there,
Ginger, yeh c0w!)”....
Ses old Cullen, the cockie, ses he:
“I’m a certain an’ solid Pertectionist now,
Fer I’m sick of the bunny that’s free.
‘Low Tariff’ is lettin’ in rabbits to eat
The grass that you want fer yer sheep,
An’ doin’ in chances of raisin’ good meat
In the hope of a pie on the cheap.
“An’ if these politicians ’as got any sense —
(An’ I’ve ’eard as there’s some with a
head)
They will give over patchin’ their chock-an’-
log fence,
An’ put up new nettin’ instead.
Fer I’ve found that the nettin’s the sensible
way,
An’ I’m happy an’ prosperous now,”
Ses Cullen the cockie, he ses to me. “Hey!
Git on with yeh, Ginger, yeh cow!”
VICTORIA.
